Cell fate reprogramming is a crucial aspect of development, yet the molecular mechanisms that promote or inhibit reprogramming in intact organisms are not well understood. In the C. elegans germline, reprogramming germ cells into somatic cells requires chromatin perturbation. The GLP-1/Notch signaling pathway, known for maintaining undifferentiated germline stem/progenitors, has been found to facilitate this reprogramming. Through a combination of genetics, tissue-specific transcriptome analysis, and functional studies of candidate genes, it was discovered that GLP-1/Notch promotes reprogramming by activating genes silenced by the Polycomb repressive complex 2 (P2) and identifies the conserved histone demethylase UTX-1 as a crucial GL-1/Notch target facilitating reprogramming. Notch signaling was identified as a critical player in this reprogramming model, which was unanticipated since signaling through the Notch receptor GL-1 maintains germl stem/proitor fate. To understand this novel reprogramming-promoting role of GL-1/Notch, genetics was combined with tissue-specific profiling. In the C. elegans germl, GL-1/Not signaling is essential for maintaining undifferentiated cells/progen, but also has an unexpected role in promoting cell reprogramming. Genes activated upon increased GL-1/Not signaling suggested an antagonistic relationship between GL-1/Not and P2. The C. elegans P2 consists of MES-2, -3, and -6, and levels of corresponding transcripts were not altered by GL-1/Not signaling. Analyzing genes with higher germl expression, the disproportional X-link of Not-activated genes was striking, with fifteen times more than expected. X-linked genes are largely silenced by C. eleg P2. Genes regulated by GL-1/Not include those maintaining germl/progen cells and genes repressed by P2. GL-1/Not and P2 have antagonistic effects on germ cell decisions and co-regulate common target UTX-1. Findings have implications from development to diseases with abnormal Notch signaling.
Klebsiella pneumoniae is a significant respiratory, blood, and bladder pathogen of clinical concern. The adaptor protein SK2 is required for protection against K. pneumoniae ATCC 438 pulmonary infections. Sk2-/- mice had a 100-fold higher bacterial burden compared to wild-type, controlled by SK2 expression in innate immune cells. Sk2/- neutrophils and monocytes were present in infected lungs, and neutrophils degranulated normally in response to K pneumonia infection, but K pneumonia-stimulated reactive oxygen species production was abolished. K pneumonia-induced neutroph ROS required activity of SFK, Syk, Btk, PLC2, and PK. Loss of SK2 hindered K pneumonia-induced phosphorylation of SFK, Sy, and Py2, implicating SK2 as proximal to activation in pathogen-signaling. SK2-dependent signaling in neutils is essential for K pneumonia-activated ROS production and bacterial clearance. Sensitivity of K pneumonia to ROS is supported by increased bacterial burden in Cybb-/- mice and observations in CGD patients. In murine neutils SK2 was required for full phosphorylation of SF, Sy, and Py2, demonstrating a critical role in K pneumonia-induced pathways for ROS production. SK2 plays a key role in host defense in hematietic compartment against pneumonia 438 in pulmonary infections in murine model. SK2 indispensable for pneumonia-stimulated neutroph ROS, not degran or phagocyt, suggesting increased bacterial burden in Sk2- mice affected by loss of ROS. K pneumonia 438 strain resistant to neutroph phagosis regardless of SK2 expression but elicits SK2-independent degran during infection. ROS is important for protection against pneumonia as mice lacking essential gp91phox of NAD oxidase were susceptible. SK2-expressing neutils contributed to pulmonary containment of pneumonia in lungs independent of monocytes. Sk2/-neutroph defective in triggering cascade for ROS after pneumonia binding to surface. Investigation of SK2 in K pneumonia-induced ROS evaluated requirement for several proteins critical for receptor-mediated ROS after pneumonia infection.
Plasmodium vivax relapse infections occur when latent liver-stage parasites, known as hypnoites, activate and cause new blood-stage infections weeks or months after the initial infection. A within-host mathematical model of liver-stage hypzoites has been developed and validated against data from tropical strains of P. vivax. This model demonstrates the build-up of the hypzoite reservoir following new infections and its depletion through hypzoite activation and death. The hypzo reservoir is predicted to be over-dispersed, with many individuals having few or no hypzoites and some having intensely infected livers. Individuals with more hypzoites are predicted to experience more relapses and contribute more to P.viv transmission. Incorporating hypite killing drugs, such as primaquine, into first-line treatment regimens is predicted to cause substantial reductions in P.v transmission as individuals with most hypites are more likely to relapse and be targeted for treatment. Relapses from hypite activation have important consequences for transmission dynamics of P.viv and constitute a third malaria reservoir, in addition to blood and mosquito reservoirs. Models will play a crucial role in estimating the effectiveness of interventions targeting the hypite reservoir due to the absence of diagnostics for detecting liver-stage parasites. Most existing models do not account for the liver reservoir, but hypite has been incorporated into some P transmission models as a state or broods of hypites. The developed within-host model explains epidemiological patterns of relapse by assuming hypites activate and die at a constant rate and is integrated into Ross-Macdon models to account for relapse characteristic P malaria. Mathematical models provide insights into the impact of control interventions on parasite reservoirs in the vector, blood, and liver. The hypite reservoir build-up and depletion through activation and death is key to understanding P transmission dynamics, with primaquine treatment predicted to reduce transmission by targeting individuals with most hypites.
Selective logging in Amazonian forests results in the emission of over 90 Tg of carbon to the atmosphere annually, which is counterbalanced by forest regrowth. A study utilizing a network of 133 permanent forest plots across Amazonia found regional differences in climate, soil, and initial biomass impact survivors and recruits' carbon flux, providing predictions of post-logging recovery. Net aboveground carbon recovery over 10 years is higher in the Guiana and western regions, with 21 Mg ha-1, compared to southern regions with 12 Mg ha-1, where environmental stress is high due to low rainfall and high seasonality. Survivors play a key role in forest regrowth, and a comprehensive map of post-disturbance recovery potential in Amazonia has been developed. Long-term data from 13 experimentally disturbed sites in the Amazon Basin and Guiana, meeting specific criteria including annual precipitation above 100 mm, total censused area above 1 ha, and multiple censuses, were analyzed. Selective logging, due to its economic implications, is a long-studied human disturbance in tropical forests. Regional differences in Amazonian response to large carbon losses induced by other disturbances are also considered. Predictive maps of carbon changes over 10 years under 40% loss, common after conventional logging, were developed using global maps of carbon, climatic conditions, and soil density. Human activities on carbon cycles, particularly 2.1 Pg yr-1 loss by anthropogenic disturbances in tropical forests, draw increasing attention, with selective logging being a significant contributor. Carbon recovery following selective logging in Amazonian forests varies by region, influenced by climate, soil, and initial biomass, with survivors contributing to regrowth.
Researchers have identified and isolated two distinct populations of neural stem cells in the adult mouse forebrain, specifically in the lateral ventricular subventricular zone, using flow cytometry. The first population, referred to as GEOT cells, expresses high levels of Glast, EGFR, and PlexinB, and low levels of CD24, PSA-NCAM, Ter119, and CD45, and accounts for 3.2% of all cells in the zone. GEOT cells are highly mitotic, short-lived, and can form neurospheres, with 36% of them forming neurospheres over 50 micrometers in diameter and 74% undergoing multilineage differentiation. In contrast, pre-GEOT cells are quiescent, express higher levels of Glast and lower levels of EGFR and PlexinB, cannot form neurospheres, but express stem markers such as Slc1, GFAP, Sox2, and Gli, and are long-lived. Conditional deletion of the Bmi-1 polycomb protein depletes both GEOT and pre-GEOT cells, but pre-GEOT cells are more dependent on Bmi for Cdk2 repression. GEOT cells can be ablated by temozolide, while pre-GEOT cells survive and repopulate the zone. Fate mapping using Ascl1Cre2 and Dlx2 shows GEOT cells persist transiently in the zone, while pre-OT cells are long-lived based on mapping with Slc1, GF, Sox2, and G1. The study distinguishes quiescent neural stem cells from neurosphere-initiating cells, enabling further study of their properties in vivo.
Piezo1 is a mechanically activated ion channel involved in sensing forces in various cell types and tissues. Cryo-electron microscopy has revealed that the Piezo structure is bowl-shaped and capable of inducing membrane curvature via its extended footprint. Researchers used cell-attached patch-clamp electrophysiology and pressure-clamp to examine large numbers of membrane patches from cells expressing Pie1 endogenously at low levels and overexpressing Pie1 at high levels. The data, together with stochastic simulations of Pie1 spatial distributions, show that at endogenous densities and non-physiological densities, Pie1 density has no effect on pressure sensitivity or open probability in the absence of membrane tension. The results suggest Pie channels, at densities likely to be physiologically, behave as independent mechanotrans. This property is essential for cells to transduce forces homogeneously across the entire membrane. Electrophysiological data and simulations show Pie1 channels function as independent mechanotrans, enabling cells to transduce forces with spatial homogeneity. This is important as Pie1 channels have no intrinsic tendency to spatially concentrate and functionally modulate each other's mechanical sensitivity, ideal for cells to transduce mechanical forces homogeneously. Cells are exposed to mechanical forces and have evolved molecular mechanisms for detection, with rapid sensing achieved by force-gated channels converting mechanical energy into electrochemical signals. Pie1 is a mechanically gated cation channel sensing membrane tension with high sensitivity. The study aimed to quantify Pie1 spatial distribution and potential interactions across channel densities using electrophysiology, measuring channel number, single-channel current, open probability, and pressure sensitivity. Cell-attached patch-clamp electrophysiology was used, considering limitations such as spatial resolution limited by pipette size. The findings indicate Pie1 channels inherently function as independent mechanotrans at physiologically relevant densities, crucial for homogeneous force transduction across the cell membrane.
The influence of evidence volatility on choice, reaction time, and confidence in perceptual decision-making has been investigated through a novel sensory manipulation that mimics the addition of unbiased noise to motion-selective regions of the visual cortex. This manipulation was verified with neuronal recordings from macaque areas MT and MST, and its effects were tested on both humans and monkeys. The results showed that increasing noise led to faster decisions and greater confidence over a range of stimuli for which accuracy was minimally impaired. The magnitude of the effects was in agreement with predictions of a bounded accumulation model, where an increase in noise should lead to faster decisions, reduced accuracy, and paradoxically, higher confidence. A well-studied motion task was used, where much is known about the underlying physiology, and the effect of the manipulation was verified by recording from neurons in the visual cortex of the macaque. A framework based on bounded accumulation of evidence accounted for the joint effect of volatility on choice, reaction time, and confidence. The manipulation increased the variance of momentary evidence on decision, termed volatility, and increased decision speed and associated confidence. Testing the influence of volatility on decision process required independent control over signal and noise in evidence, achieved by changing statistical properties of a dynamic stimulus. Recordings from direction selective neurons in middle temporal and medial temporal areas of macaque visual cortex verified the desired properties of the manipulation. The relationship between volatility and decision speed was studied with reaction-time version of random-dot discrimination task, where three human participants completed 663 trials and one macaque monkey completed 14,137 trials. Each trial started with fixating on a central spot, followed by presentation of two targets. The manipulation tested influence of noise on three observables of behavior, accuracy, reaction time, and confidence, in monkeys and humans, showing faster decisions and greater confidence with minimal impairment in accuracy.
The insulin/IGF-1 signaling pathway maintains a relatively short wild-type lifespan in Caenorhabditis elegans by phosphorylating and inactivating DAF-16, the ortholog of FOXO transcription factors in mammalian cells. DAF16 is phosphorylated by AKT kinases, preventing its nuclear translocation. Calcineurin, a PP2 phosphatase, also limits the lifespan of C. elegans, but the mechanism through which it does so is unknown. Research has shown that TAX-6CN-1 and UNC-43, the C. elegans Calcineurin and Ca2+/calmodulin-dependent kinase II orthologs, regulate lifespan through DAF16. UNC43 regulates DAF16 in response to various stress conditions, including starvation, heat, or oxidative stress, and cooperatively contributes to lifespan regulation by insulin signaling. Unlike insulin, UNC43 phosphorylates and activates DAF16, promoting its nuclear localization. The phosphorylation of DAF16 at serine 286 by UNC43 is removed by TAX6CN1, leading to DAF inactivation. Mammalian FOX3 is also regulated by CAMII and Calcinein. The direct targets of worm Calcinein have been identified as DAF16, which is phosphorylated and activated by UNC43 at serine 286. The phosphoryl group is removed by TAX6CN1. UNC43 and TAX6CN regulate C. elegans lifespan through reversible phosphorylation of DAF16. Data suggests CAMII and Calcinein, by phosphoryating or dephosphlating serine 286 of DAF, extend or shorten the lifespan of C.elegans. Multiple signaling pathways, including insulin/IGF1 signaling, germline signaling, mitochondrial signaling, and dietary restriction regulate aging in C.elegans. The insulin pathway includes the upstream insulin/IG1 receptor DAF2 and downstream FOX transcription factor DAF16. Insulin signaling overpowers CAMII and Calcine in regulation of DAF localization in C.eleg. TAX6 or UNC43 failed to abolish DAF nuclear accumulation by DAF2 RNAi, and TAX or UNC43 failed to overcome inhibition of DAF nuclear localization by DAF18 RNAi. A model shows regulation of DAF by insulin, UNC43, and TAX6CN.
Researchers have investigated the neural mechanisms underlying the sequential execution of motor movements in Drosophila, specifically the process of grooming, which involves the serial execution of distinct movements to clean different body parts. By using genetically targeted activation of neural subsets, distinct motor programs that clean individual body parts were driven, revealing that these programs are organized into a suppression hierarchy, where motor programs that occur first suppress those that occur later. Cleaning one body part reduces the sensory drive to its motor program, relieving suppression of the next movement, allowing the sequence to progress down the hierarchy. A model featuring independently evoked movements activated in parallel but selected serially through hierarchical suppression successfully reproduced the grooming sequence. This provides the first example of an innate motor sequence implemented by the prevailing model generating human action. The study demonstrates that dust on the body elicits different independently evoked motor programs for five body parts that progress in series and that these programs are organized into a suppression hierarchy. An empirically derived computational model demonstrates how suppression mediates the sequential selection of the motor programs. The evolutionarily ancient ability to select among behaviors through hierarchical suppression may have provided the necessary infrastructure to perform motor actions in sequence. Activation and parallel models describe different mechanisms generating sequential behavior, while tools have invigorated interest in identifying neurons driving behavior, enabling experiments to probe the organization of serial behavior through control of individual actions. The organizational principles underlying neural control of serial selection in innate behavior, common to most limbed animals, grooming, were studied. Animals have mechanisms for selecting favorable options and suppressing others due to different values on motor programs. The demonstration of hierarchical suppression among behaviors leading to sequence emergence is significant. Identifying circuitry for each cleaning module and determining interactions will reveal serial organization of behavior encoded in the nervous system through hierarchical mechanism.
Shugoshin-like protein 2 (Sg2) plays a crucial role in coordinating essential cell processes during meiosis I in oocytes, ensuring accurate chromosome segregation by regulating cohesion, kinetochore-microtubule interactions, and the spindle assembly checkpoint. S2 protects centromeric cohesin through interaction with PP2A and silences the spindle assembly checkpoint by binding to Mad2, promoting congression and bi-orientation of bivalents on meiosis spindles. Additionally, S2 facilitates kinetochore-microtubule interactions and limits bivalent stretching by recruiting MCA and inhibiting Aurora kinase activity at kinetochores. Depletion of S2 in oocytes leads to pleiotropic consequences, including inefficient silencing of the spindle checkpoint, increased bivalent stretching, and alterations in kinetochore-microtubule attachments. The absence of S2 causes delayed bi-orientation of bivalents, increased bivalent stretching, and Aurora kinase activity at kinetochores, accompanied by changes in kinetoch-microtubule attachments and delayed APC/CC20 activation. Notably, S2 regulates sister chromatid cohesion, chromosome traction, the spindle checkpoint, and Aurora kinases. Mutations in S2 alter some but not all processes, indicating its pleiotropy is not due to secondary effects of a single function loss. S2 defective in PP2 binding fails to protect centromeric coin, yet its deficiency causes changes in chromosome and microtubule dynamics. Overall, S2 coordinates diverse processes for faithful chromosome segregation through multiple interactions, including PP2, Mad2, MCA, and Aurora kinase.
Researchers investigated the role of RNA localization and protein synthesis in experience-dependent changes in brain connectivity, focusing on whether local stimulation triggers directed trafficking of RNAs from the nucleus to synapses in addition to promoting local translation. Imaging of RNA localization and translation in cultured Aplysia sensory-motor neurons showed RNAs were delivered throughout the neuron arbor, but translation was enriched at sites of synaptic contact and stimulation. The study revealed a role for calcium-dependent retrograde netrin-1/DCC signaling in triggering local translation. Spatially restricting gene expression by regulating translation maximizes the readiness of the neuronal arbor to respond to local cues. Local stimulation regulates trafficking of RNAs from the nucleus to specific subcellular sites, with previous studies indicating activity can regulate mRNA localization within neurons. A trans-synaptic signal promotes translation of localized mRNAs, with netrin-1/DCC signaling involved in local translation at synapses. rRNA, specific mRNAs, ribosomal proteins, and translation factors were delivered throughout the neuron to both synaptic and non-synaptic sites, but translation was enriched at sites of synaptic contact and stimulation. Spatial regulation of gene expression in Aplyia sensory-motor neurons is primarily at the level of translation rather than RNA targeting from the nucleus. Every measure of translation demonstrated localized translation at synapses and stimulated synapses, suggesting mRNAs are delivered throughout the neuronal arbor. Release of netrin-1 from sensory or motor neurons during synapse formation and synaptic plasticity triggers translation at synaptic but not non-synaptic contacts. Incubation of Aplyia sensory-motor neuronal cultures with recombinant Fc-netrin-1 tested netrin-1 regulation of localized transcript synthesis. Netrin-1/DCC signaling plays a key role in local translation at synapses, maximizing the readiness of the neuronal arbor to respond to local cues by regulating translation rather than directing delivery of mRNAs to stimulated synapses.
Researchers have developed a method to control the spatial patterning of human induced pluripotent stem cells (hiPSCs) using CRISPR interference, allowing for the creation of complex multicellular structures with distinct cell types. Current methods to model morphogenic events lack control over cell-type emergence and the ability to selectively perturb specific subpopulations. The study employed an inducible CRISPR interference system in hiSCs to silence key proteins regulating cell adhesion and cortical tension, specifically ROCK1 and CDH. Mosaic knockdown of ROCK1 or CDH resulted in differential patterning within hiSC colonies due to cellular self-organization while retaining an epithelial pluripotent phenotype. The knockdown induces a transient wave of differential gene expression within mixed populations that stabilizes in coordination with observed self-organization. This approach enables genetic interrogation of emergent multicellular properties, facilitating understanding of molecular pathways regulating symmetry-breaking during morphogenesis. By creating mixed populations of hiSCs with and without the CRISPR system and inducing mosaic knockdown, symmetry-breaking events were genetically controlled within hiSC populations. Silencing target genes by CRISi within subpopulations provides avenues to control emergence of asymmetric phenotypes and multicellular pattern development. Mosaic knockdown interrogates creation of physical asymmetries in homogeneous populations leading to multicellular organization and pattern formation. Induction of mosaic ROCK1 or CDH results in 'bottom-up' cell-driven pattern formation of hiSC colonies while preserving pluripotency. The study identifies a strategy to direct emergence of heterotypic populations to control multicellular organization in pluripotent cells, facilitating creation of robust models for mechanistic study of human tissue patterning and formation. This method allows for selective perturbation of specific cell subpopulations, enabling better understanding of interactions between distinct cell types in multicellular structures. By manipulating hiSC subpopulations, researchers can control driven self-organization into repeatable patterns, addressing limitations of current methods that fail to discriminate between cells or account for emergence of heterotypic interactions in multicellular organization.
Researchers have proposed a normative theory to explain the decrease in encoding precision of visual working memory with an increase in the number of items, suggesting the brain minimizes a weighted sum of error-based behavioral cost and neural cost. A model constructed from this theory predicts set size effects, which are mediated by probing probability, aligning with previous findings. The model accounts for effects of set size and probing probability on encoding precision in nine delayed-estimation experiments and supports the prediction that total invested resource varies non-monotonically with set size. It is sometimes optimal to encode only a subset or none of relevant items in a task, raising the possibility that cognitive limitations arise from cost minimization rather than constraints. The theory provides a formalism to test speculation that selectivity of attention arises from balancing performance against costs associated with spiking and explains set effects in attention tasks. The model was tested on data from nine experiments, accounting for effects of set and probing with few parameters, including non-monotonic relation between set and total invested resource. At the neural level, this translates to a prediction of non-monotic relation between population-level spiking activity and set, which can be tested using neuroimaging experiments. The theory makes predictions about effects of incentive manipulations on precision as they affect the expected cost function.
Plants utilize autophagy as a defense mechanism to safeguard against infectious diseases, but the methods by which pathogens interfere with autophagy-related processes are not well understood. Research has shown that Pex54, an effector from the Irish potato pathogen Phytophthora infestans, binds to the autophagy protein ATG8, stimulating autophosome formation. Pex54 depletes the autagy receptor Joka out of ATG8 complexes and interferes with Joka's positive effect on pathogen defense. This indicates a plant pathogen has evolved to antagonize a host autagy receptor to counteract host defenses. Pex54 has an AT8 interacting motif that binds with high affinity to the autagy protein ATGCL and stimulates the formation of AT8-marked autagosomes. Unlike the Legionella effector RavZ, Pex54 activates selective autagy possibly to eliminate defense-related compounds or reattribute cellular resources by promoting nutrient recycling. Pex54 competes with the autagy receptor J2 and depletes it of AT8 autagosomes to promote disease susceptibility. Pathogens can disrupt and activate autagy for their own benefit, with Pex54 outcompeting the autagy receptor J2 and enhancing virulence. The molecular mechanisms that mediate defense-related autagy are poorly understood and how pathogens manipulate defense-related autagy or subvert autagy for nutrient uptake is unknown. Pex54 has evolved to bind host autagy protein AT8 to stimulate autophosome formation and depletes the autagy receptor J2 of AT8 complexes to counteract host defenses. Future studies will investigate pathogen modulation of the spatio-temporal dynamics of autagy and subcell trafficking during infection to determine whether other pathogens secrete effectors that target autagy in similar ways.
Human cardiac fibroblasts play a crucial role in tissue adaptation and inflammation in response to pathological conditions such as ischemia, where microenvironmental changes trigger a series of cellular responses. A comprehensive model of how environmental changes induce cardiac fibroblast responses is lacking due to the absence of approaches involving complex and simultaneous stimulation. This study provides an analysis of human cardiac fibroblast behavior using a multi-stimulus device for combined cyclic strain and controlled oxygen application, revealing differential responses to various stimuli combinations. Individual stimuli cause proliferative effects, including increased PH3 mitotic cells, YAP translocation, and PDGF secretion, or collagen presence. However, only the combination of hypoxia and simulated loss of contractility induces increased release of inflammatory and pro-fibrotic cytokines and matrix metalloproteinases.

Cardiac fibroblasts have been studied under physico-chemical stimulation such as mechanical stress, oxygen deprivation, and biochemical stimulation with pro-fibrotic cytokines. This study controlled mechanical strain and oxygen regimes sensed by fibroblasts to model environmental changes in cardiac injury, providing insights into individual and synergistic contributions of signals in activation of fibroblast responses relevant to fibrotic disease. After ischemic myocardial insult, tissue oxygen levels drop, and during the proliferative phase of myocardial healing, excessive fibroblast proliferation and phenotypic switch give rise to fibrot response. The study examined fraction of mitotic cells under combined stimulation and oxygen changes, showing mimicking hypox and reduced contractility proved crucial in eliciting inflammatory and fibrotic responses, while individual stimuli regulated proliferative and collagen-related responses.

The findings propose an innovative model recreating pathological environment for understanding fibroblast responses during injury, replicating the combination of oxygen and mechanical cues, highlighting adaptive mechanisms. The study provides a tool for understanding pathological mechanisms and tailoring reparative strategies, impacting future studies of myocardial remodeling. The combination of hypox and simulated loss of contractility is crucial for inflammatory and fibrotic responses, while individual stimuli regulate proliferation and collagen. The study elucidates human fibroblast behavior under controlled strain and oxygen, providing insights into environmental changes in cardiac injury and fibrotic disease. The model described in vitro provides a tool for understanding pathological mechanisms, tailoring reparative strategies, and studying myocardial remodeling.
Researchers have identified that alternative isoforms of the DOM nucleosome remodeling ATPase, DOM-A and DOM-B, specify two distinct multiunit complexes in Drosophila, both necessary for transcriptional regulation but through different mechanisms. The DOM-B complex incorporates H2A.V, the fly ortholog of H2.Z, genome-wide in an ATP-dependent manner, similar to the yeast SWR1 complex. In contrast, the DOM-A complex functions as an ATP-independent histone acetylase complex similar to yeast Nu4, targeting lysine 12 of histone H4. The DOM-A features arranged domains separated by the ATPase domain, suggesting it may serve as the central unit of a Nu4-type complex in Drosila. The ATP activity of DOM-A is dispensable for H4K12 acetylation by DOM, a principle possibly conserved across metazoans. H2.Z is primarily found at active promoters and enhancers, regulating transcription initiation and elongation. In Saccharomyces cerevisiae, H2.Z is introduced into chromatin by the SW1 complex, a multiunit ATP-dependent chromatin remodeler with IN80-type ATP SWR1 at its core. In yeast and humans, nucleosome remodeling and histone acetylase complexes originate from gene duplication and paralog specification, whereas Drosila generates diversity through alternative splicing of a single gene. Knockdowns of DOM-A, DOM-B, TIP, or H2 resulted in changes in transcription, indicating functional differences of the two isoforms on transcription. The study provides an example of evolutionary strategies leading to functional separation, illustrating how alternative splicing generates diversity amongst chromatin regulators. The SW1 and Nu4 complexes are separate entities in yeast, while in humans paralogous SRCAP and EP400 proteinases organize distinct complexes serving conserved functions. Data suggests most TIP resides in the DOM complex in Dros cells, and the existence of a Nu4-type complex with functional and structural similarity to yeast has not been reported. Overall, the research highlights the distinct roles of DOM-A and DOM-B in transcriptional regulation and chromatin remodeling in Drosila.
Neuromuscular junction formation requires proper interaction between motoneurons and muscle cells, and β-catenin (Ctnb1) in muscle is critical for motone differentiation. However, the relevant retrograde signal is not well understood. This study used an in vivo transgenic approach to dissect the functions of muscle Cnb1 and found that Cnb mutant without the transactivation domain was unable to rescue presynaptic deficits of Cnb mutation, indicating the involvement of transcription regulation. The cell-adhesion function of Cnb is dispensable. A screen for proteins that may serve as a Cnb-directed retrograde factor identified Slit2. Transgenic expression of Sl2 specifically in the muscle diminished presynaptic deficits by Cnb mutation in mice. Sl2 immobilized on beads induced synaptin puncta in axons of spinal cord explants, suggesting Sl2 serves as a factor utilized by muscle Cnb to direct presyn differentiation. Rescue experiments indicated a necessary role for transcription activity of muscle Cnb in presyn differentiation. Exploration of targets of Cnb as muscle-derived retrograde factors led to the identification of Sl2, an environmental cue that repels or collapses neuronal axons. Muscle overexpression of the Cnb mutant without the TAD was unable to rescue presynaptic deficits, indicating transcription is critical for muscle Cnb to control presyn differentiation. Recent reports of stable Cnb in muscle impairing presyn differentiation in mutant mice support the notion that Cnb in muscle is critical for a retrograde to nerve terminal development. Cnb associates with Tcf/Lef-elements in the promoter of the Sl2 gene, and Sl2 expression in cells is promoted by elevating Cnb. Sl2 is concentrated at the neuromuscular junction. Transgenic mice expressing Sl2 specifically in muscle diminished presynaptic deficits by Cnb mutation, including poor innervation, reduced vesicle density, and active zone, and depressed miniature end-plate potential. The study determined which function of Cnb is required for neuromuscular junction formation by characterizing transgenic mice expressing wild-type or Cnb mutants impaired in transcription regulation or cell-adhesion, suggesting muscle Cnb regulates presyn differentiation via gene-expression.
Researchers have developed computational and experimental approaches to quantify the dynamics of force transmission in cell clusters with subcellular and multicellular resolution, applying this technology to spontaneously forming adherent epithelial cell clusters. The study found that basal force fluctuations were coupled to E-cadherin localization at the level of individual cell junctions and cell-cell force exchange depended on the position within a cluster, adapting to reconfigurations due to cell divisions or positional rearrangements. Force transmission through a cell required coordinated modulation of cell-matrix adhesion and actomyosin contractility in the cell and its neighbors. Using a generalized cell-cell force measurement and analysis, the transmission of mechanical forces through cell-cell junctions of dynamic epithelial clusters was quantified spatially and temporally. Time-resolved force measurements demonstrated cell-cell forces at individual junctions fluctuate over time during interphase and cell divisions. The study investigated how basal force variations were coupled to the localization and recruitment of molecular components of cell-cell junctions, focusing on E-cadherin, the backbone of adherens junctions for epithelial cells. Local mechanical stresses correlated with localization of E-cadher molecules along the cell junction, and force propagation through cells can be intercepted by anchorage to the extracellular matrix and actomyin contraction. Force transmission across a cell is short-ranged, typically on the length of one cell diameter, due to anchoring and scrambling forces by cell-matrix adhesions and basal actomyin contractility. The study provides insights into tissue mechanics and highlights methods as a resource for studying mechanotransduction in cell adhesions, revealing mechanisms controlling mechanical homeostasis in dynamic epithelial tissues. Force exchange profiles along cell-cell interfaces were calculated at any point of a time-lapse video using fluorescence signals of GFP fused to E-cadherin, allowing for quantification of cell-cell force in a 3-cell cluster with changing topology.
Researchers investigated the structural and biochemical properties of huntingtin, a protein associated with Huntington's disease, using various biophysical experiments, including circular dichroism, single-particle microscopy, and cross-linking mass spectrometry. Huntingtin is composed of five distinct domains and adopts a spherical α-helical solenoid structure, with the amino-terminal and carbox-terminal regions folding to contain a circumscribed central cavity. The polyglutamine expansion increases α-helical properties of huntingtin and affects intramolecular interactions among domains. The study aimed to solve the puzzle of how the amino-terminal polyglutamine tract confers novel properties on huntingtin, causing disease, by conducting systematic biophysical and biochemical analyses of purified human recombinant huntingtins with varied polyutamine tracts. The impact of the polyglutamine tract on huntingtin's basic features suggests it may undergo conformational change upon interaction with binding partners. Single-particle microscopy of recomant human huntingtins with polyutamine lengths of 23-78-residues showed the protein's shape enables structural impact of the amino-terminal polyglutamine. Biophysical analyses of allelic series native recomant human huntingtins provide a solution to the mystery of how the amino-terminal polyglutamine modulates hunting structure and function. Circular dichroism experiments showed an increase in α-helical properties with longer polyglutamine tracts, with an average mean residue ellipticity of deg cm/dmol at 222nm, characteristic of α-helix, plotted against polyglutamine length. The study provides an elegant solution to the conundrum of how the extreme-terminal polyglutamine confers novel properties on huntingtin, causing disease, and delineates structural characteristics of full-length huntingtin affected by the polyutamine expansion.
Researchers have investigated the role of NCO4, a selective receptor for the autophagic turnover of ferritin, in regulating intracellular iron bioavailability. Analysis of the NCO4-FTH interaction revealed a direct association between a key arginine in FTH and a C-terminal element in NCO4 is required for delivery of ferritin to lysosomes via autophagosomes. NCO4 abundance is under dual control via autophagy and the ubiquitin proteome, with ubiquitin-dependent NCO4 turnover promoted by excess iron involving an iron-dependent interaction between NCO4 and the HER2 ubiquitin ligase. When iron levels are high, NCO4 abundance is low, promoting ferritin accumulation and iron capture, but when iron is low, NCO4 levels increase to promote ferritinagy. The interaction between NCO4 and F1 is mediated by a conserved NCO4 C-terminal domain and a conserved residue on F1, with mutation at these sites abolishing binding in vivo and abolishing ferritinagy. The HER2 ligase recognizes NCO4 under high iron conditions to mediate NCO4 turnover via the ubiquitin-proteome system, reducing steady-state NCO4 levels and increasing ferritin for iron capture. NCO4 plays an essential role in erythroid differentiation in zebraf and cultured cells. The study reveals the molecular nature of the NCO4-ferr complex and explains how intracellular levels modulate NCO4-mediated ferritin in cells in an iron-dependent setting. NCO4-mediated ferritinagy is important for maintaining cellular homeostasis and aberrant degradation of ferritin in lysosomes would be deleterious to the cell when intracellular iron is high. The mechanism of NCO4 suppression under iron-replete conditions involves the HER2 ubiquitin ligase and ubiqu-proteome system. Overall, NCO4 is crucial for erythropoiesis and its regulation by iron-dependent HER2-mediated proteolysis is essential for maintaining intracellular iron homeostasis.
The lateral prefrontal cortex (LPFC) plays a crucial role in higher-level cognition, but its functional organization has remained unclear. An influential theory proposes that the LPFC is organized hierarchically, with progressively rostral areas processing increasingly abstract information to facilitate efficient and flexible cognition. Recent human fMRI data revealed rostral-caudal gradients of abstraction in the LP, with dynamic modeling showing asymmetrical LP interactions indicative of hierarchical processing. Contrary to dominant assumptions, the strength of efferent versus afferent connections positioned midFC as the apex of the hierarchy. Cognitive demands induced connectivity modulations towards midFC consistent with a role in integrating information for control operations. The strengths of these dynamics were related to trait-measured higher-level cognitive ability, suggesting the LP is hierarchically organized with midFC synthesizing abstract and concrete information to control behavior.

The rostral-caudal axis of LP operates according to abstraction principles, with gradients of domain-generality and temporal control over action. Interactions among LP subregions showed asymmetrical influences indicative of hierarchical processing under cognitive demands. Hierarchical orientation of midFC in fixed connectivity and top-down influences from rostral LP to midFC during engagement of cognitive control were positively related to higher-level ability. The functional organization of LP is tied to cognitive capacity, with top-down control within LP a determinant of higher-level function. Stronger asymmetrical projections from midFC to other LP regions were related to better-level ability, suggesting a beneficial effect of hierarchical organization with midFC at the apex.

LP interactions were examined by varying demands on domain and control, engaging most of LP for a comprehensive account of function. Progressively rostral areas processed abstract levels of control, with Feature, Context, and Temporal control eliciting increasingly rostral activations. Dynamic modeling showed asymmetries in connectivity strongest in midFC, with cognitive demands eliciting top-down influences from rostral to mid and bottom-up from caudal to mid. Effective connectivity and modulations suggest midFC forms a nexus where information converges to influence action. Collectively, results indicate LP is hierarchically organized, with midFC positioned to synthesize abstract and concrete information to control, and top-down control within LP important for higher-level function.
The hominoid-specific gene TBC1D has been found to play a crucial role in the generation of basal progenitors and cortical folding in mice. Expression of TBCD3 in ventricular cortical progenitors via in utero electroporation caused delamination of ventricular radial glial cells and promoted generation of self-renewing basal progenitors with morphology of outer radial glia, which are most abundant in primates. Downregulation of T1D3 in human brain slices decreased generation of outer radial glia. Localized proliferation resulting from in utero electroporation or transgenic expression of T1D3 often underlie cortical regions exhibiting folding. T1 is required for outer radial glial generation regulating cortical expansion and folding. T1 promotes generation of outer radial cells in mice with high proliferative ability observed in primates accompanied by cortical folding. Human-specific ARHG11 has been shown to promote production of basal and cortical folding in electropated brain. T1 appeared earlier than ARHG11 during human evolution and acts as candidate controlling other genes in cortex and folding. Study provides proof that brain development can be regulated by duplicated genes during homoid evolution. Folding observed at regions containing electropated cells at postnatal and more pronounced at P7 suggesting cumulative effect of T1 on formation of gyrus cortical in mice. T expression in neurogenitors in utero at E13 caused reduced Trnp in flow cytometry-sorted cells at E15 indicating effect on intrinsic stemness pathways basal progenitors.
The activation of systemic acquired resistance in plants is associated with transcriptome reprogramming induced by the coactivator NPR1, which is facilitated by immune-induced ubiquitination and proteasomal degradation. Research investigated the role of ubiquitination of NPR1 prior to proteasomal turnover, revealing that it is a progressive event where initial modification by Cullin-RING E3 ligase promotes chromatin association and expression of target genes. Polyubiquination of NPR1, enhanced by the E4 ligase UBE, targets it for proteasomal degradation, while ubiquitin ligase activities are opposed by UBP/7, two proteome-associated deubiquitinases that enhance NPR longevity. Immune-induced transcript reprogramming requires sequential actions of E3 and E4 ligases balanced by opposing deubiquinases that fine-tune activity of NPR without strict requirement for sacrificial turnover. Loss of UBE largely uncouples NPR target gene expression from proteome activity, demonstrating the importance of progressive ubiquination for NPR activity. Initial CRL-mediated ubiquination is required for NPR to attain full transcriptional activity, while stepwise formation of ubiquitin chains mediated by UBE inactivates NPR and promotes degradation. Initial ubiquination boosts NPR transcriptional coactivator activity by enhancing promoter occupancy and potentially genomic mobility. The E4 ligase UBE extends pre-existing ubiquitin chains and has been implicated in degradation of NLR immune receptors. Prior to degradation, ubiquination acts as a transcription signal, providing a window for NPR to activate target genes. The study indicates ubiquitin chain and trimming activities fine-tune transcriptional outputs of unstable eukaryotic activators without requirement for sacrificial turnover. Ubiquination of NPR is a stepwise event requiring actions of CRL and E4 UBE, with CRL-mediated turnover preventing autoimmunity in absence of pathogen threat.
In Arabidopsis thaliana, 24 nucleotide small interfering RNAs guide cytosine methylation and silencing of transposons and a subset of genes. The biogenesis of these 24nt siRNAs requires nuclear RNA polymerase IV, RNA-dependent RNAase 2, and DICER-like 3. Researchers identified Pol IV and RDR-dependent RNAs that accumulate in dcl mutants and are diced into 24nt RNAs by DCL in vitro. These P4 RNAs are mostly 26-45nt and initiate with a purine adjacent to pyrimidine, characteristics shared by Pol IV transcripts generated in vitro. R2 terminal transferase activity demonstrated in vitro may account for non-templated nucleotides at P4 RNA 3 termini. The 24 siAs primarily correspond to the 5 or 3 ends of P4As, suggesting a model where siAs are generated from either end of P4 duplexes by single dicing events. Sequence features of 24 siAs and P RNAs show frequencies at which the four nucleotides are present at the 5 terminus of 20-25 small RNAs in wild-type plants or the 5i of PAs in d2d3d4 triple mutants. Pol and R2 are each essential for biogenesis of 24 siAs and physically associate in Arabidopsis and maize. Dozens of PAs can overlap at a given locus but individual PAs represent Pol-R2 transcription units. Data indicate short-stranded PAs in d3 mutants are immediate precursors of siAs based on ability of D3 to cut precursors into 24As in vitro. Browser views of si loci show PAs accumulate in d2d3d4 mutants observed in wild-type plants but not in Pol or R2 mutants. si signals reduced upon ribonuclease digestion yet unaffected by ribuclease suggesting si pool exists double-stranded. PAs in d2/3 triple mutants are substrates for D3 and RNA isolated from d2/3 mutant incubated with FLAG-D3 had affinity-purified from transgenic plants using anti-FLAG resin.
Mature neural networks synchronize and integrate spatiotemporal activity to support cognition, with the emergence of these patterns and functions believed to be developmentally regulated. However, the postnatal time course for neural networks to perform complex computations remains unknown. Research using high spatiemporal resolution in vivo electrophysiology in immature mice reveals that mature cortical processes emerge rapidly and simultaneously after a discrete but volatile transition at the beginning of the second postnatal week of rodent development. This transition is characterized by relative neural quiescence, after which spatially distributed, temporally precise, and internally organized activity occurs. A similar developmental trajectory is demonstrated in humans, suggesting an evolutionarily conserved mechanism that facilitates a transition in network operation. The transient quiescent period is hypothesized to be requisite for the subsequent emergence of coordinated networks. The transition between immature and mature dynamics is marked by decreased cellular and synaptic activity at the beginning of the second week, followed by the emergence of precise synchronization and oscillatory coupling in space and time. Multiple cognitive functions emerge during early development, with neural networks enabling precise spatiotemporal coordination of synaptic and cellular activity in mature brain functions. The maturation of network dynamics is preceded by a discrete quiescent evolutionally conserved period during postnatal development, with oscillations and spiking displaying a nadir signaling a shift from local, loosely correlated, sensory-driven patterns to internally organized, spatially, temporally precise activity. Immature patterns disappearance herald the emergence of advanced network properties, with dramatic changes in anatomical and functional connectivity and synaptogenesis in superficial layers. Neural spiking is recruited to oscillatory activity with strong phase-locking and temporal precision, characteristic of adult networks in information processing, indicating a transient developmental perturbation as an evolution conserved indicator of a shift in network operations.
The population structure of multi-copy antigen-encoding genes in Plasmodium falciparum is a complex issue with significant implications for epidemiology and control. An agent-based model has been developed to extend earlier strain theory by addressing the var gene family of Plasium falciparum, considering the extensive diversity of multi-copy genes that undergo antigenic variation via sequential, mutually exclusive expression. The model tracks the dynamics of unique repertoires in a population of hosts and shows that even under high levels of sexual recombination, strain competition mediated through cross-immunity structures the parasite into a subset of coexisting repires of var genes whose degree of antigen overlap depends on transmission intensity. Empirical comparison of patterns of genetic variation at antigen and neutral sites supports the role of immune selection in structuring parasite diversity. The model considers the effects of immune selection in populations of pathogens whose antigen-encoding loci are multi-copy and recombining, exploring whether structured populations resulting from selection pressures exerted by competition among distinct repires for hosts, mediated by cross-immunity, can emerge. Mathematical models have demonstrated that immune selection can lead to the maintenance of discrete, non-overlapping antigenic repires, even when antigen variability is driven by several unlinked genes that undergo recombination. The var model suggests control strategies that reduce transmission will not drastically reduce gene diversity, as a reservoir of parasite genomes with diverse repires remains. The agent-based model extends strain theory to address the effect of immune acting on multi-copy genes using var family of Plasmodium falcum as the basis, encoding Plasmodium falcum erythrocyte membrane protein, the major surface antigen of parasite's blood stages binding host endothelial receptors allowing sequest within host tissues.
Deamidation of a glutamine residue in the ubiquitin-conjugating enzyme UBC13 by the enteric bacteria Shigella flexneri deamase Osp converts it to glutamate, resulting in the silencing of polyubiquination activity in complex with the RING-finger ubiquitin ligase TRAF6 and the downstream NFκB inflammatory response. The precise role of deamation in silencing the U13/TRAF complex involves disrupting the interaction between U13 and TR6 RING-domain by perturbing native and transient interactions. Deamation creates a new intramolecular salt-bridge in U13 that competes with a critical intermolecular salt-bridge at the native U13/TR6 interface, preventing transient interactions necessary to form a typical U13/RING complex. Additionally, repulsion between the negatively charged E100 and the negatively charged surface of RING prevents transient interactions in the U13/RING complex. This post-translational modification perturbs the conformation and stability of transient complexes to inhibit protein-protein association. The Shigella flexi effector OI suppresses the inflammatory NF-κ pathway via deamation of U13 at Q100, inhibiting synthesis of K63-linked polyubitin chains by the U13/TR6 complex and preventing NF-κ activation. Q100 is present at the U13/TR6 interface but does not form direct contact with TR6, serving as a scaffold for TAK2 and IK kinases, leading to phosphorylation and activation of IK kinases, Iκ degradation, and nuclear translocation of transcription N-κ. OI functions as a deamase targeting glutamine in U13, converting it to glutamate. Deation inhibits U13 binding to TR6 and longer dimer TR6, confirming secondary interactions between Ub/RING or Ub/zincfinger cannot compensate the loss of primary E/RING interaction. Unlike RKIP, deamation does not alter U13 fold, with intramolecular salt-bridges competing intermolecular salt-bridge in U13. Hotspots and electrostatic substitutions at the interface can increase protein-protein affinity, shifting the equilibrium between native and transient complexes. Deam disrupts U13/TR interaction by three mechanisms: creating a new intramolecular R/E100 salt-bridge, pertbing U13/TR transient complexes, and repulsion between negatively charged E100 and TR interface.
Researchers investigated the effects of activating and inactivating cortical interneurons, specifically somatostatin-positive (Sst+) and parvalbumin-positive (Pb+) interneurons, in the auditory cortex of awake mice. Using optogenetic manipulation and single-unit recordings, they found that inactivation of S+ interurons increased response gain, while inactivation of P+ interurons weakened tuning and decreased information transfer, suggesting these neurons support distinct functions. However, activating S+ and P+ interurons revealed no differences. A network model was used to understand this asymmetry, showing that small changes in parameters such as spontaneous activity or manipulation strength determined whether activation and inactivation produced consistent or paradoxical conclusions regarding interneuron functions. Inactivation of S+ interurons increased response, while inactivation of P+ interurons weakened tuning and decreased transfer, implying specific functions in the auditory cortex. Activating S+ and P+ interurons showed no differences. The study tested the assumption that causal data can be interpreted in a straightforward way by manipulating S+ or P+ interurons while recording neural responses to tones in the auditory cortex. A linear framework was applied to frequency-tuning curves of units from Ch2/S and Ch2 mice, excluding units suppressed by more than 80% and including narrow-spiking and broad-spiking units. Stimulus preferences of interneurons are important for determining output, with P+ cells activated by a broad set of tones and S+ responding to a narrower range. Comparing a neuron's frequency-tuning curve in light-on versus light-off conditions revealed whether the change in firing fit a multiplicative, divisive, additive, or combination model.
Researchers investigated the role of oscillatory synchronization in working memory, a cognitive function that emerges from large-scale network interactions, by applying transcranial alternating current stimulation to modulate oscillatory activity in the right frontoparietal network. Externally induced synchronization improved performance when cognitive demands were high, with simultaneously collected fMRI data revealing stimulation effects dependent on phase and internal processing state. Synchronous stimulation during verbal tasks increased parietal activity, correlating with behavioral performance, while functional connectivity results showed frontopari phase influences information flow within the network. The study aimed to provide causal evidence for oscillatory synchronization's role in demanding working memory, investigating neural mechanisms by which frontopari phase influences verbal working memory through two experiments using transcranial current stimulation to entrain endogenous rhythms. Findings indicate a direct link between behavioral performance in demanding working-memory tasks and large-scale synchronization across the rightopari network, demonstrating manipulations of phase modulate underlying brain activity and influence long-range connectivity in phase and state-dependent manner. Hypotheses tested included enhancement of behavioral performance by entraining synchronous activity, phase-dependent modulation of regional brain activity, correlation of BOLD changes with performance, and phase-dependent modulation of functional connectivity. Results showed front interactions increased with synchronous stimulation and posterior interactions with desynchronous stimulation, indicating information flow through brain regions can be differentially affected by phase. Synchronous stimulation caused improvement and increased frontopari activity when demands were high, demonstrating external manipulations of large-scale synchronization shape activity and connectivity within networks, detectable by fMRI. Overall, findings demonstrate a link between behavioral performance in demanding working-memory tasks and large-scale synchronization, providing a direct demonstration of phase synchronization's role in cognitive processes and associated neural correlates.
Embryonic polarity in in invertebrates, amphibians, and fish is specified by maternal determinants that fix cell fates early in development, whereas amniote embryos remain plastic and can form multiple individuals until gastrulation. In chick embryos, the earliest known factor is cV1, a transforming growth factor beta signal expressed posteriorly before gastrulation. A molecular screen to find upstream regulators of cV1 in normal and manipulated embryos to form twins uncovers the transcription factor Pitx as a candidate. Pitx is essential for axis formation and acts as a direct regulator of cV1 expression by binding to enhancers within neighboring genes. Pitx, Vg/GDF, and Nodal are key actors in left-right asymmetry, suggesting the same ancient polarity mechanism has been co-opted to different functions during evolution. 

In most invertebrates and anamni vertebrates, embryonic polarity is established by localisation of maternal determinants in cytoplasm and/or cortex of fertilized egg, generating differences between blastomeres that form by cell division, culminating in orientation of embryonic axes. Higher vertebrate embryos retain regulative development until late stages, suggesting localization of maternally inherited determinants is not essential for specifying embryo polarity. The transcription factor Pitx regulates position of embryonic axis, embryonic regulation, and twinning. Pitx is a good candidate for upstream regulator of c1, a transcription factor expressed in same domain as c1 in whole embryos, bisected marginal zone, and before c1. Time-course expression of Pitx in whole embryos, isolated halves, and comparison with c1 shows Pitx is essential for axis formation, acting as direct regulator of c1. Results implicate Pitx as earliest gene regulating position of embryonic axis and twinning.
Temporal experience of odor gradients is crucial for spatial orientation in animals, and the fruit fly Drosophila melanaster exhibits robust odor-guided behaviors in odor fields. To investigate how early olfactory circuits process temporal variations of olfactory stimuli, researchers subjected flies to precisely defined odor concentration waveforms and examined spike patterns of olfactory sensory neurons (OSNs) and projection neurons (PNs). A significant transformation between OSN and PN spike patterns was found, with PN output strongly signaling OS spike rate and its rate of change. A two-dimensional model admitting OS spike rate and its rate of change as inputs closely predicted PN output. When cascaded with rate-change encoding by OSNs, PNs primarily signal acceleration and rate of change of dynamic stimuli to higher brain centers, enabling animals to respond reliably to onsets of concentrations.

PNs relay olfactory information to higher centers such as the mushroom body and lateral horn. Recent studies reported Drosila OSNs encode not only odor concentration but also its rate of change as a function of time. Building on this, researchers asked how PNs contribute to internal representations of dynamic olfactory environments. First and second-order neurons in Drosila olfactory system were tested with varying concentrations, constructing a 2D model for temporal olfactory processing. The 2D nonlinearity and low prediction error supported the hypothesis that PN rate is strongly dependent on rate of change of feedforward OS input.

Detecting encountered odor plumes is crucial for flying insects before drifting relative to adjacent plumes. Dros early olfactory system signals acceleration of concentrations to higher centers, allowing detection at an early phase. Studies investigated dynamic stimuli in early olfactory systems, observing temporal processing of signals. Researchers hypothesize acceleration encoding in Dros antennal lobe allows higher centers to respond to onsets of slowly rising stimuli. A cascade of rate-change encodings leads to rapid detection, advancing peak time near stimulus onset in response to triangle-shaped input. This mechanism enables animals to detect onsets of odor concentrations, crucial for orientation in dynamic environments.
In flowering plants, seed development is initiated by the fusion of the maternal egg and central cells with two paternal cells, leading to the formation of the embryo and endosperm, respectively. The fertilization products are surrounded by a maternally derived seed coat, whose development prior to fertilization is blocked by epigenetic regulators belonging to the Polycomb Group protein family. Research has shown that fertilization of the central cell results in the production of auxin, likely exported to maternal tissues, which drives seed coat development by removing Polycomb function. Mutants of the MADS-box transcription factor AGL62 have impaired transport of auxin from endosperm to integuments, resulting in seed abortion. AGL regulates auxin transport from endosperm to integuments, leading to removal of Polycomb block on seed development. Data suggests production of auxin in developing endosperm is the trigger for seed coat development. AGL62 mutants abort early after fertilization, correlating with endosperm cellularization and failure to develop a seed coat. A dual role for post-fertilization auxin production is proposed, driving endosperm and seed development. AGL, like auxin, is involved in mediating endosperm and seed developmental pathways. Multiple lines of evidence support this hypothesis, including auxin accumulation in integuments after fertilization, impaired auxin biosynthesis in endosperm causing seed coat defects, and ovules producing auxin ectopically in central cell initiating seed development without fertilization. Failure of seed development in AGL mutants correlates with failure of auxin transport. The seed-initiation signal is generated in fertilized central/endosperm, requiring auxin production and transport to ovule integuments in AGL-dependent manner. Fertilization of central cell results in auxin production, exported to integuments, lifting Polycomb block, allowing cell elongation and differentiation, dependent on AGL activity. AGL seeds fail to export auxin from endosperm, leading to accumulation and failure to develop seed coat.
The interaction between a bacteriophage and its host is mediated by the phage's receptor binding protein, and despite its fundamental role in governing phage activity and host range, the molecular rules of receptor binding protein function remain a mystery. Researchers systematically dissected the functional role of every residue in the tip of the T7 phage receptor binding protein domain by developing a high-throughput, locus-specific phage engineering method, creating 166 variants. This dataset allowed comparison of functional profiles across hosts to identify regions of importance, many previously unknown. Substitution patterns showed host-specific differences in position and physicochemical properties, revealing molecular adaptation to individual hosts. Gain-of-function variants against resistant hosts and host-constrict variants that eliminated certain hosts were discovered. Engineered T7 variants against urinary tract pathogens demonstrated therapeutic utility, presenting a framework for characterizing sequence-function relationships in phage-bacterial systems.

A high-throughput locus-specific phage engineering method, ORACLE, was developed to create a large unbiased library of phage variants at a targeted locus. Using ORACLE, the tip domain was comprehensively mutated, making single amino acid substitutions at every site, and the functional role of variants on multiple hosts was quantified. High-resolution maps were generated, delineating regions with function-enhancing substitutions and host-specific patterns, many previously unknown. T7 variants with greater virulence than wildtype T7 were discovered, demonstrating natural phages can be engineered for higher efficacy. Results indicate ORACLE can generate phage variants superior to wildtype, potential starting points for engineering therapeutic phages. Further experiments are required to assess in vivo efficacy of T7 variants.

Most phages selectively target a narrow range of hosts, unable to infect closely related hosts, suggesting directionality to phage-bacterial interactions and orientational bias of the tip with respect to the bacterial surface. Key lessons from host screens include single amino substitutions generating broad functional diversity, highlighting evolutionary adaptability of the receptor binding protein. Engineered T7 variants avert emergence of spontaneous resistance in pathogenic Escherichia coli urinary tract infections, explaining molecular drivers of adaptability of the domain and key regions determining activity and host range. The study presents a framework for characterizing sequence-function relationships in many phage-bacterial systems and demonstrates therapeutic utility of engineered T7 variants against urinary pathogens.
Individuals with congenital amusia have a lifelong history of unreliable pitch processing and tend to downweight pitch cues during speech perception, instead relying on other dimensions such as duration. A study investigated the neural basis of this strategy using fMRI, where 15 individuals with amusia and 15 controls read sentences with a comma indicating a grammatical boundary and then selected the best match from two spoken sentences differing in pitch and/or duration cues. Prominent reductions in functional connectivity were detected in the amus group between left prefrontal language-related and right hemisphere pitch-related regions, reflecting between-group differences in cue weights. Connectivity differences were not present during a control task. Results indicate the reliability of perceptual dimensions is linked to functional connectivity between frontal and perceptual regions, suggesting a compensatory mechanism. Individuals with amus exhibited decreased connectivity between frontal areas and right hemisphere pitch-related regions, consistent with prior findings that they rely less on pitch to process spoken language. The study used a whole-brain-driven approach to examine differences in functional connectivity during speech encoding in pitch-related tasks, finding right hemisphere areas are less integrated with frontal regions when processing speech and language. Null results for group differences in activation during speech processing are consistent with prior reports that amus and controls do not differ in pitch representations within sensory regions. Brain encoding of pitch in speech and musical stimuli is unimpaired in individuals with amia, suggesting amus neglect pitch due to implicit learning of unreliable pitch memory, reflected in decreased connectivity between auditory and task-relevant areas integrating perceptual information.
Devil Facial Tumour 2 (D2) is a recently discovered contagious cancer affecting the Tasman devil, a species that already harbors another contagious cancer, Devil Facial Tum 1 (D1). In contrast to D1, D2 cells express major histocompatibility complex (MHC) class I molecules, indicating that loss of MHC is not necessary for the emergence of contagious cancer. However, the highly expressed MHC class I alleles in D2 cells are common among host devils or non-polymorphic, reducing immunogenicity in the population sharing these alleles. D2 cells express classical and non-classical MHC I molecules both in vitro and in vivo, unlike D1, which has lost MHC I expression. The dominantly expressed MHC I allele in D2 is shared with host devils, and MHC I expression varies among D2 tumors in vivo, suggesting MHC I negative subclones could emerge as D2 transmits more widely. The similarity of MHC I alleles between D2 and devils may reflect a shared geographical and genotype pool to the cancer's founder, and the frequency of this allele among the population may impact D2 spread. Heterogenic expression of MHC molecules in D2 biopsies suggests MHC expression is not fixed and may be gradually lost in D2. The study proposes contagious cancers exploit histocompatibility between tumor and host, but loss of allogenic antigens could facilitate D2 transmission. The immune response to D2 and its role in tumor evolution requires further analysis, and it is possible D1 downregulated MHC I expression after becoming transmissible, with initial transmission among individuals sharing MHC I alleles. Overall, D2 reveals a mechanism for the emergence of contagious cancer, with partial histocompatibility between tumor and host and potential loss of allogenic antigens facilitating transmission.
The granular retrosplenial cortex (R) is crucial for both spatial and non-spatial behaviors, but the underlying neural codes remain poorly understood. Research using optogenetic mapping in mice reveals a dissociation allowing parallel circuits in superficial R to process disparate inputs. The anterior thalamus and dorsal subiculum, sources of spatial information, strongly recruit low-threshold (LR) pyramidal cells in R, while regular-spiking (RS) cells are preferentially controlled by claustral and anterior cing inputs, sources of non-spatial information. Precise sublaminar axonal and dendritic arborization within R layer 1 permits parallel processing. Thalamocortical synaptic dynamics enable computational models of LR neurons to compute the speed of head rotation despite receiving head direction inputs that do not explicitly encode speed. Parallel streams identify a distinct neuronal subtype positioned to support spatial computations in R. LR neurons receive directional and spatial inputs from the anterior thalam and subiculum. Synaptic dynamics of thamic inputs to cells give rise to coding of angular speed in cells. Anterior thalam and dorsal subiculum control LR neurons, while claustrum and anterior cing control RS cells. Synaptic dynamics of thamic inputs allow robust encoding of angular speed by cell models, though inputs contain information about head direction, not speed. Layer 3 of R contains two types of pyramidal neurons: low-threshold and regular-spiking. Distinct morphology and intrinsic properties of LR neurons make them suited to integrate head input from thalam and spatial inputs from hippocal formation via subiculum. Integration of directional and distance information is key function of retrosial as whole.
Traumatic brain injury (TBI) is a prominent risk factor for dementias including tauopathies like chronic traumatic encephalopathy (TE). The mechanisms that promote prion-like spreading of Tau aggregates after TBI are not fully understood, in part due to lack of tractable animal models. Researchers have introduced a 'tauopathy reporter' zebrafish expressing a genetically encoded Tau biosensor that reliably reports accumulation of human Tau species when seeded via intraventric brain injections. Subjecting zebrafish larvae to a novel TBI paradigm produced various TBI features including cell death, post-traumatic seizures, and Tau inclusions. The application of dynamin inhibitors or anticonvulsant drugs rescued TBI-induced tauopathy and cell death, suggesting a role for seizure activity in the prion-like seeding and spreading of tau following TBI. Zebfish larvae offer a potent complement to rodent models of TBI and cellular models of tauopathy, allowing the revelation of post-traumatic seizures as a drable mechanistic link between TBI and prion progression of tauopathy. Neuronal excitability and seizure activity after TBI can play a role in accelerating the dissemination of Tau pathology. A novel in vivo biosensor in zebish visualizes pathological spreading and accumulation within the intact CNS, revealing a substantial burst of neural activity during TBI and a higher propensity for spontaneous seizures in zebfish exposed to TBI. TBI induces Tau aggregates in the Tau biosensor model and post-traumatic seizures correlate with spreading pathology. Manipulating seizure activity mitigates Tau aggregation, revealing a critical role for endocytosis in the prion spread of seeds in vivo. The results implicate seizures and dyn-dependent endocytosis in the spread of Tau, offering potential therapeutic targets. The study suggests anti-convants that dampen post-traumatic seizures as a route to moderating subsequent tauopathy.
Brain connectivity is often considered in terms of communication between functionally distinct brain regions, with many studies investigating the extent to which patterns of coupling between neural populations relate to behavior. The spatial arrangement of cortical regions interacts with measures of brain connectivity, and the shape and location of brain regions strongly influence the modeling of connectivity. The spatial arrangement of functional regions is predictive of non-imaging measures of behavior and lifestyle. Cross-subject variations in the spatial configuration of brain regions are often interpreted as changes in functional connectivity, highlighting the importance of understanding these effects when interpreting the relationship between functional imaging and cognitive traits. Interactions between neural populations and the spatial configuration of regions influence functional connectivity, with resting state functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) revealing complex patterns of functional connectivity through spontaneous fluctuations in distinct brain regions. The organization of the brain into large-scale networks has been extensively investigated over the past decades, with significant advances made to tackle the issue of spatial misalignment across individuals. Partial canonical correlation analysis results show network matrices contain little unique trait-level cross-subject information not reflected in spatial organization, implying cross-subject information in commonly adopted connectivity fingerprints largely reflects spatial variability in location rather than coupling strength. The spatial arrangement of brain regions interacts with measures of connectivity and is predictive of behavior and lifestyle, emphasizing the need to disambiguate the influence of spatial topography to enable estimation of fMRI measures that uniquely reflect coupling strength between neural populations. Understanding these effects is crucial for interpreting the relationship between functional imaging and cognitive traits, as cross-subject variations in spatial configuration are often misinterpreted as changes in functional connectivity. Recent studies have used functional fingerprints to characterize individuals' brain activity, but evidence suggests these may reflect spatial variability in location rather than coupling, highlighting the need for a better understanding of these effects to accurately interpret functional data and cognitive traits.
Researchers investigated the role of TBP and TBP2 in the transcriptional regulation of muscle-specific genes in skeletal cells, as previous studies suggested that replacement of the canonical TFIID-TBP complex with TRF3/TBP is required for muscle gene expression. However, TBP null mice did not exhibit a developmental phenotype, prompting further analysis of TBP's role in adult myogenesis. Results showed TBP null mice have intact muscle regeneration potential upon injury and TBP is not expressed in C2C12 muscle cells or primary muscle stem cells. TFIID subunits and TBP are downregulated during myoblast differentiation, but reduced amounts of these proteins form a complex detectable on muscle gene promoters, essential for their expression, indicating TBP does not replace TBP during differentiation. TBP is the essential component of the pre-initiation complex that promotes muscle gene expression in differentiated muscles. TBP2 is not expressed in myotubes derived from C2C12 myoblasts or primary muscle stem cells and TBP-deficient mice displayed intact regeneration potential. Muscle stem cells isolated from TBP-deficient muscles differentiated into myotubes with the same efficiency as wild-type cells. TBP expression was detected in muscle-derived myotubes and C2 myotubes. The requirement of TBP for muscle differentiation and absence of T2 in myotubes suggests the TFI complex may drive gene transcription during myoblast differentiation. Expression of TAF subunits of TFI complex was detected in C12 myoblast and myot at RNA and protein levels. Comparable numbers of muscle stem cells were found in wild-type and TBP null mice 12 days after injury, indicating an intact capacity for self-renewal and differentiation during muscle regeneration in the absence of T2. These findings demonstrate that TBP, not T2, is essential for muscle gene expression and T2 does not replace TBP during muscle differentiation, limiting amounts of TFI-TBP required to promote muscle-specific expression.
