Researchers investigated the effects of targeted cortical reorganization using optogenetics in non-human primates, specifically examining how brain stimulation modulates the excitability of neural circuits and drives neuroplasticity. A large-scale optogenetic interface enabled simultaneous stimulation of excitatory neurons and electrocortic recording across primary somatosensory and motor cortex in macaques. The study tracked two measures of network connectivity, the response to focal stimulation and the coherence between electrodes, which were strongly correlated before stimulation. Stimulation in primary somatosensory or motor areas significantly strengthened functional connectivity between these areas within minutes. At a finer scale, stimulation led to heterogeneous changes across the network, reflecting correlations introduced by stimulation-evoked activity consistent with Hebbian plasticity models. The findings demonstrate a relationship between stimulus-evoked correlations and changes in baseline connectivity across the network, explained by a Heian model of large-scale stimulation-induced plasticity. Stimulation-evoked activity drives large-scale plasticity in a Heian manner, informing future neurorehabilitation strategies. A large-scale optogen interface enabled simultaneous stimulation of populations of excitatory neurons while recording large-scale electrocortic activity across brain areas, establishing two measures of functional connectivity providing complementary views of mechanisms of plasticity. Hebb rules explain synaptic plasticity, and large-scale-induced changes are consistent with Hebbianity, comparing correlations directly induced by stimulation with changes in network correlations after conditioning. The ability to record artifact-free signals during stimulation allowed this test, supporting a role for Hebb learning in sensorimotor corticality. The large-scale interface enabled robust estimation of functional connectivity and stimulation-evoked correlations, extending Hebb models to large circuits with implications for stimulation-based neurorehabilitation.
A rare case of a Rhesus macaque monkey, likely born without primary visual cortex V1, was studied to understand the neural consequences of such damage on visual perception. Despite the absence of V1, the monkey's behavior was unremarkable, but visual task performance was impaired. Multimodal magnetic resonance imaging showed that visual structures outside the lesion appeared normal. Visual stimulation under anesthesia with checkerboards activated the lateral geniculate nucleus, while full-field moving dots activated the pulvinar. Cortical activation was sparse but included face patches, consistently across lesion and control monkeys, functional analysis revealed an intact network of bilateral dorsal visual areas temporally correlated with V5/MT activation, even without V1. Despite robust subcortical responses to visual stimulation, there was little evidence for strengthened subcortical input to V5/MT supporting residual function or blindsight phenomena. The monkey's visual structures, both cortical and subcortical, outside the large lesion of primary cortex remained intact, with little evidence of atrophy, suggesting a role in residual function consistent with an intact network of extrastriate cortical areas comparable to control monkeys. The structural connectivity between subcortical regions and area V5MT was weak, but the microstructure was intact. No significant V5MT activation was found in the monkey to either checkerboard or moving stimuli, however, connectivity analysis revealed a network of cortical areas showing similar BOLD response over time to V5MT. Foci of activation in the region of V5MT in dorsal STS of both hemispheres suggested possible subthreshold activation, difficult to detect with BOLD. Retinal information may reach dorsal areas via weak pathways LGN and pulvinar, but lack of consistent cortex activation possibly due to anesthesia made this difficult to confirm. The lack of differences between monkey and controls in pathways LGN-V5MT and pulvin-V5MT suggested either could support residual abilities. Overall, an intact extrastriate visual network was preserved in the monkey despite substantial naturally occurring damage to primary visual cortex.
The innate immune sensor IFI16 recognizes foreign DNA in the nucleus by scanning along the duplex. The ability to recognize double-stranded DNA of pathogenic origin in the intracellular environment is an essential defense mechanism of the human immune system. However, the molecular mechanisms underlying distinction between foreign DNA and host material inside the nucleus are not understood. By combining biochemical assays and single-molecule techniques, researchers show that the nuclear immune sensor IF16 one-dimensionally tracks long stretches of exposed foreign dsDNA to assemble supramolecular platforms. Nucleosomes represent barriers that prevent IF16 from targeting host DNA directly by interfering these one-dimensional movements. This unique scanning-assisted mechanism allows IF16 to distinguish friend from foe and assemble oligomers efficiently on foreign DNA. The interferon-inducible protein 16 is a key innate sensor detecting foreign DNA and uses it as a scaffold to assemble signaling platforms in the nucleus and cytoplasm. IF16 plays a central role in defense against pathogens such as herpes simplex-1. Researchers report the observation of a unifying mechanism explaining how IF16 resolves central issues in initiating foreign-DNA pathways. IF16 assembles faster on longer DNA composed of functional domains flanked unstructured linkers, namely pyrin domain and two DNA-binding domains. The 1-diffusion assembly plays a role regulating IF16 immune responses. Chromation acts a key feature allowing IF16 distinguish host from foreign DNA in the nucleus. By time-resolved bulk and single-molecule assays demonstrate IF16 scans exposed DNA assemble distinct clusters and chromation inhibits targeting host DNA for assembly. In vivo this mechanism allows a number of IF16 molecules to allocate large genomes of invading pathogens. The molecular mechanism by which innate sensors distinguish self from foreign DNA has been a major question in immunology. Using time-resolved bulk and single-molecule assays demonstrate IF16 scans along DNA assemble distinct clusters that chromation sufficient inhibit targeting host DNA for assembly.
Spatially Targeted Optical Microproteomics (STOMP) is a novel proteomics technique that allows for the interrogation of micron-scale regions of interest in mammalian tissue without the need for genetic manipulation. Methanol or formalin-fixed specimens are stained with fluorescent dyes or antibodies to visualize regions of interest, then soaked in solutions containing a photo-tag, 4-benzoylbenzyl-gly-hexahidine. Confocal imaging with two-photon excitation is used to covalently couple photo-tags to all proteins within the region of interest to a resolution of 0.67µm in the xy-plane and 1.48µm axially. After solubilization, photo-tagged proteins are isolated and identified by mass spectrometry. The technique was tested on amyloid plaques in an Alzheimer's disease (AD) mouse model and post-mortem AD case, confirming known plaque constituents and discovering new ones. STOMP can be applied to various samples including cell lines, primary cultures, ex vivo specimens, biopsy samples, and post-mortem tissue.

Gel electrophoresis of indiscrimately tagged proteins reveals a different pattern of protein bands compared to specimens where amyloid plaques were specifically targeted for STOMP analysis. The volume of tissue needed for photo-tagging depends on instrument sensitivity, fixation method, sample complexity, and other factors. A semi-automated technique, STOMP combines two-photon laser microscopy with photochemical labeling and mass spectrometry. Two-photon excitation drives microscopic 3D-resolved photochemistry. The predominant protein tagged in STOMP sample is Aβ, which is not visible in non-plaque due to low abundance. The photo-tagging procedure uses two-photon microscopy rather than conventional confocal microscopy. STOMP offers single-micron three-dimensional resolution, an order of magnitude better than current state of the art.

Laser from microscope is used to image fluorescently stained specimen and photochemically crosslink purification ligands to protein components within pathological deposits. The photo-ligand used is a peptide sequence 4-benzlbenz-Gly-His-His-His-His-His-CON2. Benzoylbenzene forms covalent bonds with CH and NH groups upon excitation. STOMP provides clues to etiology of neurodegenerative disease, advancing research in cell biology by yielding composition of small features not amenable to biochemical purification. The technique has potential for various applications including cell lines, primary cultures, ex vivo specimens, biopsy samples, and post-mortem tissue. STOMP represents the first application of two-photon to photolabeling, offering high resolution and sensitivity.
The Frizzled3 (Fz3) gene plays a crucial role in controlling axonal development in distinct populations of cranial and spinal neurons. Disruption of the F3 gene leads to defects in axonal growth in the VIIth and XIIth cranial nerves, the phrenic nerve, and the dorsal nerve in fore- and hindlimbs. In F3−/− limbs, dorsal axons stall at a precise location in the nerve plexus and do not reroute to other trajectories. Affected motor neurons undergo cell death 2 days prior to the normal wave of developmental cell death that coincides with innervation of muscle targets, indicating that developing neurons with long-range axons are programmed to die unless their axons arrive at intermediate targets on schedule. The absence of F3 leads to a specific stalling defect among LM axons in the limb, distinct from phenotypes produced by defects in other signaling systems in axon guidance. Affected cranial and spinal neurons die shortly after axons stall, providing in vivo evidence that growing axons arrive at intermediate essential survival signals are relayed to cell bodies. The primary cause of motoron deficiency in F3/− mice is not defective neuron differentiation or excessive neuron death, but rather defective axon growth and guidance. The majority of F3/ motorons do not follow erroneous trajectories, and aberrant pathfinding is unlikely to be relevant. A wave of precocious motor death is observed in F3 embryos at E11.5-12.5, which is not seen in F controls, indicating a subset of neurons exhibits axon growth and apoptotic death. Controlling axonal growth may be the only function of F3 in neurons, as F3 and Cels brains have identical defects in multiple ax tracts and F3, Cels, and Vangl spinal cords have the same rostral turning defect in commissural axons of dorsal sensory neurons. Planar cell polarity signaling is implicated in motoron growth, highlighting the question of how PC proteins, which form cell-cell complexes in epithelia, function in the context of axonal growth.
Researchers investigated the role of transposable elements (TEs) as enhancers in mouse embryonic and trophoblast stem cells, as they are thought to have helped establish gene regulatory networks in both embryonic and extraembryonic lineages of the early embryo. Epigenomic and transcriptomic data suggested a large number of TEs contribute to tissue-specific gene expression, and genetic editing of individual TEs confirmed a subset of these regulatory relationships. However, CRISPR interference of RLTR13D elements in embryonic stem cells revealed only a minority play significant roles in gene regulation. The results indicate a subset of TEs are important for regulation in early mouse development and highlight the importance of functional experiments when evaluating gene roles of TEs. Some TEs play minor or no role in regulation despite strong correlative evidence at chromatin composition and conformation. CRISPR interference targeted RL13D elements showed TE enhancers may support lineage expression of a subset of genes, but correlations may emerge without causal links between TEs and expression. CRPR-mediated excision of selected TEs validated the effect of some TE enhancers on expression. Genetic editing of two RLTRD elements showed little effect on neighboring gene expression. The study used genetic and epigen editing approaches to test TE function in ESCs and TSCs and compared with epigen and transcript data, identifying TEs driving expression of lineage genes. Extensive work is necessary to evaluate the proportion of TE enhancers acting redundantly with regulatory elements, contrasting with epigen editing work showing LTRs play roles in human carcinoma cells.
C4 photosynthesis has evolved independently from the ancestral C3 pathway in at least 60 plant lineages, but the evolutionary path to this complex trait is unclear. A meta-analysis of 18 lineages containing species that use C3, C4, or intermediate C3-C4 forms of photosynthesis was conducted to parameterise a 16-dimensional phenotypic landscape, revealing diverse and flexible evolutionary paths that group into four major trajectories. A novel Bayesian approach based on a hidden Markov model was developed and experimentally verified to predict how the C4 phenotype evolved. The results show that the polyphyletic appearance of C4 photosynthesis is associated with alternative evolutionary histories underlying the appearance of C photosynthesis determined by ancestral and initial phenotypic alterations unrelated to photosynthesis. The order of C4 trait acquisition is flexible and driven by non-photosynthetic drivers, facilitating the convergent evolution of this complex trait.

The emergence of the entire C4 phenotype cannot be comprehensively explored experimentally, making C4 photosynthesis an ideal for mathematical modelling of complex trait evolution transitions on an underlying phenotype landscape. Distinct sub-types evolved as a consequence of alternative histories in response to non-photosynthetic pressures, with early events determining downstream phenotypes of C sub-types by restricting lineages to pathways. Mechanistic diversity underlies multiple pathways generating C4 photosynthesis, facilitating convergent evolution of complex traits. This may aid efforts to recapitulate C4 through genetic engineering of C3 species, expanding the molecular toolbox to establish C traits in distinct backgrounds.

Cellular alterations are acquired early in the evolution of C4 photosynthesis, consistent with observations from several genera. Including additional traits in the analysis did not alter the predicted order, suggesting conclusions are robust. Ancestral lineage and initial phenic alterations unrelated to photosynthesis determine the evolutionary paths to C4 photosynthesis. Non-photosynthetic pressures drive the convergent evolution of the C4 trait. The study provides insights into the evolution of one of the most complex traits to arise in multiple lineages, C4 photosynthesis, using a predictive Bayesian approach to infer evolutionary trajectories from sparsely sampled data. The results have implications for understanding the evolution of complex traits and potential applications in genetic engineering.
Researchers investigated the bacterial cytoskeletal protein SpoIVA, which hydrolyzes ATP to polymerize irreversibly during Bacillus subtilis sporulation, and evolved from a TRAF class P-loop GTPase. To understand the evolutionary pressure driving the change in nucleotide specificity, they reengineered the nucleotide-binding pocket of SpoIVA to mimic its ancestral GTP activity. SpoIVAGase functioned properly as a Gase but failed to polymerize due to not forming an NDP-bound intermediate required for polymerization. Incubation of SpoAGase with limiting ATP did not promote polymerization, revealing the nucleotide base, in addition to energy from hydrolysis, is critical for specific functions. Data suggests increased levels of ATP relative to GTP at the end of sporulation drove the change in nucleotide preference in SpoIVA. SpoAGase, functional in vivo, used ATP or GTP to induce a conformational change after hydrolysis but only polymerized in the presence of ATP, indicating a specific requirement for the nucleotide base. Physiological levels of intracellular ATP facilitated conformation changes in SpoIVA and SpoAGase. Both failed hydrolysis-mediated changes in 0.06mM GTP but occurred in 1.5mM ATP. Examination of Spo polymerization at different ATP concentrations showed neither Spo nor SpoAGase polymerized in 0mM ATP. SpoIVA is derived from an Era-like GTP, a pan-bacterial ribosomal maturation protein belonging the TRAC class P-loop GTP. SpoAGase promiscuously hydrolyzed ATP and GTP functioned similar to extant SpoIVA in vivo, leading to questions on why the protein evolved further. The study reveals the importance of nucleotide base in biological functions beyond energy release from hydrolysis.
Leptin plays a crucial role in regulating energy balance and exhibits neurotrophic effects during critical developmental periods, but its function during development is not fully understood. To investigate the importance of leptin in early life, researchers restored leptin signaling at the fourth or tenth week of age in mice lacking the leptin receptor gene and found some previously irreversible defects, including poor development of arcuate neural projections, were recovered in adulthood. However, early leptin deficiency led to irreversible obesity via suppressed energy expenditure. Reactivation of leptin in adulthood resulted in persistent reduction of hypothalamic Pomc, Cart, and Prlh mRNA expression and defects in reproductive and brain growth. Early leptin signaling defects cause permanent metabolic, neuroendocrine, and developmental problems. Changes in leptin during development affect neural circuits regulating homeostasis, favoring metabolic diseases later in life. Early postnatal leptin blockage leads to long-term leptin resistance and susceptibility to diet-induced obesity. Lept or Lep deficiency results in reduced brain mass, disruption of projections from ARH neurons to the paraventricular nucleus of the hypothalamus, behavioral and cognitive problems. Rodents exhibit a postnatal leptin surge between the first and second week not associated with fat or food intake. Findings indicate specific metabolic, neuroendocrine, developmental consequences arise from leptin absence in early life, contributing to understanding of metabolic programming in the context of childhood obesity.
Arabidopsis heterotrimeric G proteins, composed of the non-canonical G protein XLG2, the G protein AGB, and the G proteins AGG1 and AG2, regulate immunity by directly coupling to the FLS2 receptor. The FLS receptor perceives bacterial flagellin epitope flg22 to activate defenses through the cytoplasmic kinase BIK. In the pre-activation state, XLG2 interacts with FLS and BIK, and functions with AGB and AG1 to attenuate proteasome degradation of BIK, allowing optimum immune activation. Following activation by flg22, XL2 dissociates from AGB and is phosphorylated by BIK in the N terminus. The phosphorylated XL2 enhances the production of reactive oxygen species by modulating the NAD oxidase RboD. Introduction of a transgenic copy of BIK restored fl22-triggered immune responses and disease resistance in x2 and ag1 mutants, demonstrating that a major function of the heterotrimer G is to maintain signaling competence of FLS-BIK in preactivation. The proteins dynamically interact with FLS-BIK during signaling and findings are consistent with observed defects in fl22-induced responses and RboD phosphorylation in G mutants. AGB is required for accumulation of BIK but not FLS and BAK. XL2 directly interacts with FLS and BIK to regulate FLS-mediated immunity, supporting that plant kinases fulfill roles of GPC by coupling to heterotrimer G. The proteins coupled to FLS complex are subject to multiple regulations during FLS signaling. The heterotrimer proteins formed by XL2, AGB, AG1, and AG2 attenuate proteasome degradation of BIK and P20. Regulation of BIK stability contributes to the role of heterotrimer proteins in fl22-induced responses. Phosphorylation of XL2 in N terminus enhances production of reactive oxygen species likely modulating NAD oxidase Rbo. The study demonstrates G proteins directly couple to FLS receptor and regulate signaling through pre and post-activation mechanisms.
The Arabidopsis circadian clock orchestrates gene regulation across the day-night cycle, generating a 24-hour rhythm through multiple feedback loops, but the robustness and coordination of the clock at the individual cell level and across the plant remains unclear. Researchers examined clock activity at the single cell level in Arabidopsis seedlings over several days under constant conditions, revealing robust single-cell oscillations that are desynchronized. Two waves of clock activity were observed, one going down and one up the root, with evidence of cell-to-cell coupling, particularly in the root tip. A model showed that cell-to-cell coupling and measured period differences between cells can generate the observed waves. The results reveal the spatial structure of the plant and suggest the Arabidopsis clock has multiple coordination points, unlike the centralized mammalian clock. Single-cell data allowed estimation of coupling strength between cells and evidence of coupling, especially in the root tip. A model suggests observed period and cell-to-cell coupling can generate waves in gene expression, revealing the structure and robustness of the circadian system. However, results indicate the plant clock structure is more complicated, with a hierarchical model not explaining short oscillations in the root or wave of gene expression from the tip, supporting a decentralized model of clock coordination. Previous studies argued cellular oscillations are uncoupled, while others attempted to measure the clock at single-cell resolution but focused on specific parts for short periods. Using a reporter for the core gene CIRIAN CLOCK ASSOCIATED 1, researchers examined the Arabidopsis clock across the whole plant at single-cell level over days. Coherent waves of expression suggested the plant signal could be coupled, estimating coupling using the Kuramoto parameter and technique developed for mammalian circadian cells. Damping of rhythms at the plant level is mainly due to desynchronization of oscillating cells with different periods, not noise or low molecule numbers. Two waves of gene expression were observed, up and down the root, with variable periods at each position causing desynchronization.
Researchers investigated how the brain processes speech in noisy environments where multiple speakers' voices mix together, focusing on adults with normal hearing who experience difficulties understanding speech in such settings. Despite having normal hearing, approximately 10% of patients visiting clinics report communication difficulties. The study found that the ability to understand speech in multi-talker environments varies widely among adults with normal hearing and can be predicted by neural responses to frequency modulation cues measured through ear EEG recordings. Combining neural processing, listening effort, and frequency modulation cues accounted for 78% of variability in multi-talker speech understanding. The study applied psychophysical and neurophysiological tests to identify biomarkers for poor speech intelligibility in adults with normal hearing, aiming to develop new diagnostic measures for those struggling in noisy environments. Results showed considerable individual variability in measures among subjects with normal hearing, but found a correlation between pupil function and speech intelligibility. Individuals with steeper pupil recruitment had more difficulty in multi-talker tasks, indicating a link between pupil change and speech threshold. The study aimed to develop objective markers for poor communication complaints in individuals with normal hearing, suggesting a strong association between psychophysical tests for frequency detection and speech-noise-intelligibility due to early auditory processing encoding. The correlation between low-level encoding and speech intelligibility may reflect a link between temporal processing fidelity and speech. The findings could inform the design of next-generation clinical tests for hidden hearing disorders, providing a battery of behavioral and physiological tests accounting for nearly 80% of variability in multi-talker speech intelligibility without linguistic cues.
Researchers have developed a method to non-invasively image single-cell blood flow in the smallest to largest vessels in the living retina using 15 kHz adaptive optics. The transparency of the mammalian eye allows for a non-invasive view of the microvessels of the retina, a part of the central nervous system. However, imperfections in the optics of the eye blur microscopic retinal capillaries and single cells flowing within, limiting evaluation of microvascular diseases that originate in capillaries. Measured flow ranged four orders of magnitude across the full spectrum of retinal diameters without surgery or contrast. The ultrafast imaging pipeline and automated measurement of millions of blood speeds enabled high-throughput measurement of hundreds of thousands of cells per second. Single-cell velocity and flow in the smallest to largest vessels in the mouse retina was enabled by ALO imaging with ultrafast capture to noninively image cells without foreign agents. Pairing this novel imaging with an automated workflow enabled quantitative measurements in larger vessels containing hundreds of thousands of cells flowing per second. Development of mouse flow approaches will be applicable to clinical approaches using the same instrumentation. Imaging strategies and custom algorithms image single cells noninively in smallest to largest vessels of retinal circulation without exogenous agents facilitate measurement. Label-free adaptive optics achieved improved contrast of single cells in the retina compared to dye techniques, especially in high flow conditions. Noninvasive studying the retina serves as a window to understanding microcirculation dynamics in CNS and systemic microvascular health. Combined capabilities enabled single cell resolution as flowed in tight and sparse formations inside microvessels. All cells imaged noninively with infrared backscatter without dye, enabling submicrometer precision measuring lumen diameters of retinal vessels. In total, 123 vessels in 19 mice were studied with measured flow ranging 0.0001-1.55µL/min across 3.2-45.8µm vessel diameters.
Spontaneous symbiotic reprogramming of plant roots is triggered by receptor-like kinases, with Symbiosis Receptor Kinase (SYK) being indispensable for the development of phosphate-acquiring arbuscular mycorrhiza and nitrogen-fixing root nodule symbiosis. The mechanisms that discriminate between the two symbiotic developmental fates have been unclear, but research shows that ectopic expression of receptor kinase genes Nod Factor 1 and N5, along with SYK, initiate nodule organogenesis and nodulation-related gene expression in the absence of rhizobia. Overexpression of N1 or N5 associated with endogenous SYK in roots of legume Lotus japonicus reveals that the active SYK allele initiates signaling independently of N1 or N5 and upstream of a set of genes required for generation or decoding calcium-spiking in symbiosis. Only SYK, not N overexpression, triggers the expression of AM-related genes, indicating the receptors play a key role in the decision between AM or root nodule symbiosis development. The SYK loss of specificity may be explained by complex formation with N1 and N5, and related Lys-RLs that mediate recognition signals from the fungus, resulting in release of negative mechanisms or unbalanced stoichiometry of SYK and regulators. The common symbiosis genes of legumes are required for AM and nodule symbiosis, with a subset essential for generation or decoding of calcium-spiking. In L japonicus, the group encodes RL Symb Receptor Kinase SYK, and N5 Lys-RL has been pinpointed as a candidate for perception. The discovery that expression of three symbiotic RL constructs leads to spontaneous nodule formation in transgenic L japonicus indicates a critical step in signal initiation, with ligand-induced receptor dimerization being key in mammalian tyrosine kinases and plant RLs. A hallmark of nitrogen-fixing symbiosis is the accommodation of rhizobia in plant cells in specialized organs, providing a favorable environment for nitrogen fixation, with the common symbiosis pathway operating in most land plants.
Researchers have developed a new software toolkit called DeepKit for fast and robust animal pose estimation using deep learning, addressing limitations in speed and robustness of current methods. DeepKit utilizes an efficient multi-scale deep learning model, StackedNet, and a fast GPU-based peak-detection algorithm for estimating keypoint locations with subpixel precision, improving processing speed by over two times without loss of accuracy. The toolkit demonstrates versatility in multiple animal estimation tasks in laboratory and field settings, including groups of interacting individuals, reducing barriers to using advanced tools for measuring behavior. DeepKit builds on state-of-the-art individual estimation using convolutional neural networks to achieve fast inference without reducing accuracy or generalization, offering considerable improvements over existing models. The software, code, and documentation are available under a permissive open-source license. Achieving fast pose estimation typically relies on massively parallel processing on GPU with large batches or downsampling images, increasing error, but StackedNet can run at 30-110 Hz depending on resolution with a batch size of one. Deep learning-based methods can achieve near human accuracy by annotating data and training a convolutional neural network to estimate animal body parts from images. DeepKit's advances could lead to larger, higher-resolution, reliable datasets, revolutionizing understanding of relationships between behavior, brain, and environment. The toolkit automatically measures animal posture from images and has broad applicability across behavioral sciences, from neuroscience to ecology, providing quantitative measurements for answering scientific questions.
Disruption of glycolytic flux serves as a signal for inflammasome signaling and pyroptotic cell death in innate immune cells, such as macrophages, when challenged with environmental stresses or infections by pathogens. The assembly of multi-protein complexes called inflammasomes initiates pro-inflammatory responses and a form of cell death termed pyroptosis. The identification of an intracellular trigger of NLR3-mediated inflammatory signaling, IL-1 production, and pyroptosis in primed murine bone-derived macrophages is mediated by disruption of glycolytic flux, resulting in a drop of NAD levels and induction of mitochondrial ROS. This signal can be rescued by addition of products that restore NAD production. The disruption of glycolytic flux is a biologically relevant trigger of NLR3 inflamome activation, mediated by mitochondrial redox changes, revealing a mechanistic link between cellular metabolism and initiation of inflammation. Innate cells sense perturbed metabolite production and flux through the glycolytic pathway, activating NLR3 to initiate inflammatory responses. Inhibitors of flux-limiting enzymes and Salmonella typhimurium effectively limit glycolysis through distinct mechanisms, resulting in NLR3-mediated inflamome formation and pyptosis. NAD consumption increases upon pyruvate treatment, indicating induction of productive metabolism downstream glycolysis in infected BMD. Glycolytic perturbation is a mechanism by which innate cells sense altered homeostasis during S. typhurium infection, inducing pro-inflammatory response via NLR3 inflamome and pyotic cell death. Innate cells sense metabolic perturbation as danger, resulting in inflamome formation, caspase-1 activation, and cytokine secretion. Disrupting glycolytic flux triggers inflammation and death in macrophages. Supplementation of glycolytic pyruvate prevents inflamome formation and reduces caspase-1 activation, IL-1 secretion, and cell death. Pyvate supplementation does not impair NLR3 inflamome signaling by nonspecific mechanism.
Researchers have investigated the role of the PALB2 DNA binding domain in DNA repair, specifically its interaction with BRCA1 and BRCA2 proteins, which are tumor suppressors linked to various malignancies including breast cancer and Fanconi anemia. PAL2 coordinates BRCA1 and BRCA during homology-directed repair and interacts with several chromatin proteins. The study identified a major DNA-binding site of PAL2 and found that mutations in this site reduce RAD51 foci formation and overall efficiency in cells by 50%. The PAL2 N-terminal DNA-binding domain stimulates RAD51 recombinase function and possesses strand exchange activity without RAD51. Surprisingly, it also stimulates inverse strand exchange and can use DNA and RNA substrates. Data reveal a versatile DNA interaction of PAL2 and demonstrate a critical role of PAL2 DNA binding for chromosome repair in cells. PAL2 is described as a scaffold linking BRCA with BR2 during homology-directed repair and interacting with other chromatin proteins. However, a truncation variant with preserved BRCA and BR2 binding motifs but without the middle portion containing DBDs does not support BR2 and RAD recruitment to DSBs. Mutations of four amino acids significantly reduce RAD51 foci and efficiency in a model system. PAL2 NDB stimulates RAD strand reactions and supports forward and inverse exchange even without RAD51 and uses RNA as a substrate. PAL2 interaction with DNA is critical for recruitment of BR2 and RAD to DSB and efficient repair in cells. PAL2 NDB stimulates RAD-mediated strand exchange in vitro. Breast cancer proteins BR1 and 2 regulate efficient nonmutic pathway of chromosome repair and initiate RAD-mediated homologous recombination and facilitate stalled replication. PAL2 truncation of 500 amino acids between BR1 and BR2 motifs containing DB does not support BR2 RAD foci in cells during DNA damage. Results point to the importance of DB in PAL2 function. PAL2 complemented cells display a defect in HR due to defective DNA binding. PAL2 interaction with DNA is critical for BR2 and RAD recruitment to DSB and efficient repair in cells. PAL2 NDB stimulates RAD-mediated strand exchange in vitro highlighting the importance of PAL2 DNA binding in chromosome maintenance.
Researchers have discovered that the prokaryotic actin homologue MreB forms pairs of protofilaments in an antiparallel arrangement, both in vitro and in vivo, which is unprecedented among actin-like proteins. The atomic structure of antiparallel protoaments of Caulobacter MreB reveals that a protofilament doublet is essential for Mre's function in cell shape maintenance. In vivo site-specific cross-linking demonstrates the antiparallel orientation of Mre protoaments in E. coli and 3D cryo-EM shows pairs of protoaments of Caulobacter Mre bind tightly to membranes. Crystal structures of different nucleotide and polymerisation states of Caulobacter Mre reveal conserved conformation changes accompanying antiparallel filament formation. Antimicrobial agents A22/265 bind to the bound nucleotide of Mre, preventing nucleotide hydrolysis and destabilising double filaments. Mre from Caulobacter crescent forms pairs of antiparallel protoaments as revealed by crystal structures. The straight antiparallel arrangement of Mre protoaments ensures each subunit interacts with membrane-bound components. Non-polar nature of Mre filaments explains common assembly and disassembly for filament ends. The mechanism of protofilament formation of C. crescent Mre is elucidated by crystallography, revealing typical domain closure upon polymerisation. Co-crystallisation of Caulob Mre with antimicrobial reagents explains inhibitory mechanism and sheds light on activation of ATP hydrolysis during polymerisation. The antiparallel arrangement of protoaments of C.crescent Mre ensures amphipathic helix of both filaments bind to membrane, interacting with bitopic proteins. Deleting the N-terminal amphipathic helix makes protein less prone to aggregation. The crystal structure of Caul Mre reveals the actin fold containing two domains on either side of a nucleotide binding cleft. Both domains are separated into subdomains A and B. The antiparallel arrangement is unprecedented among actin family proteins, describing a novel architecture of actin-like protoament pairs of eukaryotic or prokotic origin.
The role of APETALA1 in petal number robustness is crucial for reproductive success and resistance to natural perturbations. In Arabidopsis thaliana, petal number is invariant, whereas in the closely related Cardamine hirs, petal number varies, and the genetic basis for this difference between species is unknown. Research shows that divergence in the pleiotropic floral regulator APET1 accounts for the species-specific difference in petal number robustness. The effect of AP1 is explained by epistatic interactions, where A thalian AP1 confers robustness by masking the phenotypic expression of quantitative trait loci controlling petal number in C hirs. However, C hirs AP1 fails to complement this function of A thalian AP, resulting in variable petal number. Upstream regulatory regions of AP1 contribute to this divergence, and variable petal number is maintained in C hirs despite sufficient genetic variation in accessions to produce four-petaled flowers. AP1 is an important regulator of A thalian flower development, acting early to initiate flowers and later to specify floral organs. The study identified AP1 as a gene of major effect in the evolutionary transition from robust phenotype of four petals in A thalian to variable petal number in C hirs. AP polymorphisms do not directly contribute to within-species variation in C hirs petal number, instead, the canalization of petal in A thalian reflects robustness to genetic variation. Complementation of ap mutants with the AP genomic locus from C hirs resulted in variable petal number, suggesting hidden variability in the gene network controlling petal number in A thalian. Floral organs with petal identity show no variation if they arise outside the second whorl, and ectopic expression of AP1 is associated with an invariant number of petals, whereas endogenous AP expression in whor two of C hirs floral meristem is associated with variable petal number. The results suggest AP divergence likely exposed cryptic variation in C hirs contributing to maintaining variable petal number within accessions of C hirs.
Proinsulin misfolding is an early event in the progression to type 2 diabetes. The biosynthesis of insulin begins with the folding of the proinsulin precursor, including the formation of three evolutionarily conserved intramolecular disulfide bonds. However, normal pancreatic islets contain a subset of proinsulin molecules with at least one free cysteine thiol. In human or rodent islets with perturbed endoplasmic retic folding environment, non-native proins enters intermolecular disulfide-linked complexes. In genetically obese mice with wild-type islets, disulfide-linked complexes of proins are more abundant, and in leptin-deficient mice, the increase of such complexes tracks the onset of islet deficiency and diabetes. Proins-Cys B and Cys A are necessary and sufficient for the formation of proins disulfide-linked complexes. Proins Cys B-Cys B coent homodimers resist reductive dissociation, highlighting a structural basis for aberrant proins complex formation. Increased proins misfolding via disulfide complexes is an early event associated with prediabetes that worsens ß-cell dysfunction in type 2 diabetes.

Proins misfolding includes the inability to complete internal disulf bonds with a subfraction proins molecules in the ER bearing unpaired cysteine residues. This population of improperly folded proins enters aberrant disulfide partnerships with proins in the ER, resulting misfolded proins complexes identified in human islets. Islets from hyperphagic, glucose-intolerant LepRk2.1 mice demonstrated increased disulf-linked proins complexes with 90 of proins entangled. Rodent islets and human islets in states that alter ER environment and T2 progression prior to ß-cell dedifferentiation or death show similar results. Proins in islet lysates of prediabetic Lepdb mice contain steady level proins with 90 molecules entangled disulfide dimers and higher-order complexes. Young normoglycemic homozyous Lepdb male islets destined for diabetes show increased proinsulfide complexes and insulin levels compared to heterozygous Lep males that do not progress to diabetes. The formation of disulfide-linked proins complexes is necessary and sufficient for proins misfolding, indicating an early event in type 2 diabetes progression.
SUV39H1 is a histone H3 lysine 9-specific methyltransferase that targets pericentric regions and is crucial for assembling heterochatin. The protein recognizes trimethylated H3K9 via its chromodomain and H3Kme3 allows SUVH to target chromosomal regions. Research has shown that Suvh1's chromodomain binds nucleic acids, which is important for its function in heterochatin assembly, with higher affinity for RNA than DNA and independent of H3Kme recognition. Suv1 binds major satellite RNAs in vivo and knockdown of major RNAs lowers Suv retention on perentere. Mutational studies indicate both nucleic acid and H3me binding activities of Suv are crucial for perentric heterochatin assembly. Results suggest chromatin-bound RNAs contribute to SUV target specificity. Suv1's nucleic acid activity integrates with recognition of methylated H3 to establish perentric heterochatin, including Hme formation, HP accumulation, and transcription repressive status. Suv binds major RNA in a manner dependent on nucleic-binding and knockdown of RNAs lowers S retention on perentric. Data indicate chrom-bound RNAs contribute to targeting and retention of S at chromosomal regions. In addition to Hme, Suv's RNA activity contributes to stable association with Hme-rich perent regions. Knockdown of major repeat transcripts supports notion that RNA binding contributes stability on chromatin. Suv-YA interacts with major RNAs but loosely binds chromatin, suggesting interaction with cellular RNAs without stable binding. The HP-binding and chromatin activities of Suv's N region contribute to perentric H formation. Collectively, results demonstrate four activities of Suv regulate perentric heterochatin assembly, including nucleic acid, Hme, HP, and chromatin binding, in addition to lysine methyltransferase activity.
Researchers investigated the role of entorhinal cortex grid cells in spatial navigation and mental operations, hypothesizing that their activity may not depend on physical movement. To test this, they recorded from single units in the entorhinal cortex of monkeys trained to perform a covert attention task, maintaining central fixation while attending to a moving stimulus in the periphery. A significant population of entorinal cells, 20/141 or 14% at FDR <5%, showed spatially structured fields with triangular tiling of space, contrasting with hippocampus recordings where grid-like representations were not observed. This suggests neurons in macaque entorhinal cortex do not rely on physical movement for activation. Monkeys performed the task with >70% accuracy, completing between two and eight cycles per session, with errors mainly due to saccades outside the fixation window. The standard gridness measure was used to evaluate spatial representations among enthinal neurons. Results provide strong evidence that activation of cells in enthinal cortex with spatially firing does not require physical environment movement, supporting the hypothesis that grid cells participate in range of cognitive functions and can be activated by movements of attention. Previous studies showed stationary monkeys' grid cells can be activated by eye movements while visually exploring scenes. The neural substrate for overt and covert attention is largely overlapping, indicating grid cells can represent not only gaze but also locus of attention. This study demonstrates enthinal cells are modulated by spatial attention even without movement, contributing to understanding their role in navigation and mental operations.
Researchers have developed a standardized task to probe decision-making in mice, which produces reproducible results across multiple laboratories, addressing concerns of reproducibility in neuroscience, particularly for measurements of mouse behavior. A head-fixed task was adopted to assay perceptual and value-based decision-making, with standardized training, experimental hardware, software, and procedures. One hundred forty mice were trained across seven laboratories in three countries, generating a publicly available database of five million mouse choices. Learning speed varied, but once training was complete, there were no differences in behavior across laboratories. Mice adopted similar reliance on visual stimuli, past successes, failures, and estimates of stimulus probability to guide choices, revealing complex behavior can be reproduced across multiple laboratories. This establishes a standard for reproducible rodent behavior and provides an open-access dataset to study decision-making in mice, indicating a path toward achieving reproducibility through collaborative open-science approaches. 

The experimental equipment and protocols were identical, and mice learned the task in all laboratories, performing comparably after learning. To facilitate reuse and reproducibility, an open approach was adopted, describing and sharing hardware, software, and protocols, as data and techniques should be available to the broader community. Results from a large cohort of trained mice demonstrate reproducible methods and results, with most learning the task, although at different paces, and performing comparably after, with no significant differences. Physiological studies of decision-making in mice utilize genetic, imaging and physiological tools, and this collaboration aims to explore neural basis of behavior in multiple laboratories. This enables comparison of results, knowing differences are unlikely due to behavior, and hopes to catalyze new adaptations, accelerating high-quality studies of neural correlates of decision-making.
The proper function and repair of the digestive system are vital to most animals, and deciphering the mechanisms involved in these processes requires an atlas of gene and cell types. Researchers applied laser-capture microdissection and RNA sequencing to characterize the intestinal transcriptome of Schmidtea mediterranea, a planarian flatworm that can regenerate organs including the gut, identifying hundreds of genes with intestinal expression undetected by previous approaches. Systematic analyses revealed extensive conservation of digestive physiology and cell types with other animals, including humans. Spatial laser-capture microdissection enabled the discovery of previously unappreciated regionalization of gene expression in the planarian intestine along the medio-lateral axis, especially among intestinal goblet cells. Two intestine-enriched transcription factors were identified that regulate regeneration, specifically hedge signaling effector gli-1, or maintenance, Rb2 of goblet cells. The planarian intestinal transcriptome is a foundational resource for investigating numerous aspects of intestine regeneration and physiology. Twenty-two intestine-enrich transcription factors were identified, including those required for production and maintenance of goblet cells, setting the stage for further studies of this cell type. Intestine-enrich transcription factors play distinct roles in gob cell differentiation and maintenance, and depletion of goblet cells reduces planarians willingness to feed and viability with negligible effects on regeneration of other cell types. Characterization of the planarian transcriptome provides a framework for several steps, defining gene expression and cell types in the intestine. The intestine expresses genes involved in metabolism, nutrient storage, transport, innate immunity, and physiological roles demonstrating functional homology with digestive systems of other animals, including humans. Transcription factors gli-1 and Rb RNA phenotypes suggest distinct mechanisms govern differentiation and maintenance of goblet cells. Observations reinforce evolutionary conservation of intestinal expression indicating roles performed by multiple human organs consolidated in the planarian intestine. Definition of the intestinal transcriptome enables identification of genes for regeneration and functions of distinct cell types.
Researchers investigated how evolutionary divergence in sensory perception relates to physiological coding of stimuli in mormyrid fish, which detect subtle variations in electric signals and encode them into spike-timing differences between sensory receptors. In contrast, receptors insensitive to waveform variation produce spontaneously oscillating potentials. The study found that oscillating receptors respond to electric pulses by resetting their phase, resulting in transient synchrony among receptors that encodes signal timing and location, but not waveform. These receptors were most sensitive to frequencies found in collective signals of conspecifics and correlated with increased behavioral responses to these frequencies. Different perceptual capabilities correspond to different receptor physiologies, suggesting divergent mechanisms represent adaptations for different social environments. A novel mechanism for peripheral coding was described where sensory receptors with spontaneous oscillatory activity respond to stimuli with phase resets that result in synchrony across the population, providing information about pulse timing and polarity but not precise cues within the waveform. Mormy fish communicate through species-specific electric discharge at variable interpulse intervals and oscillating receptors are tuned to lower frequencies, conferring sensitivity to interpulse intervals produced by groups. Playback experiments demonstrated species with oscillating receptors responded strongly to sub-millisecond interpulse intervals within the range of enhanced sensitivity. Behavioral experiments tested the hypothesis that species with oscillating receptors exhibit selective responses to sub-millisecond interpulse intervals and species with spiking receptors do not, presenting a single fish with a train of conspecific electric discharges at constant interpulse intervals. The findings provide evidence for sensory coding through oscillatory synchrony, suggesting an adaptation for detecting communication from large groups of fish.
Bacteriophage BPP1 infects and kills Bordetella species that cause whooping cough and has a diversity-generating retroelement providing a naturally occurring phage display system. However, engineering efforts have been hampered without atomic structures. The cryo-electron microscopy structure of BPP1 head at 3.5 Å resolution reveals an atomic model showing two of three protein folds representing viral lineages: jellyroll for cement protein and HK97-like for major capsid protein. The topology of the major capsid protein is permuted non-circularly from the Johnson topology previously seen in viral and cellular proteins. This new topology is likely the only feasible alternative to the old topology with β-sheet and electrostatic interactions contributing to non-covalent chain formation in BPP1, unlike covalent interprotein linkages in HK97. The termini of both proteins are positioned for DGR-based phage engineering. 

The 3D structure of the BPP head at 3.5 Å resolution was determined by single-particle cryoEM and derived an atomic model. The jellyroll motif in BPP1 forms an auxiliary protein to stabilize the viral capsid made of another fold, unlike its role as major capsid in other viruses. BPP1 adopts two characteristic folds of major lineages, jellyroll and HK97. The Johnson fold in BPP is non-circular permuted compared to the known topology. Until now, the Johnson fold has been observed in Siphiridae and Myoviridae families of tailed-bacteriophages, exemplified by gp5 of HK97. The BPP topology and HK topology represent two feasible topologies of the Johnson fold. All known structures of proteins with Johnson adopt the HK topology, including gp of HK97 in Siphidae. 

Bacteriophage B1 is a short-tailed, dsDNA virus and member of Podiridae, infecting Bordella causing whooping cough and respiratory diseases. It has a T=7 icosahedral capsid and 670 Å diameter. A 7 resolution cryEM structure showed the capsid has an HK-like fold with an additional component. An atomic description of B1 will enhance bioengineering, reveal noncovalent interactions, stable capsid formation, and evolutionary relationships of B1 proteins with other viruses. The termini of both proteins are positioned for DGR-based phage engineering, despite complex interactions. Noncoval molecular interactions contribute stable capsid formation, unlike coval interprotein linkages in HK97. The structure provides insight into evolutionary relationships of B1 and proteins in other viruses.
Dengue virus (DENV) cycles between mosquito and mammalian hosts, and to examine how DENV populations adapt to these different environments, researchers used serial passage in human and mosquito cell lines and estimated fitness effects for all single-nucleotide variants in these populations using ultra-deep sequencing. This allowed determination of the contributions of beneficial and deleterious mutations to the collective fitness of the population. The analysis revealed the continuous influx of a large burden of deleterious mutations counterbalances the effect of rare, host-specific beneficial mutations to shape the path of adaptation. Beneficial mutations preferentially map to intrinsically disordered domains in the viral proteome and cluster to defined regions in the genome, these phenotypically redundant alleles facilitate host-specific DENV adaptation. The evolutionary constraints described in the system mirror trends observed across DENV and Zika strains, indicating it recapitulates key biophysical and biological constraints shaping long-term viral evolution. The findings quantify the cost of deleious mutation across the deng genome, highlighting negative selection shapes diversity in viral populations. The ability to recapitulate and identify shifts in the DMFE uncovers molecular mechanisms of selection and adaptation operating on DENV populations in cells and host. Clusters of adaptive mutations in specific regions of deng genome suggest discrete elements targeted by selection in each host. The fitness of the population represents a balance between the dynamics of rare beneficial mutations and significant and constantly replenished load of deleterious alleles during adaptation. Adaptation relies on host-specific mutations clustered in regions of DENV and enriched in regions of proteome exhibiting structural flexibility. CirSeq characterizes fitness landscapes of DENV adapting distinct environments and cellular machineries of human and mosquito cells by tracing allele trajectories almost all possible single nucleotide variants. The influence of positive and negative selection shapes evolutionary paths of DENV in distinct environments is revealed. Beneficial mutations cluster at specific locations in genome, suggesting hotspots of host selection, mapped on three-dimensional structure of deng protein, a transmembrane protein forming outer layer of viral envelope.
Researchers utilized single-particle tracking with photoactivated localization microscopy and detailed analysis to investigate the interaction between membrane nanodomains and KRas12, an active mutant of Ras, in U2 cells. The results showed that distinct membrane domains dictate KRas12 diffusion and trafficking, with KRas12 exhibiting an immobile state in ~70 nm domains, embedded in a larger domain ~200 nm that confers intermediate mobility, while the rest of the membrane supports fast diffusion. KRas12 is continuously removed from the membrane via the immobile state and replenished to the fast state, similar to internalization and recycling. The diffusion and trafficking properties of KRas12 remain invariant over a broad range of protein expression levels. The partitioning of KRas12 into three states, immobile, intermediate, and fast, remains invariant over a wide range of KRas12 expression levels, demonstrating that KRas12 diffusion and trafficking through the mobility states and nanodomains is maintained in a homeostatic condition. The measured sizes of immobilization and intermediate domains of KRas12 were ~70 nm and ~200 nm, respectively, consistent with previous findings that nanoscopic domains regulate Ras organization on the membrane. A new model is proposed where nested membrane nanodomains dictate the diffusion and trafficking of KRas12 with implications in multimer formation and signaling. Membrane nanodomains directly influence the mobility, trafficking, and potentially multimer formation and signaling of KRas12, although details of trafficking and multimer processes are yet to be defined. The study sheds light on how nanodomains organize the membrane to dictate Ras diffusion and trafficking, offering guidance for future experiments to determine molecular and structural identities of Ras-associated nanodomains and mechanisms of multimer formation and signaling.
Researchers investigated the mechanism of Bone Morphogenetic Protein (BMP) gradient formation in zebrafish embryos, which patterns the dorsoventral axis in vertebrates and invertebrates. The prevailing view in vertebrates for BMP gradient formation involves a counter-gradient of BMP antagonists and ligand shuttling to generate peak signaling levels. To determine the mechanism in zebrafish, they quantified BMP activity in wild-type and mutant embryos, combining data with a mathematical model-based computational screen to test hypotheses. Analysis ruled out BMP shuttling and transcriptionally-formed gradient mechanisms, instead supporting a source-sink mechanism where restricted BMP antagonist distribution acts as a sink driving BMP flux dorsally and gradient formation. Measuring Bmp2 diffusion supported the source-sink model, suggesting a new mechanism to shape BMP gradients during development. 

The study established a quantitative imaging method to measure the BMP gradient, integrating results with a mathematical approach to inform model selection and identify key parameters. Recent observations of bmp2 and bmp7 expression led to the hypothesis that BMP signaling reflects expression, but three results did not support this. Quantification and modeling presented a new view of BMP antagonists and ligand establishing the signaling gradient patterning the dorsoventral axis in zebish. BMPs act as morphogens during development, including patterning the embryonic axis, neural tube, and Drosophila disc, established by a network of extracellular regulators. In vertebrates, the mechanism by which BMP ligands and antagonists shape the gradient is unclear, with proposed mechanisms including an inverse gradient of antagonists and shutling ligand to regions. 

The source-sink mechanism relies on restricted antagonist distribution acting as a sink, driving BMP flux dorsally and formation, differing from prevailing views of counter-gradients and shutling. BMPs play a crucial role in development, including patterning the dorsovent axis, neural tube, and Drosila disc, with BMP systems established by extracellular regulators. The study provides a new understanding of BMP gradient formation in zebrafish, with implications for understanding development and patterning in vertebrates and invertebrates.
Lysosomes are crucial sites for intracellular degradation and proteolysis, and the export of low-molecular-weight catabolic end-products is facilitated by polytopic transmembrane proteins. Researchers investigated MFSD, a previously uncharacterized lysosomal member of the major facilitator superfamily, and found it is not N-glycosylated and contains a dileucine-based sorting motif for transport to lysosomes. Mfsd knockout mice developed splenomegaly and severe liver disease, and proteomics of isolated lysosomes revealed GLMP as a critical accessory subunit for MFSD. MF1 and GL physically interact, and GL is essential for maintaining normal levels of MF1 in lysosomes, and vice versa. Gl knockout mice mimic the phenotype of Mf1 knockout, indicating a tightly linked MF1/GL lysosomal membrane transporter complex. Previous studies identified putative lysosomal transporters, and this study characterized MF1 in detail, establishing an essential role for GL and MF1 in liver homeostasis, particularly in liver sinusoidal endothelial cells. MF1 is endogenously localized in lysosomes under steady state, ubiquitously expressed in murine tissues, and its transport to lysosomes is mediated by a dileine-based motif in the N-terminus. Mf1 knockout mice were generated to determine physiological functions, and by differential proteomics, GL was identified as essential for MF1, a highly glycosylated lysosomal protein of unknown function. Deficiency of Mf1 leads to reduced GL and vice versa. MF1 belongs to the major facilitator superfamily of transporters, one of the largest family mediating secondary or passive processes, and Chapel et al aimed to identify lysosomal transporters, with MF1 as a top candidate. Lysosomes from wildtype and Mf1 knockout mice were isolated, and proteomics revealed drastically reduced levels of GL. The data indicate MF1 is not a transporter for tested amino acids under conditions, and its deficiency affects lysosomal composition. The study reveals a critical role for MF1 and GL in liver homeostasis, particularly in liver sinusoidal endothelial cells, and establishes a tightly linked MF1/GL complex essential for liver function.
Researchers have gained insight into the process of microtubule-based transport by kines motors, powered by ATP hydrolysis, which is essential for various vital processes in eukaryotes. Atomic models of the monomeric kines motor domain on microtules were developed from cryo-EM reconstructions at 5-6 Å resolution, providing high-resolution structures of kines on microtubules. By comparing these models with existing X-ray structures of ADP-bound kines, a mechanistic scheme was inferred where microtub attachment triggers a clamshell opening of the nucleotide cleft and release of ADP, mediated by a universally conserved 'linpin' residue in kines. Binding of ATP re-closes the cleft, tightly coupling translocation of cargo via kines' 'neck' element. These structural transitions are similar to nucleotide-exchange steps in myosin and F1-ATPase motors, informing how two heads of kines dimer 'gate' each other to promote coordinated stepping along microtubules. The mechanism allows microtules to regulate kines affinities for ADP and ATP by modulating conformation of switch loops, yielding an elegant basis for kines nucleotide behavior on microtubules. High-resolution cryoEM reconstructions of human monomeric kines motor domain attached to microtub in different chemical states were solved, representing the motor before and after ATP binding. No-nucle and ATP-bound models of kines-microtub complex were compared to X-ray structures of AD-bound kines, revealing clam-like closure of kines nucleotide cleft triggered by ATP on microtules. CryoEM analysis of AD and ATP-bound states of N255 mutant supported the hypothesis, revealing previously unidentified binding modes of kines where motor attaches to microt tightly but is rotationally mobile. The findings provide a structural basis for kines nucleotide-gated force generation and inform how microtules regulate kines affinities, shedding light on the mechanism of kines microtub-attached cycle.
Maintaining the essential functions of mitochondria requires mechanisms to recognize and remove misfolded proteins, however, quality control pathways for misfolded mitochondrial proteins remain poorly defined. A novel quality control pathway at the mitochondrial outer membrane has been established using temperature-sensitive peripheral mitochondrial outer membrane proteins as model substrates in Saccharomyces cerevisiae. The ts-proteins sen1-Hs and sam35-Hs are degraded from the mitochondrial outer membrane by the ubiquitin-proteasome system, with ubiquination of sen1-Hs mediated by ubiquitin ligase Ubr1 and sam35-Hs primarily by San1. Mitochondria-associated degradation of both substrates requires the SSA family Hsp70 and Hsp40 Sis, providing evidence for chaperone involvement. The Cdc-Npl-Ufd1 AAA-ATP complex, Doa, and a mitochondrial pool of transmembrane Cdc adaptor Ubx are also implicated in their degradation. This study reveals a unique quality control pathway comprised of a combination of cytosolic and mitochondrial factors that distinguishes it from other cellular pathways. Ubiquination and proteasomal degradation play roles in the turnover of sam-Hs and sen-Hs at the mitochondrial outer membrane. The known ubiquitin ligases acting on mitochondrial proteins in yeast are SCdm and Rsp, however, the known substrates for these are native proteins. Temperature-dependent misfolding and degradation have been used to study protein quality control in the ER, cytos, and nucleus. The ts-alleles sam and sen have been used to elucidate how misfolded mitochondrial proteins are targeted for destruction. Pathways converge post-ubiquination where the Cdcpl-Ufd complex facilitates unfolding and extraction of substrates prior to targeting 26S proteomes for degradation. Mitrial proteins are subject to oxidative insults resulting in damage and misfolding. Ubiquinated forms of ts-proteins accumulate in the mitochondrial fraction under proteome impairment, correlating with unmodified species distributions. Deletion of proteome shuttling factors does not affect degradation, distinguishing from ERAD, indicating ubiquinated mitochondrial substrates require the Cdcplfd complex for efficient proteasomal degradation. The Cdcplfd complex is recruited via Cdc interaction with co-factors, establishing a unique quality control pathway at the mitochondrial outer membrane.
Extracellular signal-regulated kinase (ERK) is a key effector of many signaling pathways, and its regulation is crucial for homeostasis in the epidermis and other tissues. Researchers used a FRET biosensor to visualize epidermal ERK activity in living mice, revealing bursts of ERK activation patterns where ER activity radially propagated from cell to cell under steady-state conditions. The frequency of this spatial propagation, termed Spatial Propagation of Radial ER Activity Distribution (SPREAD), correlated with the rate of epidermal cell division. READs and proliferation were stimulated by 12-O-tetradecanoylphorbol 13-acetate (TPA) in a manner dependent on EGF receptors and their cognate ligands. At wounded skin, ERK activation propagated as a wave parallel to the wound edge, suggesting ER activation can be superimposed. Visualizing the cell cycle showed that READs were associated with G2/M cell progression. Long-time imaging of intact epidermis revealed radial propagation of ER activity, originating from a few cells and propagating radially up to 50 μm over 30 minutes until disappearance. READs were observed in the epidermis of back skin, indicating they are generally present in mouse epidermis. The ERK pattern mainly propagated in the basal layer of epidermis and to a lesser extent in the suprabasal layer. ER activation is triggered by the activation of epidermal growth factor receptors through binding to cognate ligands. Exquisite control of ERK MAP signaling is required for homeostasis in the epider and tissues. The study provides new insights into how proliferation and transient ER activity are synchronized in a living tissue, showing two types of novel ER activation patterns, and how growth signal is regulated in steady-state and wounded skin.
Circadian rhythms are biological oscillations that schedule daily changes in physiology, but outside the laboratory, circadian clocks do not generally free-run and are driven by daily cues whose timing varies with the seasons. Researchers have developed experimental platforms for driving the cyanobacterial circadian clock both in vivo and in vitro and found that the phase of the circadian rhythm follows a simple scaling law in light-dark cycles, tracking midday across variable day lengths. The core oscillator comprised of Kai proteins behaves similarly when driven by metabolic pulses in vitro, indicating such dynamics are intrinsic to these proteins. A mathematical framework was developed based on instantaneous transformation of the clock by external cues, which predicts clock behavior under many environments. The entrained behavior of the circadian clock can be captured quantitatively by a mathematical framework with a small number of parameters, guiding the design of experiments to probe molecular origins of key features. The cyanobacterial clock can be reconstituted in vitro from purified Kai proteins and presents a unique opportunity to elucidate molecular mechanisms in circadian biology. Biochemical simulations of seasonal effects in the reconstuted Kai oscillator by driving proteins with nucleotides on a 24-hour cycle showed similar scaling of clock phase with simulated day length, indicating core mechanisms for seasonal adaptation are intrinsic to Kai proteins. The linear model holds promise of predicting behavior of circadian rhythms in varying environments from minimal data characterizing oscillator response and may be applicable to clocks in many organisms. The key determinant of entrained behavior is how the clock cycle is deformed by coupling to the environment. The cyanobacterial circadian clock follows midday both in vivo and in vitro, and its core oscillator comprised of Kai proteins drives genome-wide transcriptional rhythms, which have been extensively studied biochemically in constant conditions. The ability to track midday in vivo is intrinsic to the core oscillator and does not require additional factors present in the cell. The model is general and expected to be useful for other organisms, providing insights into molecular mechanisms of circadian biology and seasonal adaptation.
Cardiovascular incidents and sudden cardiac death are leading causes of premature death, yet the origins remain unidentified in many cases. Genome-wide studies have identified Meis1 as a risk factor for sudden cardiac death. Research has shown that Meis inactivation in the neural crest leads to altered sympathovagal regulation of cardiac rhythmicity in adults, characterized by chronotropic incompetence and conduction defects, increasing susceptibility to sudden cardiac death. Meis1 is a major regulator of sympathetic target-field innervation, and Meis deficient neurons die by apoptosis from embryonic to perinatal stages. Meis regulates transcription of key molecules necessary for endosomal machinery, and traffic of Rab+ endosomes is severely altered in Meis-inactivated neurons. This suggests Meis interacts with trophic signaling pathways during postmitotic differentiation. Neurodevelopmental defects lead to sympathetic apoptosis, positioning Meis as a novel actor in sympathetic differentiation by its role in distal-field innervation. Genes regulated by Meis include transcriptional regulation of proteins for retrograde transport, endosomes, and traffic. Genetic inactivation of Meis results in imbalanced sympathovagal regulation of heart functions, conduction defects, and premature sudden death. Meis acts independently of well-studied transcription factors governing early neuron specification to orchestrate later phases of differentiation. Loss of Me1 function in sympathetic neurons leads to imbalanced sympathovagal regulation in adult mice, increasing susceptibility to sudden death. Inactivation of Me1 in peripheral neurons impairs traffic of Rab+endosomes and compromises target innervation. This study complements research demonstrating Meis function in neonatal cardiomyocyte mitosis following myocardial infarction. However, Meis inactivation or overexpression in cardiomyocytes does not affect cardiac functions. The Meis family participates in cardiogenesis, indicating Meis role in cardiac regulation through sympathetic neuron differentiation and susceptibility to unexplained sudden death.
Researchers have investigated the morphogenesis of surface-attached bacterial communities, known as biofilms, using Vibrio cholerae as a model system, combining mechanical measurements, theory, simulation, quantitative image analysis, surface characterization, and mutagenesis. The study reveals that mechanical instabilities, including wrinkling and delamination, underlie the morphogenesis of growing biofilms. Interfacial energy is identified as a key driving force for mechanomorphogenesis, dictating the generation and annihilation of new and existing interfaces. A feedback between mechanomorphogenesis and biofilm expansion shapes the overall biofilm contour. The findings suggest that exploiting mechanical principles to drive key morphogenic events is an ancient process that occurs in bacteria and has been reused in eukaryotes. Biofilms represent a model to investigate the role of mechanical forces in morphogenesis and provide a system for morpho-engineering. The study proposes a mechanomorph model where biofilms wrinkle and delaminate as growth builds up compressive stress, and changing the spatial distribution of mechanical stress can alter biofilm topography. Experiments where V. cholera biofilms were inoculated onto the same agar plate and allowed to collide support this model. The Δz P aerosa mutant forms peripheral wrinkles and delaminations due to the same mechanical instability described in V. cholera, illustrating how gene regulation and cell physiology can be coupled to mechanical instability to promote biofilm morphologies. Theoretical work on bacterial biofilms has considered mechanical instabilities, and findings suggest genes or compounds that alter biofilm morphology by mechanics could be discovered to address biofilm problems. Morphological patterns involve gene programs, but mechanical findings in V cholera are expected to apply generally to morphogenesis in tissues in higher organisms.
The mechanisms underlying the development of epileptic networks following severe seizures are not well understood, but research has shown that interfering with the function of the neuron-restrictive silencer factor (NRS/REST), a transcription factor that influences neuronal phenotype, can attenuate the disorder. This study found that epilepsy-provoking seizures increased NRS levels in the hippocampus several fold, yet only a subset of approximately 10% of potential NRS target genes were repressed. The repressed genes were rescued when NRS binding to chromatin was blocked. The genes selectively repressed by NRS had mid-range binding frequencies to the repressor, rendering them sensitive to moderate fluctuations in NF levels. These genes, regulated by NRS during epileptogenesis, coded for ion channels, receptors, and other contributors to neuronal function. Dynamic regulation of NRS target genes may play a role in influencing neuronal properties in pathological and physiological contexts. Hundreds of NRSE-containing hippocampal genes are potential targets, but only a subset are repressed by NRS in the context of seizure-induced epilepsy, evident from their rescue when NRS function is blocked. The basis of selective regulation includes mid-range binding frequencies to NRS, influenced by moderate fluctuations in NRS levels. NRS-regulated hippocampal genes include ion channels, receptors, calcium molecules, and transcription factors governing neuronal function and plasticity, suggesting their repression contributes to epilepsy pathogenesis. Key findings are that NRS contributes to epileptogenesis by regulating genes influencing neuronal function, hundreds of hippocal genes are potential targets but only a dozen are repressed in seizure-induced epilepsy, and selective regulation basis is due to mid-range binding frequencies to NRS. The study used large-scale transcriptome arrays, qPCR, chromatin immunoprecipitation and gene enrichment analysis to examine NRS-dependent expression during epileptogenesis. A twofold to threefold increase in NRS levels led to repression of a subset of regulated genes, rescued by interfering with NRS function.
Researchers have investigated the parallel pathways formed by mitral and tufted cells in the olfactory system of mice to understand how circuits in parallel pathways maintain or enhance the encoding of specific stimulus features. The study found differences in activity-dependent lateral inhibition between mitral and tuft cells, likely due to differences in the activation of deep and superficial granule cells. Simulations showed that these circuit-level differences allow mitral and tuft cells to best discriminate odors in separate concentration ranges, indicating that segregating information about different ranges of stimulus intensity may be an important function of parallel pathways. The mammalian olfactory bulb contains two classes of projection neurons, mitral cells and tuft cells, which receive input from olfactory sensory neurons and exhibit distinct responses to odors due to differences in connectivity. The combination of activity-dependent inhibition at low and intermediate rates enables mitral and tuft cells to collectively encode odors better than either population alone, supporting novel computations that are unlikely with a single neuron type. Local circuit activity creates selectivity in parallel pathways remains poorly understood, particularly in brain areas where distinct outputs are generated from initially homogeneous sources, such as the olfactory system. The study identified a novel mechanism generating differential responses across parallel pathways and examined the emergence of feature selectivity in mitral and tuft cells. Similarities between the olfactory and visual systems suggest mechanisms behind parallel processing may be shared, with feature selectivity emerging due to connectivity to specific cell types, such as bipolar and amacrine cells in the retina. In the olfactory system, differences in odor-evoked responses in mitral and tuft cells emerge due to differences in connectivity to olfactory sensory neurons and external tuft cells. The proposed mechanism involves activity-dependent inhibition in mitral and tuft cells, with distinct levels of activity in the olfactory circuit consisting of presynaptic and postsynaptic mitral tuft cells and associated granule cells, allowing for segregation of information about different ranges of stimulus intensity. The findings indicate that parallel pathways may be important for encoding specific stimulus features, with implications for understanding sensory processing in the mammalian olfactory bulb.
