The M2 muscarinic receptor is a prototypic model of allostery in G-protein-coupled receptors, yet the molecular and supramolecular determinants of such effects are unknown. To identify allosteric properties gained in oligomers, monomers and oligomers of the M2 muscarinic receptor were compared. Alloster interactions were monitored using a FRET-based sensor of conformation at the alloster site and pharmacological assays involving mutants to preclude intramolecular effects. Electrostatic, steric, and conformational determinants of allost at the atomic level were examined in molecular dynamics simulations. Results showed that alloic effects in monomers were exclusively negative and primarily derived from intramolecular electrostatic repulsion between the alloster and orthosteric ligands. In contrast, alloic effects in oligomers could be positive or negative depending on the alloster-orthestic pair and arose from interactions within and between constituent protomers. The complex behavior of oligomers is characteristic of muscarinic receptors in myocardial preparations. Studies have generally understood allost at the M2 muscar receptor in terms of interactions within a monomer, disregarding the tendency of G-protein-coupled receptors to form oligomers. However, the oligomeric nature of the M2 receptor is evident in multiphasic effects of alloic ligands on dissociation of orthosteric antagonists and their binding equilibrium. The M2 muscar receptor has been a prototype for such effects within the broader family of G-protein-coupled receptors, with most studies based on modulation of events at orthestic site typically on receptors in native or detergent-solubilized extracts. To understand how multimeric complex might account for alloster behavior resisting explanation in monomers, positive and negative alloster modulators were compared for effects on oligomers and purified monomers of the M2 muscar receptor. Alloster effects were examined in molecular dynamics at atomic level starting from the crystal structure of monomeric M receptor occupied by agonist ipxo. The ligand and accessory proteins were removed, and conformations of receptor sampled from canonical ensemble within 30ns period. Different liganded states identified as X_Y where X and Y are alloster and orthestic ligands respectively. Chemical structures of alloster and orthestic ligands to M2 muscar receptor include N-methylscopolamine, quinuclidylbenzilate, strychnine, and gallamine.
SETD2, the enzyme responsible for trimethylating histone H3 lysine 36, is required for ATM activation upon DNA double-strand breaks and homologous recombination repair of DNA double-strand breaks by promoting the formation of RAD51 presynaptic filaments. SET2 is necessary for the recruitment of RAD51 to resected DNA 5' ends and SET2-depleted cells show reduced homology-directed repair of double-strand breaks. Depletion of SET2 impairs ATM activation and leads to delayed recruitment of 53BP1 to sites of double-strand breaks. SET2 mutations were found in several cancers, including clear cell renal cell carcinoma, where loss of SET2 may afford an alternative mechanism for inactivation of the p53 checkpoint without genetic mutations in TP53. Despite persistence of DNA lesions, SET2-deficient cells failed to activate p53 and showed decreased survival after DNA damage. SET2-dependent provides a chromatin bookmarking instrument facilitating signaling and repair of double-strand breaks. SET2 is necessary for the DNA damage response and depletion of SET2 impairs ATM activation. Data reveal SET2 is required for recruitment of RAD51 to resect DNA 5' ends and SET2-depleted cells show reduced homology-directed repair of double-strands. Unlike histone modifications, SET2 does not promote increased H3K36me levels at DNA damage sites or DDR-dependent recruitment of SET2 to doublestrands. The study provides a functional link between genomic alterations and inactivating mutations and deletions of histone modifiers extending understanding of the impact of altered chromatin on mechanisms maintaining genome integrity. SET2 is necessary for activation of ATM following DNA damage and compromised in SET2-depleted cells treated with NCS or etopide but marginally affected in doublestrands induced phlemycin. The study identifies a novel role of histone methyltransferase rendering cells competent to repair doublestrands by priming chromatin for response to DNA damage.
Researchers have identified a new missense variant, Rasgrp1Ana, in the Rasgrp1 guanine nucleotide exchange factor, which exhibits antinuclear autoantibodies and accumulates a CD44hi Helios+ PD-1+ CD4+ T cell population dependent on B cells. Despite reduced Ras1-Ras-ER activation in vitro, thymocyte selection in Ras1Ana is mostly normal in vivo, although CD44 is overexpressed on naïve thymocytes and T cells in a T-autonomous manner. CD44 expression is identified as a reporter of tonic mTOR signaling through a novel strain, chino, with reduction-of-function in Mtor. Elevated mTOR signaling occurs in Ras1Ana naïve CD4 cells, and CD44 expression, CD4 cell ratios, and serum autoantibodies return to normal in Ras1AnaMtor double mutant mice, demonstrating increased mTOR activity is essential for Ras1 cell dysregulation. Ras1Ana is distinct from previously described autoimmune mutations in Ras1, Zap, or Lat, with no effect on thymocyte development, normal TCR repertoires, but peripheral accumulation of distinct Helios+ PD1 T cells and production of antinuclear autoantibodies. Ras1Ana increases mTOR signaling in naïve CD4 cells, dissociating this function from its role in thymic selection. Biochemical and genetic studies identify overactive mTOR within naïve CD4 cells driving two abnormalities: constitutive CD44 increase in naïve CD4 cells and accumulation of Helios CD44 CD cells and autoantibodies. Genetic reduction of mTOR normalizes CD44 on naïve CD4 cells and abolishes excessive effector cells and autoantibodies, demonstrating a central role for increased mTOR in immune dysregulation. RasAna effect on RasER signaling in vivo was milder than expected from in vitro assays. The findings reveal a new role for Ras1 in cell intrinsic regulation of CD4 cells, analyzing miss mutation in Ras1 EF, dissociating function from thymic selection, identifying overactive mTOR as key component driving abnormalities.
Mutations in the SWI/SNF chromatin remodeling complex are common in cancer, but the mechanisms by which disruption of SWI/SNF components alter tumorigenesis remain poorly understood. To model the effect of loss of function in the SWI/SNF subunit Arid1 in pancreatic ductal adenocarcinoma (PD) initiation, researchers used shRNA triggered, inducible, and reversible suppression of Arid1 in the mouse pancreas with oncogenic KrasG12. Arid1 cooperates with Kras in the adult pancreas, as postnatal silencing of Ar1 following KrasG12 expression induces rapid and irreversible reprogramming of acinar cells into mucinous PD precursor lesions. In contrast, Ar1 silencing during embryogenesis, concurrent with Kras12 activation, leads to retention of acinar fate. Suppression of Ar1 in adult acinar cells harboring oncogenic Kras mutations accelerates acinar to duct reprogramming and specification of mucinous pancreatic precursor lesions that cannot be reversed by restoring endogenous Ar1 expression, suggesting Ar1 provides a barrier to K driven acinar duct metaplasia and early pancreatic neoplasia. Alcian blue staining confirmed retention of mucinous lesions in Ar1-restored mice. Ar1 loss enables non-reversible reprogramming of cell fate dependent on cellular and genetic context. Perturbation of the SW/SNF chromatin-remodeling complex impacts expression of SW/SNF target genes. Genomic areas rendered by Ar1 harbor elements involved in preventing acinar duct transdifferentiation in mutant Kras. Transcriptional and chromatin profiling imply Ar1 disruption in oncogenic Kras reduces chromatin accessibility to enhancers of genes sustaining acinar fate. Ar1 stabilizes acinar identity acting as a barrier to pancreatic initiation, but other factors promote the PanIN-PDAC transition. Data suggest acute Ar1 loss in pre-existing Kras rapidly perturbs identity and function of pancreatic acinar cells, enhancing sensitivity to metlastic and Pan-inducing signals from oncogenic Kras. A mouse-model platform enables inducible disruption of gene function showing Ar1 modulates early pancreatic neoplasia context-dependent on timing of Ar1 inactivation and oncogenic Kras.
Research has shown that social networks can buffer the effects of stress, but the absence of a social network can also be stressful. A study examined the neuroendocrine paraventricular nucleus (PVN) corticotropin-releasing hormone (CRH) neurons and found that social isolation alters the intrinsic properties of these cells in a sexually dimorphic fashion. Specifically, isolating pre-adolescent female mice from littermates for 24 hours increased first spike latency and decreased excitability of CRH neurons, changes not evident in age-matched males. In contrast, subjecting either males or grouped or housed females to acute physical stress increased first spike latency. The increase in first spike latency following social isolation or physical stress was blocked by glucocortic synthesis inhibitor metyrapone and mimicked by exogenous corticosterone, resulting in decreased excitability of CRH neurons. Observations demonstrate social isolation, not acute stress, has sex-specific effects on PVN neurons.

The study found brief social isolation affects biophysical properties of PVN CRH neurons in a sexually dimorphic fashion, increasing first spike latency and decreasing neuronal excitability in socially isolated female but not age-matched male mice. Acute physical stress increased first spike latency in group-housed females and single-housed males. Social isolation occluded the effects of stress on female mice. Hypothalamic brain slices examined synaptic and intrinsic properties of identified CRH neurons in PV in acute slices. Activation of these cells is obligatory for launching neuroendocrine response to stress. Social isolation altered intrinsic properties of CRH neurons in female not male mice.

Subjecting single-housed male to acute stress increased first spike latency, while prior isolation occluded effects of acute stress in females. Findings demonstrate males and females react differently to some stressors, social isolation having definitive CORT-dependent effects on CR neurons in female not male. Both males and females show indistinguishable responses to acute physical stress. Prior social isolation occludes effects of subsequent physical stress on first spike latency in females, no effect on males. The glucortic synthesis inhibitor blocked the increase in first spike latency following social or physical stress, and exogenous corticosterone mimicked the increase, resulting in decreased excitability of CRH neurons. Overall, the study demonstrates sex-specific effects of social isolation on PVN neurons.
Hemoglobin is a crucial protein for studying molecular adaptation in vertebrates, with both affinity and cooperativity of oxygen binding affecting tissue delivery. Research suggests that hemoglobin affinity and cooperativity reflect evolutionary and physiological adaptations optimized for tissue oxygen delivery. The relationship between the Hill coefficient and relative conformational changes of the Monod-Wyman-Changeux allosteric model reveals a 'biophysical landscape' describing this relation. Mammalian hemoglobin cooperativity values reside on a ridge of maximum cooperativity, allowing for gross and fine-tuning of oxygen unloading to meet distinct metabolic tissue requirements. Evolutionary tuning of mammalian hemoglobin maximum cooperativity and affinity regulates oxygen delivery by varying quatern states, meeting distinct tissue oxygen needs. Different mammalian hemoglobins exhibit varying maximum Hill values, with higher values giving rise to higher sensitivities to substrate unloading. The cooperativity of hemoglobin has been tuned during evolution to satisfy tissue oxygen supply requirements. Small animals with high metabolic rates display lower oxygen affinity than large animals, reflecting adaptive adjustment of oxygen unloading. Changes in mammalian hemoglobin binding conformation have been tuned during evolution, yielding maximum cooperativity and varying Hill values. The human physiological dataset shows greater animal metabolic rate correlates with higher Hill cooperativity and inverse correlation between metabolic rate and hemoglobin affinity. These findings reveal the mechanism of body-size adaptation of mammalian hemoglobin.
Malaria infection begins with the injection of Plasmodium sporozoites by Anopheles mosquitoes into the skin of mammalian hosts, and understanding how sporozoites locate and enter blood vessels is crucial. Researchers used intravital microscopy in mice to examine sporozoite motility and interaction with dermal blood vessels, revealing two types of motility: avascular and perivascular. Avascular motility is characterized by high speed and less confinement in areas far from vessels, while perivascular motility is more constrained near vessels. The curvature of sporozoite tracks optimizes contact with dermal capillaries. Imaging sporozoites with mutations in key proteins highlighted the importance of gliding speed and modulating adhesive properties for successful exit from the inoculation site. 

The study presents a quantitative in vivo analysis of Plmodium sporozoite motility over time and interaction with dermal vascular endothelia, characterizing changes in motility over the first two hours after intradermal inoculation. CSN and TRAP sporozoites were rendered fluorescent to study surface protein function in dermal motility and vessel recognition. CSN sporozoites spent more time with vessels but failed to enter circulation, while TRAP sporozoites had reduced gliding speed and explored less tissue volume, resulting in decreased vessel encounters. Gliding motility is essential for infectivity, with previous reports observing dermal motility but lacking in-depth analysis over time. Intrital imaging over 120 minutes post-inoculation showed significant motility changes. 

Data demonstrate sporozoite motility changes in response to environmental signals, exhibiting avascular and perivascular motility. Avascular motility has high speed and less confinement in avascular areas, while perivascular motility is constrained near vessels. Mutant sporozoites with altered motility patterns or speed had decreased vessel invasion and infectivity. The study refines understanding of sporozoite navigation through the dermis, indicating exit from the inoculation as a barrier and opportune time to intervene. The findings highlight the importance of gliding speed and adhesive properties for successful blood vessel invasion. The research provides insight into Plasmodium sporozo motility and interaction with dermal vessels, crucial for understanding malaria infection and potential interventions.
Cells are organized into functional modules, typically containing components whose removal severely compromises the module's function, yet these components are not absolutely conserved across the tree of life, suggesting cells can evolve to perform the same biological functions with different proteins. Researchers evolved Saccharomyces cerevisiae for 100 generations without the important polarity gene BEM1 and found the bem1∆ lineages initially rapidly increased in fitness and then slowly reached over 90% of the fitness of their BEM ancestors at the end of the evolution. Sequencing their genomes and monitoring polarization revealed a common evolutionary trajectory with a sequence of adaptive mutations improving cell polarization by inactivating proteins. The results show organisms can be evolutionarily robust to physiologically destructive perturbations and recovery by gene inactivation can lead to rapid divergence in parts for biologically important functions. The loss of BEM1 led to a reproducible evolutionary trajectory largely explained by interactions amongst mutated genes resulting in substantial recovery in speed and accuracy of cell polarization. Deleting BEM, a relatively young component in polarity establishment with a different role in ancestral fungi, revealed alternative evolutionarily ancient polarization modules. Multiple layers of regulation allow cells to polarize and adapt to strong perturbations under selective pressure favoring evolutionary trajectories removing components from modules over quantitatively altering properties. Adaptive mutations changed polarization dynamics to re-establish efficient polarization and bem1 cells often contained more than one polarity site, suggesting weaker positive feedback for single site polarization. The study demonstrates cells can evolve to perform the same functions with different proteins and recover from destructive perturbations through gene inactivation, leading to divergence in biologically important functions.
Neurobiologists have traditionally studied decision-making by offering successive, independent choices between goods or gambles, but real-life choices often have lasting consequences, such as investing in a house or choosing a partner. Researchers investigated how humans decide to commit or reject prospects with sustained financial returns and found that BOLD signals in the rostral medial prefrontal cortex (rmPFC) encoded value when acceptance or rejection was deferred, suggesting a role in integrating signals over time. In contrast, the dorsal anterior cingulate cortex (dACC) encoded value when participants rejected or deferred a prospect. The dACC signals reflected two biases: deferring commitments to later and weighing potential losses more heavily than gains, paradoxically maximizing reward in the task. 

Previous studies examined neural mechanisms of economic behavior through independent gambles or consumer choices, but this study devised a task involving commitment to assets with enduring financial consequences. The aim was to understand computational mechanisms of economic commitments and neural implementation in the reward circuitry of the medial prefrontal cortex. Commitments often follow deliberation, where items are considered but acceptance or rejection is deferred. It remains unknown whether value coding depends on acceptance or rejection and how encoding in the prefrontal cortex depends on economic commitment. 

The study investigated mechanisms accompanying commitment, rejection, and deferral during economic choice using a multi-alternative task with financial ramifications over episodes that could not be reversed. In half the blocks, participants chose between accepting or deferring a prospect. The dACC encoded value when a prospect was excluded, while the rmPFC encoded during deferral. Joint consideration of behavioral data and dACC activity pinpointed two pressures shaping decisions: a bias to defer and a bias to unfavorable options. The findings offer insights into pressures shaping economic decisions and computation in the prefrontal cortex. The study used a task where humans decided to commit by acceptance or rejection to prospects with sustained financial returns and found that BOLD signals in the rmPFC encoded value when acceptance or rejection was deferred into the future. The dACC encoded value when participants rejected or deferred a prospect, reflecting biases that maximized reward in the task, providing fresh insights into pressures shaping economic decisions and computation in the prefront cortex.
Paraspeckles are nuclear bodies formed around the long non-coding RNA Neat1 and RNA-binding proteins, and their role is believed to control gene expression at a post-transcriptional level by means of nuclear retention of mRNA containing 3'-UT inverted repeats of Alu sequences. This study found that in pituitary cells, all components of paraspeckles, including four major proteins and Neat1, displayed a circadian pattern. The insertion of IRAl at the 3'-UT of the EGFP cDNA led to rhythmic circadian nuclear retention of egfp mRNA that was lost when parasckles were disrupted, whereas insertion of a single antisense Alu had a weak effect. Real-time microscopy showed IRAl drove circadian expression of EGFP protein, indicating parasckles control circadian expression at post-transcription level. Nuclear retention of IRus in 3'-UT of genes provides an additional layer of gene regulation by sequestering mature mRNAs within the nucleus. Components of parasckles, namely NONO and SFP, display circadian pattern in primary cultures of pitary cells and rat pitary cell line GH4. Disruption of parasckles resulted in loss of rhythmic pattern in circadian genes, suggesting parasck retention plays a role in circadian expression. Post-transcription circadian regulation plays a major role in determining oscillations at mRNA level. Parasck bodies may rhythmically retain RNAs in nucleus, leading to rhythmic expression of corresponding gene. The GH4 pitary cell line was used to determine if posttranscription mechanisms allowing circadian expression in pitary cells involve circadian nuclear retention by parasck bodies. The presence of parasck was characterized, showing nuclear bodies were rhythmically expressed in the rat4 pitary cell line. RNA-binding proteins target antisense Alu elements within 3'-UT of mRNAs, HNPC display circadian pattern in several tissues including mouse pitary. The study shows parasckles control circadian expression at post-transcription level through nuclear retention of mRNA containing 3'-UT inverted Alu sequences.
Researchers have identified the active-zone protein Munc13 as a crucial regulator of presynaptic voltage-gated calcium channels, which are essential for converting electrical signals into chemical communication. Munc13, previously known for its role in exocytosis, interacts with voltage-gated calcium channels via a pair of basic residues in its C2 domain, dictating their behavior during various forms of activity. Studies demonstrate that eliminating this interaction by removing M13 or replacing it with a mutant M13 C2 alters synaptic calcium channel response and recovery from high-frequency action bursts and calcium influx from single action stimuli. This reveals a novel form of synaptic modulation where M13 impacts information transfer at nerve terminals by controlling both vesicle priming and the trigger for exocytosis. The presence of M13 has a potent influence on voltage-gated calcium channel function and delineates a new class of mechanisms for sculpting synaptic efficacy. The dependence of exytosis on calcium influx and control of voltage-gated channels on fast scales implies synaptic throughput can be actively controlled by voltage-gated channel-active zone interactions. M13 interacts with voltage-gated channels in a way that controls calcium channel dependence on millisecond to second time scales at nerve terminals, with a critical site pinpointed to two basic residues within the C2 domain of M13. This interaction enhances calcium influx and modulates kinetic properties of voltage-gated channels across a range of stimulation conditions, with implications for understanding synaptic transmission control. Removing M13 results in a 94% depletion of the protein at nerve terminals, blocking exytosis, and altering action-driven calcium influx. Rescue experiments suggest the increase in exytosis during paired pulses is a direct effect of the lack of M13-voltage-gated channel interaction, not a secondary effect of reduced exytosis. The interaction site in M13's C2 domain mediates voltage-gated channel reactivation in nerve terminals.
The shape and function of plant cells are highly interdependent, with puzzle-shaped cells in the epidermis of many plants being a striking example of complex cell shape, but their functional benefit has remained elusive. Researchers propose that these intricate forms provide an effective strategy to reduce mechanical stress in the cell wall of the epidermis. When tissue growth is isotropic, lobes emerge at the cellular level to prevent the formation of isodiametric cells that would bulge under stress produced by turgor pressure. Data from various plant organs and species support the relationship between lobes and growth isotropy, tested with mutants where growth direction is perturbed. Simulation models show a mechanism regulating cellular stress plausibly reproduces the development of epmal shape, suggesting mechanical stress is a key driver of cell-shape morphogenesis.

Puzzle-shaped cells in the epider of many plant species emerge from a mechanism to limit mechanical stress in tissues that grow isotropically, such as the epider of leaves and cotyledons. FEM analysis of 3D pressized cells shows cell shape influences the direction and magnitude of mechanical stress exerted on the cell. When epmal cell becomes large in two directions, stress is greatly increased. The puzzle shape, with lobes and indentations, provides a solution to avoid excessive stress in tissues growing two directions, such as the surface of broad leaves. Analyzing stress in a model of the cotyled epider of Arabidopsis thalian, stems, roots, and sili, growth is strongly anisotropic and cells can elongate, but not possible for isodiam organs such as broad leaves, cotyled, and silicle pods.

Puzzle cells in epider of isodiam organs provide means to avoid large areas in cell and high stresses. The correspondence between fruit shape and puzzle epmal cells fits the prediction that puzzle shapes are required to allow cells to enlarge in isotropically growing tissues but not in elongated organs. The model predicts plant cells regulate shape to prevent LEC, a proxy for stress, from becoming large. The puzzle shape is an adaptation to developmental constraint related to mechanical forces acting on turg plant cells in epider. Mechanically, plant cells are small balloons with considerable turg pressure up to 10 bar in Arabidopsis leaf, reaching 50 bar in specialized cells such as stomata. Researchers propose a function for the puzzle as an adaptation to developmental constraint related to mechanical forces acting on turg plant cells that reside in epider.
Human genes contain multiple introns, necessitating mechanisms to define exons and ensure their proper connection by spliceosomes. Spliceosome assembly involves both cross-intron and cross-exon interactions, but how these work together is unclear. Researchers examined human nuclear extracts dynamic interactions of single pre-mRNA molecules with fluorescently tagged spliceosomal complexes to investigate cross-intron and cross-exon processes promoting pre-splice assembly. U1 complex bound to the 5' splice site of an intron acts jointly with U1 bound to the 5' site of the next intron, increasing the rate and efficiency of U2 complex recruitment to the branch/3 splice site of the upstream intron. The flanking 5 splice sites have greater than additive effects, implying distinct mechanisms facilitating U recruitment. This synergy of 5 splice sites across introns and exons likely promotes correct and efficient splicing of multi-intron pre-mRNAs. An ordered pre-splice assembly pathway is suggested where stable U2 association in the presence of ATP requires prior U1 binding. In multi-intron preRNAs, internal exons have 5S both upstream and downstream, either of which could bind U1 and act to recruit U2 to 3S. The dynamics of U binding the 5S are largely unaffected by U2 sites positioned downstream or upstream of the 5S. Pre-splice can form on multi-intron preRNAs through two pathways, an intron recognition via cross-intron interactions or cross-exon interactions where exons are defined. Collaboration between flanking 5S increases the rate of stable U2 recruitment during pre-splice formation, a major contributor to exon inclusion. The presence of a downstream U does not affect lifetimes of stable U2 complexes once formed. U1-U2 pre-splice forms across the intron, interactions with U bound to the downstream 5S provide additional U2 stabilization or stimulation of subsequent steps of splice assembly. Implementing CoSM in extracts from human cells expressing tagged proteins shows collaboration between flanking 5S dramatically increases the rate of stable U2 recruitment during pre-splice formation, contributing to accurate splicing of multi-intron preRNAs.
Researchers have discovered a novel folding system that assists membrane proteins on the cytosolic side of the endoplasmic reticulum, specifically the Wnt signaling coreceptor LRP6, which requires help to fold efficiently. The folding of LRP6 is promoted by ubiquination of a specific lysine, retaining it in the ER while avoiding degradation. Subsequent ER exit requires removal of ubiquitin from this lysine by the deubiquinating enzyme USP19. This ubiquination-deubiquination is reminiscent of glucylation-deglucylation occurring in the ER lumen during the calnex/calretulin cycle. To avoid futile cycles, folded LRP molecules undergo palmitation and export, while unsuccessfully folded proteins are polyubiquinated on other lysines and targeted to degradation. The ubiquitin-dependent system controls proteostasis of other membrane proteins, such as CFTR and anthrax receptor 2, involved in severe diseases. Upon ubiquination on Lys-140, LRP interacts with an ubiquitin-binding protein acting as a chaperone, providing time to fold. If folding is successful, LRP is transported out of the ER, favored by palmitation. If folding is impaired, polyubiquination of other lysines targets LRP to ERAD. The identified ubiquination-deubiquination assisted-folding cycle operates on other membrane proteins, including CFTR and anthrax receptor 2. Following synthesis, LRP undergoes ubiquination on Lys-140, likely a specific form, and if folding is delayed, polyubiquination targets L6 to ERAD. The Lys140-ubiqu can interact with putative ER-ubiqu binding protein acting as chone, and upon release, L6 is deubiquinated by USP19. This system is analogous to newly synthesized mono-glucylated proteins binding calnex in ER lumen. Loss of function of CFTR and anthrax receptor 2 leads to diseases, such as Hyaline Fibromatosis. The ubiquitin-dependent folding machinery operates on the cytos side of ER, showing that following synthesis, L6 undergoes ubiquination, and if folding is successful, is transported out of ER, and if not, polyubiquinated and targeted to degradation.
Research has shown that sex-based modulation of cognitive processes may contribute to individual differences in vulnerability to neuropsychiatric disorders, with value-based decision making being a key area of interest. While overall performance in decision-making tasks often shows minimal sex differences, computational tools can uncover latent variables that define decision approaches. A study examined sex differences in mice in a classic value-based decision task, the restless two-armed bandit, and found that male and female mice achieved similar accuracy through different patterns of exploration. Male mice made more exploratory choices, getting "stuck" in exploration once started, whereas female mice explored less but learned quickly. The results suggest sex influences decision-making during learning and exploration periods rather than stable choices. Exploration during decision-making is altered in people with addictions, depression, and neurodevelopmental disabilities, pinpointing neural mechanisms of exploration as a translational avenue for sex-modulated vulnerability to neuropsychiatric diagnoses.

Male and female mice were trained on an explore-exploit task, a restless two-armed bandit, and males showed higher levels of exploration throughout. Computational tools characterized sex differences in latent variables to understand reward-guided behaviors, balancing exploration, and exploitation. The results revealed significant sex differences in exploration and learning rates. Post-hoc win-stay-shift analyses found female mice displayed more win-stay behaviors, repeating behaviors that produced rewards on the previous trial. The study used a combination of modeling approaches to characterize sex differences in a canonical explore-exploit task and found males and females used different explore-exploit strategies to learn the environment despite similar performance. The findings indicate sex mechanisms biasing preferred approaches during tasks contribute to performance and are significant contributors to task variability across species. The study highlights the importance of understanding how sex, explore-exploit states, and outcomes interact to influence choices and decision-making, particularly in the context of neuropsychiatric disorders.
Endoplasmic reticulum-associated degradation (ERAD) is a process where membrane proteins are ubiquitinated, extracted from the membrane, and degraded by the proteasome, with the cytosolic ATPase Cdc48 driving extraction by pulling polyubiquinated substrates. The mechanism of how hydrophobic transmembrane segments are moved from the phospholipid bilayer into cytosol, together with hydrophil and folded ER luminal parts, is not well understood. Using a reconstituted system with purified proteins from Saccharomyces cerevisiae, researchers have shown that the ubiquitin ligase Doa10 is a retrotransase that facilitates membrane extraction. The substrate's TM segment interacts with the membrane-embedded domain of Doa and passively moves into the aqueous phase, while luminal segments cross the membrane in an unfolded state. Unfolding occurs on the luminal side of the membrane by cytoplasmic Cdc action, revealing how a membrane-bound retrotransase cooperates with the Cdc ATPase in membrane extraction. Doa10 is a 150 kDa protein with 14 TM segments and has been suggested to act as a retrotransase for membrane proteins, along with other multipass transmembrane proteins such as Derlin family members, Hrd1, and Do10. The folding state of luminal domains during retrolocation has been unclear, whether moved in a folded state, unfolding prior to retrolocation, or directly coupled to retrolocation. Quality pathways for membrane proteins require extraction from the membrane exist not only in ER, but in other organelles like Golgi, mitochondria, and chloroplast, dependent on ubiquination and C48 for proteomal degradation in cytosol. The reconstituted system allowed mechanistic investigation of membrane extraction without indirect readouts, showing Do10 cooperates with Cdc ATPase in extraction of proteins with folded luminal domains, providing insight into ERAD mediated by ubiquitin ligase Do10 from S cerevisiae.
Hedgehog (H) proteins play a crucial role in guiding development and maintaining tissue homeostasis in both invertebrates and vertebrates. Aberrant H protein production, distribution, and responses are common causes of developmental defects and cancer, including holoprosencephaly, limb abnormalities, medulloblastoma, and basal carcinoma. In Drosophila, H signaling induces transcriptional changes in target cells by inhibiting the proteolytic processing of full-length Ci proteins to transcriptional repressors and activating full-length Ci proteins. Researchers used Ci variants expressed at physiological levels to investigate the contributions of these mechanisms to dose-dependent H signaling in Dophila wing imaginal discs. The results showed that Ci variants that cannot be processed supported a normal pattern of graded gene activation and development of adults with normal wing morphology when supplemented by constitutive repressor. This indicates that H can signal normally in the absence of regulated processing. The processing-resistant Ci variants were also activated in the absence of H by elimination of Cos2, likely through binding the CORD domain of Ci or PKA, revealing separate inhibitory roles of these components in addition to their roles in promoting Ci processing. Furthermore, Ci variants supported the development of adults with normal patterning provided a constitutive source of repressor was present to suppress ectopic expression in anterior cells. Thus, Ci can mediate H morphogen action in wing discs in the absence of regulated processing. The Ci profile was similar to wild-type with robust H-stimulated reduction in the posterior part of the AP border. In third instar Drosila wing discs, H is expressed in posterior cells and Ci in anterior cells, H signals to a band of cells at the AP border with declining strength from posterior to anterior. The results show that wing patterning by H does not require regulation of the level of repressor or full-length protein by processing. Animals lacking Fu and Su also develop normal wings, although third instar discs lack anterior induction. Overall, the study demonstrates that H can act as a morphogen in the absence of regulated Ci processing and highlights the importance of understanding the mechanisms of H signaling in development and disease.
Researchers have identified a network of excitatory neurons in the spinal dorsal horn that play a crucial role in pain processing and amplification. The study utilized optogenetics to selectively activate spinal interneurons expressing the calcium-binding protein calretinin, demonstrating that these interneurons form an interconnected network capable of initiating and sustaining excitatory signaling and directly relaying signals to lamina I projection neurons. Activation of these interneurons resulted in significant nocifensive behavior that was morphine sensitive, caused conditioned aversion, and was enhanced by nerve injury, while halorhodin-mediated inhibition elevated sensory thresholds. The findings suggest that dorsal horn circuits involving excitatory neurons are important for pain generation and amplification, identifying these interneurons as a potential analgesic target. The results confirm that calretinin neurons form an excitatory network in the dorsal horn contributing to spinal signaling and amplifying pain signals in the absence of peripheral input. The strong interconnectivity of this neurochemically-defined subpopulation positions them to alter sensory information prior to its relay to higher centers, demonstrated by the nocifensive responses elicited by photostimulation. The study focused on characterizing microcircuits and their roles in sensory experience through optogenetic activation, with future work needed to determine circuit responses during peripherally evoked processing. Several groups have implicated neurochemically-distinct subpopulations of dorsal horn interneurons in acute and chronic pain perception. Current models place calretinin neurons in polysynaptic circuits signaling through SOM neurons to activate lamina projection and initiate pain, compatible with data showing CR-Ch2 photostimulation evoking EPSCs in dorsal neurons including superficial populations. The observations extend the view of excitatory interneurons in sensory processing, ascribing a role beyond low threshold modality innocuous input to excite nociceptive circuitry. The study highlights the importance of clarifying neuronal circuits in the spinal dorsal horn to understand dysfunction leading to pathological pain and identifies calretinin neurons as a potential target for analgesic intervention.
Mutations in the gene encoding Ras-associated protein 23 (R23) cause Carpenter Syndrome, characterized by multiple developmental abnormalities including polysyndactyly and defects in skull morphogenesis. To understand how R23 regulates skull development, R23-deficient mice were generated that survive to an age where skeletal development can be studied. Along with polysyactyly, these mice exhibit premature fusion of sutures resultant from aberrant osteoprogenitor proliferation and elevated osteogenesis in the suture. FGF-driven FGFR1 signaling is elevated in R23-/- sutures with consequent imbalance in MAPK, Hedgehog and RUN2 expression. Inhibition of elevated pER1/2 signaling results in normalization of osteopitor proliferation with concomitant reduction of osteogenic expression and prevention of craniosynostosis. R23 deficient mice exhibited polysyndyacty and craniosynoses similar to Carpenter syndrome patients, with disruption of R23 leading to upregulation of Pit2, Fgf and Ffrb expression, decreased p38 and enhanced pER1-RX signaling along with osteoprogen proliferation. R23 regulates the sutural stem cell marker GL1, represses Hh and inhibits osteoprogen expansion. R23 represses FGF-driven pER1 signaling which regulates osteoprogen development, RUNX-mediated differentiation and repress GL1. R23 coordinates suture biogenesis by regulating FGF-ER1-RX activity and activation of GL. R23 controls suture morphogenesis through FGF-p1 signaling, repression of H targets and repression of GL1 by p1. FGF-p1 and H signaling regulate osteoprogen cell proliferation. Overlapping skeletal phenotypes caused by mutations in FGFR, H regulators and R23 are suggestive of common etiological mechanisms. F and H signaling have roles during intramembranous osteogenesis, regulating mesenchal condensation, osteoprogen proliferation and RUN2 activation. Inhibition of p1 normalized osteop proliferation, corrected GL and RUN expressions and rescued lamboid suture fusion. R23 is an upstream regulator of F-p1 and H-GL and additional regulation of GL by p to coordinate osteogenesis.
The inheritance of gene expression states is crucial for cells to remember past events, such as environmental or developmental cues. The Polycomb Repressive Complex 2 (PRC2) maintains epigenetic repression of many genes in animals and plants and modifies chromatin at its targets, allowing histones modified by PR2 to be inherited through cell division. However, it remains unclear whether this inheritance directs long-term memory of individual gene states, known as cis memory, or if local chromatin states are dictated by concentrations of diffusible factors, known as trans. To address this, researchers monitored the expression of two copies of the Arabidopsis Polycomb target FLOWERING LOCUS (LC) in the same plants and found that one copy can be repressed while the other is active. This mixed expression state is inherited through many cell divisions as plants develop, demonstrating epigenetic memory of LC expression is stored in cis, not trans. The study utilized the epigenetic process vernalization in Arabidopsis, where vernalization is the acceleration of flowering following prolonged cold exposure, mediated by cold-induced epigen repression of the Polycomb target and floral repressor LC. LC repression requires PR2 but is independent of DNA methylation, making it a useful system for studying cis-memory capability of Polycomb at a single gene. In vernalized plants, LC expression is bistable, with the number of cells in which LC stably repressed increasing quantitatively with the duration of prior cold exposure. The researchers developed an assay to visualize mitotic heritability of LC states at the single-cell level and crossed LC-mCherry to LC-Venus plants to generate F1 hybrids carrying a single copy of each transgene. The results showed that epigen memory can be stored locally in the chromatin environment of individual genes, in the form of post-translational modifications of histones, rather than in concentrations of diffus regulatory factors maintained through feedback loops. This study demonstrates that Polycomb-repressed chromatin at a single gene instructs its own epigenetic inheritance and constitutes a cis memory system, providing insight into the long-term memory of individual gene states.
Researchers investigated the structure and function of oligomers formed by the endoplasmic retic reticulum Hsp70 chaperone BiP, using site-specific proteolysis and crosslinking to probe their architecture. The results showed that adjacent protomers engage the interdomain linker of one molecule in the substrate site of another, attenuating the chaper function of oligomeric BiP. Native gel electrophoresis revealed a rapidly-modulated relationship between the burden of unfolded proteins and BiP oligomers and slower equilibration between oligomers and inactive covalently-modified BiP. Lumen ER calcium depletion caused rapid oligomerization of mammalian Bi and a coincidental diminution in substrate binding, indicating the relative inertness of oligomers. The equilibration between inactive oligomers and active monomeric Bi is poised to buffer fluctuations in ER unfolded protein load on a rapid timescale attainable neither by interconversion of active and covalently-modified Bi nor the conventional unfolded response. BiP oligization is rapidly tuned to the physiological state of the cell and given oligomerization inactivates participating Bi protomers, making the oligomeric form a relatively inactive storage of chaper accessible on a short timescale. Findings are consistent with a model where competition between oligomerization and substrate buffers ER against rapid fluctuations in unfolded protein load in both directions. The affinity of Hsp proteins for substrates is governed by protein-driven non-equilibrium mechanisms, allowing BiP to favor itself as client over other clients as calcium declines. Calcium depletion shifts the equilibrium between unfolded proteins and chaperones in favor of the former. The inverse correlation between unfolded protein burden and Bi oligomerization breaks down in the context of calcium depletion, which induces UPR and promotes Bi oligomerization. Enhanced production of unfolded proteins draws on the pool of inactive oligeric Bi to service its growing clientele. Reports of oligomers in stressed cells may reflect partitioning excess chaper produced during UPR made redundant following resolution. Exploring the relationship between unfolded load and oligeric Hsp chaperones is confounded by feedback dominating unfolded responses coupling chaper production to unfolded stress levels.
Flexible filamentous viruses are economically important plant pathogens that contain hundreds of copies of helically arrayed coat protein protecting a positive-sense single-stranded RNA. The near-atomic cryo-electron microscopy structure of Pepino mosaic virus, a representative of the Potexvirus family, has been determined at 3.9 Å resolution, allowing modeling of the coat protein and its interactions with viral RNA. The overall fold of Pepino mosaic virus coat protein resembles that of nucleoproteins from the Phlebovirus, a group of enveloped negative-sense RNA viruses. The main difference between potexvirus coat and phlebovirus is in their C-terminal extensions, which determine the characteristics of distinct multimer assemblies, a flexuous helical rod or loose ribonucleoprotein. This homology suggests gene transfer between eukaryotic positive and negative-sense RNA viruses. The structural homology between the coat of flexuous viruses and nucleoproteins of Phlebovirus, a group of enveloped viruses with segmented negative-sense genome, has been found. Nucleoproteins from phleboviruses are associated with the viral genome in loose ribonucleoproteins inside an envelope, which glycoproteins construct icosahedral shell. Flexible filamentous viruses are non-enveloped flexible rod-shaped virions containing hundreds of copies of coat protein arranged helically, protecting positive-sense RNA. High-resolution structures for flexuous filamentous plant viruses correspond to representatives of Potexvirus family, including current data and previously reported structures of PapMV and BaMV. Members of other families of flexuous filamentous viruses may have similar fold in their coat proteins. Both potvirus coat and phlebovirus nucleoproteins bind and protect the genomic single-stranded RNA.
Antibody binding to cell surface proteins plays a crucial role in immunity, and the location of an epitope can determine the immunological outcome of host interaction. Current techniques for epitope identification are costly and time-consuming, making them unsuitable for high-throughput analysis. Fast and efficient screening of epitope location can aid in the development of therapeutic monoclonal antibodies and vaccines. Cellular morphology varies, and antibodies bind heterogeneously across the surface, making traditional particle-averaging strategies challenging for accurate localization. A new method, SiteLoc, has been developed for imaging-based molecular localization on cellular proteins, achieving nanometer resolution through localization in one dimension, the distance from a bound ligand to a reference, using topological averaging. This method is suited for antibody site measurements on native surface morphology and can be applied to other molecular estimations. Fast and efficient screening of epitope locations is useful for therapeutic development, while averaging methods for nanometer localization exist but do not work for binding sites on proteins with heterogeneous expression. Knowing the location of epitope aids mechanistic understanding and facilitates therapeutic development. Super-resolution techniques achieve ~10 nm resolution but are costly and require complex sample preparation. Binding site localization is based on resolving axial distance between ligands and surface by implementing averaging on high-resolution images, achieving nanometer precision by reducing surface data within an image. Site measurement on antibody-coated bacteria is exemplified, and the method can estimate molecular heights on spherical and non-spherical surfaces. Site measurements for ligands on bacterial protein agree with reported data, and antibody Fc site on protein is located at a specific region. Non-specific monoclonal IgG antibody is used for localization, demonstrating the method's ability to accurately estimate molecular binding sites on native cell surfaces with nanometer precision.
Cardiovascular adaptation to hypoxia is a complex process regulated by various factors, including peripheral resistance in tissue capillary beds. The relationship between central control of vascular flow and peripheral resistance is not fully understood, particularly in response to hypoxic conditions. Researchers investigated the role of hypoxic response in individual tissues and its effect on systemic adaptation to oxygen deprivation, focusing on the skin as a model for peripheral tissue with substantial vascular bed. They found that hypoxic response via HIF transcription factors in the skin influences cardiovascular response to hypoxia in mice, affecting parameters such as heart rate, arterial pressures, and body temperature. This study demonstrates a dynamic role for oxygen sensing in peripheral tissue modifying cardiovascular response to hypoxia. Peripheral resistance and oxygen sensing in the skin play an essential role in systemic response, showing tissues can adapt to hypoxia. Analysis of hypox-responsive genes in skin revealed a decrease in temperature in mice lacking HIF-1α in keratinocytes. Experiments using 10% oxygen challenge showed significant changes in systemic pressures and cutaneous temperatures in mice lacking HIF1α, H2, NOS, and Arg1 expression. Keratinocyte HIF1 and H2 expression affects immediate and chronic phases of cardiovascular response to hypia, with hypia heightening sympathetic tone in skin to increase peripheral resistance. A proposed model for hypoxic responses involves HIF isoform and nitric oxide homeostasis, where hypia results in H1α/N2/NO increase in vascular diameter and perfusion, followed by H2/Arg-induced reduction of intracellular L-arginine. This study provides the first demonstration of a dynamic role for oxygen sensing in peripheral tissue directly modifying cardiovascular response to hypia, highlighting the importance of peripheral resistance and oxygen sensing in cardiovascular adaptation to hypox.
Researchers have developed a refined optogenetic method for mapping that enables measurements of functional connectivity with single-neuron resolution by expanding two-photon beam imaging in the plane using temporal focusing and restricting channelrhodopsin to the soma and proximal dendrites. This approach allows for the reliable evocation of action potentials in individual neurons, verification of spike generation with GCaMPs, and determination of the presence or absence of synaptic connections with patch-clamp electrophysiological recording. Optical activation of single neurons using two-photon excitation of caged neurotransmitters or optogen probes, such as channelrhodopsin, provides a powerful approach for assessing synaptic connections of single neurons. Optogen mapping utilizing channelrhodin and its variants has increased flexibility and precision, but leaves an indeterminate regions coinciding with the dendritic arbors of the recorded neuron, which can extend 200µm or more around the soma. Unintended activation of fibers of passage or local axonal boutons responding to temporal two-photon excitation can also compromise the optogen approach. Combining temporal focusing two-photon activation of channelrhodin with restriction to the soma and proximal dendrites yields a reliable method for evaluating synaptic connectivity with single-neuron resolution. The synaptic organization of neurons into circuits is the basis for interpreting sensory input and producing behavioral responses. Understanding connectivity patterns among distinct neurons is critical for elucidating circuit function and requires methods that map connectivity with single-cell resolution. This study demonstrates the ability to use optogen stimulation to selectively target single neurons by restricting channelrhodin expression to the soma and proximal dendrites, overcoming limitations of previous approaches where opsins expressed throughout dendritic and axon fields generated confounding light-induced responses. By doing so, it provides a method to map functional connectivity with single-neuron resolution, critical for understanding neural circuit function.
The interaction mode of transcription factors on eukaryotic genomes remains a topic of debate, with single-molecule data for Sox2 and Oct4 previously interpreted as evidence of ordered assembly on DNA. However, a rigorous biophysical analysis reveals that the quantity calculated does not determine binding order but rather energy away from thermodynamic equilibrium, leading to the concept of reciprocity. Single-molecule data implies Sox2 and Oct4 exhibit negative reciprocity, with expression of Sox2 increasing Oct4's genomic binding but expression of Oct4 decreasing Sox2's binding. Models show negative reciprocity can arise from energy expenditure or a mixture of positive and negative cooperativity at distinct genomic loci, implying complexity in transcription factor interaction on DNA. The analysis found the sign of reciprocity is different from ordered assembly, with Sox2 and Oct4 sometimes helping and hindering each other across the genome, which is unexpected but not surprising given transcription factors exhibit different cooperativities at different loci. The implications of this finding need to be addressed in future studies, with the most intriguing consequence being the possibility of energy expenditure in the action of Sox2 and Oct on DNA. Evidence of mixed cooperativity or energy has not been found in living cells, suggesting complexity in how Sox2 and Oct interact on DNA, with single-molecule methods offering capabilities for unraveling mechanisms. The variation in sign of cooperativity across the genome has significant ramifications, providing more information when coupled with experimental strategy, and a single number can suggest Sox2 and Oct exhibit positive and negative cooperativity genomic loci in vivo. The genomic-diversity model suggests energy expenditure maintains loci away from thermodynamic equilibrium or mixed cooperativity with Sox and Oct helping or hindering at other loci.
Detecting pathogens and mounting immune responses upon infection is crucial for animal health, but these responses come at a high metabolic cost, and avoiding pathogens before infection may be advantageous. Bacterial endotoxins lipopolysaccharides are important immune system cues, but it remains unknown whether animals possess sensory mechanisms to detect them prior to infection. Drosophila melanaster display strong aversive responses to lipopolysaccharides and gustatory neurons expressing Gr66 bitter receptors mediate avoidance of lipopolysaccharides in feeding and egg laying assays. The expression of the chemosensory cation channel TRPA1 in these cells is necessary and sufficient for lipopolysaccharide avoidance. Lipopolysaccharides stimulate Drosila neurons in a TRPA-dependent manner and activate exogenous TRPA channels in human cells. The findings demonstrate flies detect bacterial endotoxins via a gustatory pathway through TRPA activation as a conserved molecular mechanism. TRPA mediates acute nocensive responses to lipopolaccharides in mice and avoidance of bitter and noxious compounds in Drosila. Blocking neurotransmission in neurons by expressing tetanus toxin abolished lipopolysaccharide avoidance, indicating flies detect lipopolyscharides through a gustatory mechanism. The avoidance of lipopolyscharide-contaminated food during feeding and oviposition may serve to prevent Gram-negative bacterial infections, potentially compensating for the lack of adaptive immunity in these animals. The sensory mechanism exploits TRPA, suggesting a broadly conserved principle where channels play a crucial role in lipopolysaccharide detection by sensory neurons in flies and mammals, regardless of the sensory modality involved. The data show lipopolysaccharide is avoided during feeding and oviposition and TR1 expression in bitter-sensing gustatory neurons is necessary and sufficient for lipysaccharide avoidance, serving to prevent infection with Gram-negative bacteria. Control animals preferred laying eggs on control food rather than on contaminated food with lipopolysaccharides, whereas TR1 mutants did not. Lipopolyscharides stimulate Drosila neurons in TR1-dependent manner and activate exogenous TR1 channels in human cells, demonstrating a conserved molecular mechanism of detection and avoidance of bacterial endotoxins via TR1 activation.
Researchers have identified a crucial role for the zinc finger protein Zfp335 in the formation of the naïve T cell compartment, a critical for immune function. A mouse mutant, bloto, with a hypomorphic mutation in Zfp335, exhibits a naïve T cell deficiency due to an intrinsic developmental defect that begins in the thymus and continues into the periphery, affecting cells that have recently undergone thymic egress. The effects of Zfpbloto are multigenic and cannot be attributed to altered thymic selection, proliferation, or Bcl-dependent survival. Zfp binds to promoter regions via a consensus motif, and its target genes are enriched in categories related to protein metabolism, mitochondrial function, and transcription regulation. Restoring expression of one target, Ankle2, partially rescues T maturation, indicating Zfp as a transcription factor essential for late-stage intrathym and post-thic cell maturation. Analyzing mice with a hypomorphic missense mutation in a C2 zinc finger of Zfp reveals a selective defect in accumulation of naïve cells resulting from maturation block in SP thymocytes and recent thym emigrants. Zfp regulates transcription by binding to promoters of target genes, with decreased occupancy at a subset altering gene expression in mutant thymocytes. Findings provide evidence Zfp functions as a transcription factor and key regulator of transcription program for T maturation. Ankle is a functional gene of Zfp, its dysregulation by ZfpR2W contributes to maturation defect in blt cells, shown by quantitative RT-PCR and western blot analysis. An ENU-induced allele of Zfp results in defective accumulation of naïve cells due to impaired maturation in SP thymocytes and recent thymic emigrants, independent of thym selection or effects on proliferation but associated with reduced viability. Focusing on genes with clear evidence of reduced Zfp occupancy and mRNA, Ankle is prominent, an an ankyrin repeat and LEM-containing protein. Zfp335 is required for formation of the naïve T compartment and essential regulator of late-stage intrath and post-thym cell maturation.
Researchers have identified a new factor, termed mRNA-ncRNA avoidance, that influences protein expression levels in bacteria and archaea, challenging the assumption that mRNA abundance directly correlates with protein abundance. Studies have shown that mRNA features such as codon usage and secondary structure contribute to discrepancies between mRNA and protein levels, but a new evidence suggests stochastic mRNA-nc interactions also play a significant role. By analyzing 97% of species, a reduced capacity for interaction between native RNAs relative to controls was found, indicating a strong selection for avoidance of stochastic mRNA-nc interactions across prokaryotes, which has a greater impact on protein abundance than mRNA structure or codon usage. Generating synonymous variant green fluorescent protein mRNAs with varying potential for mRNA-nc interactions demonstrated that GFP levels correlate with avoidance, enabling precise modulation of protein abundance. Measuring extrinsic avoidance using shuffled mRNAs as controls revealed stochastic mRNA-nc interactions are underrepresented in 73% of prokaryotic phyla, establishing a signature of selection that minimizes interactions. However, complete avoidance is combinatorially unlikely due to thousands of potential interacting RNA species, suggesting a tradeoff between avoidance and optimal expression. Results indicate avoidance explains variation in protein abundance as well as codon usage and internal mRNA structure, and crosstalk between mRNAs and ncRNAs impacts expression levels. Taking crosstalk into account may enable design of constructs where protein levels can be precisely controlled, predicting mAs with low avoidance produce fewer proteins per transcript than those with high avoidance. Meta-analysis of proteomics and transcriptomics data revealed statistically significant regions with high correlation coefficient throughout GFP mRNAs, supporting the relationship between mRNA-nc avoidance and protein levels. These findings provide evidence that stochastic mRNA-nc interactions can be harnessed to control protein expression in bacteria and archaea.
Amyotrophic lateral sclerosis (ALS) is a fatal neurodegenerative disease characterized by upper and lower motor neuron loss, with loss of neuromuscular junctions (NMJs) being a key pathological event in patients and animal models of the disease. Denervation of NMJs is observed prior to symptom onset and is considered an early event in ALS, however, it remains unclear whether it is the first manifestation of a globally degenerating motor neuron. Using in vivo imaging of single axons and NMJs over a three-month period in SOD1G37 mice, researchers identified that single motor-units are dismantled asynchronously. The dismantling of axonal branches is accompanied by contemporaneous new axonal sprouting resulting in synapse formation onto nearby NMJs, weeks prior to complete axonal degeneration. Denervation tends to propagate from the first lost NMJ, consistent with a contribution of neuromuscular factors extrinsic to neurons, with distal branches being more susceptible. These results show that NMJ denervation in ALS is a complex and dynamic process of continuous denervation and new innervation rather than a manifestation of sudden global neuron degeneration. Longitudinal in vivo analysis reveals previously unsuspected dynamism at the NM in an animal model of ALS, highlighting a temporal window between early branch-specific dismantling and global axon degeneration that could be therapeutically exploited. Local alterations of synaptic and molecular signaling take place early at the NM, suggesting local-specific events may play a key role in NM denervation. Rescue of neuronal loss in S1 mice does not necessarily improve NM denervation, indicating local branch-specific events contribute to NMJ denervation. The study used repeated in vivo imaging of motor neuron arbors and NMJs to assess the time and dynamics of structural changes during ALS progression, predicting a MU-dependent mechanism would result in more or less synchronous degeneration of the whole MU over a brief period, while a contribution of neuuscular changes would result in asynchronous loss of NMJs within a single MU. Early NMJ denervation is observed prior to significant MN or cell loss, but whether it arises from branch-specific degeneration or is globally degenerating neuron remains ill-defined. Overall, NMJ denervation in ALS is a complex process of continuous denervation and new innervation rather than a manifestation of global neuron degeneration.
Adjuvant tamoxifen therapy improves survival in breast cancer patients, but long-term treatment comes with side effects that impact health and quality of life, including hot flashes, changes in bone density, and fatigue. Research has been hindered by a lack of proven animal models, making it unclear which tissues and cells mediate these negative effects. Mice undergoing tamox treatment experience changes in temperature, bone, and movement. Single-cell RNA sequencing reveals tamox treatment induces widespread gene changes in the hypothalamus and preoptic area, dependent on estrogen receptor alpha. Conditional knockout of estrogen receptor alpha in the hypothus-PO ablates or reverses tamox-induced gene expression. Estrogen alpha-deficient mice do not exhibit tamox-induced changes in temperature, bone, or movement. Estrogen receptor expression in the hypothusPO is necessary for tamox-induced gene expression and effects on thermoregulation, bone, and movement. Tamox treatment modulates estrogen receptor signaling in the central system to alter fundamental aspects of physiology. Estrogen receptor mediates many aspects of physiology and estrogen conditional knockout affects transcriptomic effects of tamox. Loss of estrogen receptor affects physiological responses to tamox in mice, including body temperature, movement, and bone density. Estrogen beta is also expressed in hypothusPO but physical activity is primarily regulated by estrogen signaling. Tamox acts as estrogen antagonist in estrogen-sensitive regions to suppress physical activity. Similar to endogenous estrogens, tamox has effects on bone remodeling. The study defines cell types and mechanisms mediating undesirable effects of tamox therapy, demonstrating mice as a model to study tamox physiological effects, including increased heat, decreased movement, and bone density. The rodent model mimics key effects of endocrine therapy observed in humans, implicating estrogen signaling as mediator of tamox effects on thermoregulation, movement and homeostasis.
Interferon-stimulated gene 15 (ISG15) is a linear ubiquitin-like protein with known for its anti-viral activity, but its role during bacterial infections remains unclear. Recent research reveals that ISG15 expression in non-phagocytic cells is significantly induced upon Listeria monocytogenes infection. This induction can occur independently of type I interferon and relies on the cytosolic surveillance pathway, which senses bacterial DNA signals through STING, TBK1, IRF3, and IRF7. Notably, IS15 restricts Lister infection both in vitro and in vivo. Utilizing stable isotope labeling in tissue culture (SILAC), researchers identified ISGylated proteins responsible for the protective effect. Overexpression of IS15 leads to ISylation of endoplasmic retic reticulum (ER) and Golgi proteins, correlating with increased secretion of cytokines known to counteract infection. Collectively, the data reveal a previously uncharacterized IS15-dependent restriction of Lister infection, reinforcing IS15 as a key component of the innate immune response. In nonphocytic cells, IS15 is dramatically induced upon Lister infection in an interfer-independent manner. Lister-mediated IS15 induction depends on the cytosolic surveillance pathway sensing bacterial DNA through STING, TBK, IRF3, IRF7. IS15 counteracts Lister infection in vitro and in vivo. IS15 modification of ER and Golgi proteins increases secretion of cytokines known to counteract infection. IS15 can be induced by Lister in an interfer-independent manner in human nonphocytic cells. The role of IS15 in host defense against Lister requires ISylation. IS15 is a ubiquitin modifier covalently linked to cellular and viral substrates. Transcripts are concomitantly induced after 3 hours of infection with Lister, leading to the hypothesis that IS15 could be induced in interfer-independent during Lister infection. Utilizing viral protein B18 to block interferon receptor signaling, the hypothesis was tested. The protein acts similarly to a blocking antibody.
Single-molecule Förster Resonance energy transfer (smFRET) is a method for studying the structure and dynamics of biomolecules, but the development of high-throughput methodologies and instrumentation has outpaced the development of rapid and standardized analysis methods to objectively analyze the wealth of produced data. DeepFRET is an automated, open-source solution based on deep learning, requiring only human intervention in setting a user-adjustable quality threshold to transition from raw images to histograms of biomolecule behavior. Integrating standard features of smFRET analysis, DeepRET outputs common kinetic metrics with accuracy on ground truth data reaching over 95%, outperforming human operators and commonly used thresholds, requiring only 1% of the time. DeepFRET's precise and rapid operation demonstrates its capacity to objectively quantify biomolecular dynamics, contributing to benchmarking smFRET for structural biology.

DeepFRET is an all-inclusive software with a pre-trained deep neural network at its core for rapid, objective, and accurate assessment of smRET data, quantifying biomolecular dynamics with minimal human intervention, requiring only a threshold on data quality as an initial step to output detailed quantification of structural dynamics from images. The software operates without extensive preprocessing or empirically defined rules, offering reproducible and less opinionated evaluation of single-molecule data, and is significantly faster than human annotation for large datasets. DeepFRET recognizes patterns and learns features directly from raw data, allowing implementation of routines that do not require preprocessing, and comes as a free-to-use executable for Windows and Mac users, enabling end-users with limited programming skills to operate it.

DeepFRET's classification was achieved without preprocessing or post-inspection, illustrating its power to operate without human intervention, and potential to benchmark reproducibility of smFRET data methods for multiple biomolecular systems across laboratories. The discordance between ground truth and classified data was quantified and displayed using a confusion matrix, demonstrating DeepRET's accuracy. The development of DeepRET and similar approaches is expected to enable fully automated methodologies for biomolecular recognition, protein folding, and dynamics, and super-resolution, increasing the breadth and impact of single-molecule studies in structural biology.
The Polycomb repressive complex 1 (PRC1) is essential for epigenetic regulation of gene expression, and SCML2, a mammalian homolog of Drosophila SCM, is a Polycomb-group protein that associates with RC1. SCMLA, an SC2 isoform tightly associated with chromatin, contributes to RC1 localization and directly enforces repression of certain Polycomb target genes. SC2 binds RC1 via its SPM domain and interacts with ncRNAs through a novel RNA-binding region (R). Targeting of SC2 to chromatin involves coordinated action of MBT domains, RNA binding, and interaction with RC through the SPM domain. Deletion of R reduces occupancy of SC2 at target and overexpression of mutant SC2 lacking R causes defects in RC recruitment. SC2 appears to enforce repression directly, independent of RC recruitment, via mechanisms that remain to be elucidated. The identification of SC2 as Pc protein that binds RNA and contributes to repression of Polycomb targets sheds light on interplay between ncRN and epigen regulation and combinatorial factors that drive specificity of diverse Pc complexes. RNA binding plays role in SC function as required for localization to chromatin. Interaction of SCM and RC relies on C-terminal SPM domain conserved in humans. Data supports model where R contributes to localization of SC2 to chromatin, interacts with RC, and creates chromatin environment refractory to transcription. SC2 binds at least one Pc target and recognized methylated lysines by MB domain, RNA activity required to recruit or stabilize SC on chrom. SC retained at genes through interaction with RC and contributes stabilizing RC. SC recruited to target through RNA activity and presence of mutant SC2R not chrom-bound causes partial release of BMI and RC. SC binds RNA through R required for genome-wide targeting and recruitment of RC.
Regulation of translating ribosomes is a crucial component of gene expression control, and in Escherichia coli, ribosome stalling by the C-terminal sequence of SecM regulates the SecA-dependent pathway. Researchers have used cryo-EM to determine the structures of SecM-stalled ribosomes at 3.3-3.7 Å resolution, revealing two distinct stalling mechanisms at different elongation steps of the translation cycle. One mechanism involves inactivation of the ribosomal peptidyl-transfer center, inhibiting peptide bond formation with incoming proly-tRNA, while the other involves prolonged residence of peptidyl-RNA at the hybrid/P site, inhibiting full-scale tRNA translocation. To understand the molecular mechanism of Sec-dependent translational stalling, researchers purified Sec-stalled ribosome nascent chain complexes and analyzed their structures using single particle cryoEM, revealing two mechanisms of stalling and providing details on how SecM nascent peptide modulates translation in distinct steps. The analysis of ribosome-Sec interactions at near-atomic level shows interactions between SecM and the exit tunnel induce conformation changes of the peptidyl-transfer center, leading to translation arrest at distinct elongation steps, including peptide-bond formation and tRNA translocation. The position of tRNA-nascent linkage of Sec-tRNA is similar to that seen in the structure of the ribosome with active peptid-transfer center. Comparison with reference states of ribosomes capable or incapable of peptide-bond formation provides an alternative mechanism of Sec-induced stalling. The results demonstrate an elegant control of the translation cycle by regulatory peptides through continuous dynamic reshaping of the functional center of the ribosome. Two predominant forms of stalled ribosomes, post-state and hybrid state, indicate a collection of interactions between SecM and the tunnel cooperatively induce conformation changes of the peid-transfer center, leading to translation arrest at distinct elongation steps. Overall, the study reveals the molecular mechanism of Sec-dependent translational stalling at the atomic level, providing insights into how regulatory peptides modulate translation.
Researchers utilized a new generation Adaptive Optics Scanning Laser Ophthalmoscope to image cones at and near the fovea in 28 eyes of 16 subjects, providing the first measures of foveal cone density as a function of axial length in living eyes. The study computed cone density and other metrics in units of visual angle and linear retinal units, finding that the fove cone mosaic in longer eyes is expanded at the fove, but not proportionally to eye length. Despite retinal stretching resulting in decreased cones per square millimeter, myopes generally have higher angular density, measured in cones per degree squared, around the fove compared to emmetropes, offering potential for better visual acuity. The findings suggest that reports of deficits in best-corrected fove vision in myopes compared to emmetes cannot be explained by increased spacing between photoreceptors caused by retinal stretching during myopic progression. The study aimed to investigate how the length of the eye affects cone density and near the fove center, focusing on two types of cone densities. Previous studies implicated retinal stretching as a possible cause of myopic deficits, but the structural changes at the fove center during myopic progression were unknown. Advances in adaptive optics enable high-contrast images of the fove photoreceptor mosaic, allowing analysis of cone packing and eye length at the fove center, crucial for spatial vision. Myopes have higher fove photoreceptor resolution with potential for better performance on visual tasks compared to emmetes, ruling out increased separation between cones by retinal stretching as the simplest explanation for visual deficits. Large-scale studies show myopes attain reasonably good visual acuity with correction, indicating that retinal image enlargement does not exactly match cone mosaic stretching, allowing eyes of different lengths to sample the visual field similarly. The study's findings have implications for understanding the physical and visual implications of fove cone topography and dependence on eye length.
Streptococcus pneumoniae is a leading cause of invasive disease in infants, especially in low-income settings, and asymptomatic carriage in the nasopharynx is a prerequisite for disease. Researchers developed a model to calculate the duration of carriage episodes from longitudinal swab data and combined these results with whole genome sequence data, estimating pneumococcal variation accounted for 63% of the variation in carriage duration, while host traits such as age and previous carriage accounted for less than 5%. The study partitioned heritability into lineage and locus effects, finding serotype accounted for 17%, drug resistance 9%, and other locus effects 7% of the variation in carriage duration. A pan-genome-wide association study identified prophage sequences associated with decreased carriage duration independent of serotype, potentially by disrupting the competence mechanism. The findings support theoretical models of pneumoccal competition and antibiotic resistance. Genetic determinants of pneumoccal carriage, other than serotype, were previously unknown, but this study quantified and mapped the genetic contribution to carriage of S. pneumonia, showing variants in the pneumoccal genome alter duration, linked to antibiotic resistance. Sequence variation of pneumoccal genome explains 63% of variability, common serotypes and resistance to erythromycin 19%, and prophage presence 7%. The study provides a quantitative estimate of how transmission pairs share carriage duration, showing differences between and within serotypes. The implication of phage having a significant effect on duration has interesting corollaries on pneumcal genome diversification through frequent infection and loss of prophage during episodes. The genetics of S pneumonia is an important in determining carriage, and if environmental conditions are associated with streptoccal genotype, heritability may differ.
