A lower bound on heritability can be calculated by fitting a linear model through maximum likelihood to common SNPs.
The slow rectifier current, IKs, plays a crucial role in cardiac repolarization and is mediated by the tetrameric voltage-gated channel KCNQ1 and its β-subunit KCNE. The stoichiometry between α and β-subunits has been a topic of controversy, with studies suggesting either a strict 2:4 or variable ratio up to 4:4. Researchers used fusion proteins linking KCNE to one, two, or four KCN1 subunits to reproduce 4:4, 2:4, or 1:4 stoichiometries. Whole cell and single-channel recordings showed EQ and EQQQ have hyperpolarized activation, reduced conductance, and shorter latency compared to EQ, abolished by KC1 addition. Using UV-crosslink unnatural amino acid in KCNE, EQQ and EQQ crosslinking rates were slowed compared to KCN1, demonstrating no intrinsic limits to association of up to four βunits within the IK complex. KCN1 is a voltage-gated potassium channel expressed throughout the body and paired with KCNE has unique properties, particularly in myocardium and inner ear. Together, KCN1 and KCNE conduct the slow rectifier current, responsible for stabilizing repolarization and shortening action potential duration at high heart rates. The stoichiometry can be regulated based on availability, altering expression could change channel composition, shifting overall kinetics. Five KCNE genes can be expressed in cardiomyocytes. Fusion channels were evaluated in mammalian cells using whole patch clamp, single-channel recordings, and UV-crosslink unnatural acid approaches. The role of the ion complex in health and disease is significant, yet the stoichiometry of binding between KCN1 and KC1 remains unclear. Studies reported a ratio of two βunits to four αunits since identification of components almost 20 years ago. Flexibility in KCN1 β-subunit association allows a mechanism to modulate repolarization in the heart through changes in expression.
Cellular responses to injury are crucial for complete tissue regeneration, but the underlying processes remain incompletely understood. Research on zebrafish fin regeneration has shown that myeloid-defective mutants display apoptosis of regenerative cells during fin regeneration, which is induced by prolonged expression of interleukin-1 beta (Il-1β). Myeloid cells are considered the principal source of Il-1, but epithelial cells express Il-1 in response to tissue injury and initiate the inflammatory response. Macrophages are necessary for the resolution of inflammation and survival of regenerative cells. Il-1 plays an essential role in normal fin regeneration by regulating expression of regeneration-induced genes. The study reveals that proper levels of Il-1 signaling and tissue inflammation, tuned by macroages, play a crucial role in tissue regeneration. Il-1 signaling and inflammation act as a double-edged sword, required for regeneration but impairing tissue in excess. Tissue injury induces Il-1 expression in epithelial cells and macroages attenuate Il-1 expression and inflammation during tissue regeneration. The pro-inflammatory cytokine Il-1 is provided by epidermal cells in response to injury and apoptosis occurs if Il-1 action at the injury site is prolonged. Macrophages are responsible for attenuation of Il-1 expression and resolution of inflammation. Normal Il-1 expression and inflammatory response are necessary in tissue regeneration to activate expression of regeneration-induced genes. Excessive Il-1 signaling and inflammation in response to injury represent a cause of apoptosis in the mutant. Myoid cells, such as neutrophils and macroages, migrate to the injured fin and Il-1 signaling plays an indispensable role in tissue regeneration by activating expression of regeneration genes. A proper level of Il-1 expression and inflammatory response, tuned by macroages, are necessary for progression of tissue regeneration. The study elucidates the role of macroages in attenuating Il-1 expression and inflammation during tissue regeneration and highlights the function of Il-1 in tissue regeneration.
Researchers studied the dependence of protein mobility on surface properties and ionic strength in the cytoplasm of Escheria coli, Lactoccus lactis, and Haloax volcanii using surface-modified fluorescent proteins and determined their translational diffusion coefficients. The results show that in E. coli, the diffusion coefficient depends on the net charge and its distribution over the protein, with positive proteins diffusing up to 100-fold slower than negative ones. This effect is weaker in L. lactis and H. volcanii due to electrostatic screening. The decrease in mobility is caused by interaction of positive proteins with ribosomes as shown in vivo measurements and confirmed in vitro with purified ribosomes. The slowdown in diffusion with increasing positive surface charge in L. lactis and H. volcanii is less than in E. coli due to electrostatic screening, mainly caused by binding of positive proteins to ribosomes with KD values on the order of µM. The findings indicate that ribosome properties may limit the composition of the cytoplasmic proteome. Positive proteins not only diffuse slowly but their binding to ribosomes might inhibit protein synthesis by affecting the assembly or activity of ribosomes. Analysis of proteomes of E. coli, L. lactis, H. volcanii, and four endosymbiotic bacteria was conducted to determine how a protein will diffuse in light of the composition of the proteome and how ribosomes affect the diffusion of proteome constituents. Many processes in cells depend on interactions between macromolecules and their ability to find each other by translational diffusion, especially in prokaryotes due to the absence of active mechanisms of cytoplasm transport. The study reveals that macromolecules need to diffuse for cells to function and that ribosome properties may impose limits on the nature of the cytoplasmic proteome.
Regeneration is a widespread phenomenon across the animal kingdom, but the mechanisms that activate it remain poorly understood. Planarians are capable of whole-body regeneration and exhibit distinct molecular responses to wounds that result in tissue absence and those that do not. Researchers have identified a follistatin homolog, Smed-follatin, required for planarian regeneration, which blocks responses to tissue absence but does not prevent normal tissue turnover. Two activin homologs, Smed-activ-1 and S-activ-2, are required for the S-follatin phenotype. S-follatin is wound-induced and expressed at higher levels following injuries that cause tissue absence, suggesting it inhibits S-activ proteins to trigger regeneration specifically following injuries involving tissue absence. This identifies a critical mechanism for regeneration initiation, a process important across the animal kingdom. Animals distinguish between injuries involving varying amounts of tissue loss and regulate distinct wound programs remains unknown, with S-follatin required for molecular and cellular'missing-tissue' responses during regeneration. Follist-mediated inhibition of activin signaling is necessary for regeneration to occur, with S-foll expression controlled by the extent of tissue absence following injury. A gene encoding a homolog of the TGF-β inhibitor, follist, is required for regeneration and regeneration-specific cellular and molecular responses to injury. Inhibition of activin signaling by Fst is necessary for initiating a regenerative response at wounds following major injury, with fst wound-induced and persisting at high levels longer following major injury. TGF signaling plays a major role in many forms of regeneration, and uncovering'missing-tissue' signals in planarians will inform a broad understanding of core regenerative mechanisms. Planarians exhibit distinct cellular and molecular programs responding to simple injury versus missing tissue, mounting unique mitotic and apoptotic responses and producing an extended program of wound-induced gene expression following substantial tissue loss. Wound-induced fst expression allows regenerative responses to be initiated as a consequence of tissue absence, with fst the first gene known to be required for regeneration-specific responses in planarians. Not all missing-tissue responses are abolished following fst inhibition, with neoblast migration to amputation sites occurring normally in fst RNA animals despite the absence of a proliferative response.
Mismatch repair (MR)-deficient tumors exhibit a distinct molecular fingerprint characterized by persistent DNA replication errors that manifest as mutations, rendering them resistant to standard therapy. Whole-genome and whole-exome sequencing confirm a unique MR-deficient mutation signature, distinct from other tumors but similar to germline DNA, suggesting a substantial fraction of human genetic variation arises from mutations escaping MR. A set of recurrent indels may serve to detect microsatellite instability (MSI), and using endometrial tumors with immunohistochemically proven MR deficiency, a novel marker detects MSI with greater specificity and selectivity than standard tests. Recurrent indels are enriched for DNA double-strand break repair by homologous recombination, indicating reduced double-strand break repair in MR-deficient tumors and triggering sensitivity to double-strand break inducers. Whole-genome sequencing of 28 tumor-normal pairs, including 22 MR-deficient, revealed an average of 201 somatic events in MR-deficient tumors versus 39 in MR-proficient, a 52-fold increase. Validation rates for substitutions and indels were 87% and 86%. MR-deficient tumors accumulate indel mutations in mono- and dinucleotide repeats, a phenomenon referred to as microsatellite instability, leading to different prognosis and therapeutic outcomes after chemotherapy. MR machinery deficiency leads to DNA errors in tumor tissue, not normal tissue, and MR-deficient tumors exhibit distinct mutation spectra with clinical relevance for diagnostically assessing MSI and therapeutically targeting MR-deficient tumors.
Researchers have developed an optimized version of Magnets, a light-inducible dimerization module derived from the Neurospora crassa photoreceptor Vivid, which enables precise temporal and spatial control of biological processes in a non-invasive manner. The original Magnets require concatemerization and preincubation at 28°C to be functional, but the new enhanced Magnets (eMags) overcome these limitations. The eMags are engineered to have greater thermal stability, dimer efficiency, and faster association and dissociation kinetics, allowing them to rapidly and reversibly recruit proteins to subcellular organelles, induce organel contacts, and reconstitute OSBP-VAP-ER-Golgi tethering implicated in phosphatidylinositol-4-phosphate transport and metabolism. The eMags consist of two components, nMag (negative) and pMag (positive), which are small photoreceptors that require simultaneous photoactivation to interact, enabling high spatiotemporal confinement of dimerization with a single excitation wavelength. They exhibit fast association and dissociation kinetics and function when fused to a range of proteins, including peripheral and intrinsic membrane proteins. The eMags have been validated in various applications, including protein recruitment to different organelles, generation of organel contact sites, and reconstitution of VAP-dependent interorganel tethers that have key functions in lipid transport. The development of eMags involved optimizing the placement of charge-complement amino acids in the Vivid dimer interface using the crystal structure of the light-activated dimer as a guide and mitochondrial recruitment as a testbed. The eMags offer advantages over other systems, including rapid association and dissociation kinetics, low background activation, and suitability for optogenetic modulation of small volumes and subcellular organelles. They represent an effective tool to optogenetically manipulate physiological processes over whole cells or small subcell volumes, enabling precise control of biological processes in a non-invasive fashion.
Researchers investigated the signaling pathways triggered by ribosome stalling during translation, which has been linked to neurodegeneration, using a mouse model with a mutation in the Gbp2 gene, leading to stalled elongation complexes at AGA codons due to deficiencies in tRNA and GTP. Analysis revealed increased phosphorylation of eIFα and activation of ATF4, a key transcription factor in the integrated stress response pathway, prior to neurodegeneration. Genetic experiments showed this pathway was activated by eIF2 kinase GCN in an acylated tRNA-independent manner. GCN2 activation resulted in eIFα phosphorylation and induction of ATF-target genes in the cerebellum and hippocampus. Genetic studies demonstrated GC2 functions to attenuate neurogeneration, suggesting ribosome stalling activates GC2 as a regulatory mechanism linking elongation and initiation steps of translation. Failure to resolve stalled elongation complexes in mammalian neurons results in degeneration but the pathways evoked are unknown. GC2 activates the integrated stress response in the brain with stalled ribosomes, demonstrating a feedback loop between elongation defects and translation initiation. In the hippocampus of mice lacking GC2, degeneration of CA pyramidal cells was observed, suggesting the eIFα/ATF pathway is vital for neuron survival during ribosome stalling. ATF activation also occurs in brains with no unprocessed tRNA, raising the possibility ribosome stalling contributes to GC2 activation in conditions like amino deprivation. The mechanism for GC2 activation by ribosome stalling remains unclear. These results demonstrate interplay between translation elongation and initiation in regulating neuron survival during cellular stress and the importance of cellular context on the outcome of this pathway activation.
A comprehensive analysis of 147 population-based studies with measurements from over 18.6 million participants across 199 countries reveals significant trends in adult height over the past century. Being taller is associated with enhanced longevity, higher education, and earnings. The largest gains in adult height have occurred in South Korean women and Iranian men, who have become 20.2 cm and 16.5 cm taller, respectively. In contrast, there has been little change in adult height in some sub-Saharan countries and Asia over the past century. The tallest populations are men born in the Netherlands in the last quarter of the 20th century, with average heights surpassing 182.5 cm, while the shortest are women born in Guatemala in 1896 at 140.3 cm. The height differential between the tallest and shortest populations was 19-20 cm a century ago, remaining the same for women and increasing for men despite changes in country rankings.

Adult height has changed unevenly across the world's countries with no indication of convergence. The differential between the tallest and shortest populations was 19 cm for men and 20 cm for women a century ago, remaining the same for women and increasing for men. The largest gains since the 189 birth cohort occurred in South Korean and Iranian men, becoming 20.2 cm and 16.5 cm taller. Men in the Netherlands, Belgium, Estonia, and Denmark surpass average heights of 181 cm, with Dutch men at 182.5 cm, the tallest on the planet. The gap with the shortest countries, Timor-Leste, Yemen, and Laos, where men are 160 cm, is 22-23 cm, an increase of 4 cm on the global gap in the 189 birth cohort. Women in Norway, Iceland, the USA, and American Samoa were taller than 158 cm, while El Salvador, Peru, Bangladesh, Korea, and Japan had the shortest women. The tallest populations lived in Central and Northern Europe, North America, and Pacific islands, with Swedish and Norwegian men surpassing 171 cm, 18-19 cm taller than men in Laos. Swedish women had an average height of 160 cm, similar to a well-nourished 10-year-old girl.
The opioid overdose epidemic in the United States has quadrupled between 1999 and 2017, resulting in a staggering 130 deaths per day, necessitating innovative solutions to uncover the key brain areas and cell types mediating opioid-induced respiratory depression. Research has identified two primary changes to murine breathing after administering opioids, implicating the brainstem's breathing circuitry, confirmed by locally eliminating the µ-opioid receptor. The preBötz Complex, where breathing originates, is a critical site, with 70-140 neurons responsible for sensitivity to opioids. Characterization of these neurons may lead to novel therapies preventing respiratory depression while sparing analgesia. Approximately 140 Oprm1 glutamatergic preöt neurons mediate this effect, with 70 sufficient to rescue opioid depression of the preöt rhythm. Db1 and Foxp2 neurons are crucial in respiratory generation, suggesting opioids act by silencing presynaptic release or targeting a subset of preöt excitatory neurons generating the inspiratory rhythm. Opr1 is expressed in the central and peripheral nervous systems, including sites modulating breathing such as the cerebral cortex, brainstem control centers, and oxygen afferents. Two sites mediate the depressive effects of opioids on breathing, with one dominant driven by 140 neurons, not required for opioid-induced analgesia, suggesting a target for safer opioids or rescue strategies. Deletion of µ-opioid receptor from both sites eliminates opioid-induced respiratory depression, even with high doses of fentanyl, indicating targeting these sites rescues respiratory depression. Remaining effects are attributed to incomplete transduction or minor contributing sites.
Human hepatitis B virus (HBV) infection and HBV-related diseases remain a major public health problem, with individuals coinfected with satellite hepatitis D virus (HDV) experiencing more severe disease. The cellular entry of both viruses is mediated by HBV envelope proteins, specifically the pre-S domain of the large protein, which is a key determinant for receptor binding. Researchers have identified sodium taurocholate cotransporting polypeptide (NTCP), a multiple transmembrane transporter predominantly expressed in the liver, as the receptor for HBV and HDV. Using near distance photo-crossing and tandem purification, the receptor-binding region of pre1 specifically interacts with NT. Silencing NTCP inhibited HBV and HDV infection, while exogenous NTCP expression rendered nonsusceptible hepatarcinoma cells susceptible to viral infections. Replacing amino acids 157-165 of nonfunctional monkey NT with the human counterpart conferred its ability to support both infections, demonstrating NT is a functional receptor for HBV and HD. The pre-S domain mediates viral interaction with cellular receptors on hepatocytes, and an N-terminal myristoylated peptide corresponding to amino acids 2-48 of pre1 blocks HBV and HDV infections through an unknown component, likely a receptor. Photoreactive peptide ligands and antibodies identified pre1 binding partners by zero crosslinking, showing residues 157-165 of NT are crucial for binding the receptor region of pre1, contributing to NT-mediated HB and HDV infections. HepG2 cells expressing hNT with monkey-like mutations abolished HBV, while NT-bearing human residues restored infection, confirming NTCP's role in HBV and HD infections.

Long Interspersed Element-1 (LINE-1 or L1) retrotransposition poses a mutagenic threat to human genomes, and human cells have evolved strategies to regulate L1 retroposition. The APOEC3 (A3) family of enzymes catalyze deamination of cytidine nucleotides to uridine nucleotides in single-strand DNA substrates, with APOEC3A (A3) being the most potent inhibitor of L1 retroposition in cultured cell assays. However, previous characterization of L1 retroposition events generated in the presence of A3 did not yield evidence of deamination, leaving the molecular mechanism by which A3 inhibits L1 retroposition enigmatic. This study demonstrates that A3 can inhibit L1 retroposition by deaminating transiently exposed single-strand DNA that arises during the process of L1 integration. Engineered L retroposition events from human cells coexpressing A3 and uracil glycosylase inhibitor contain strand-specific A3-mediated C-to-U deamination, indicating A3 can inhibit L1 retroposition by deaminating transiently exposed single-strand DNA. Overexpression of wild-type APOEC3 cytidine deamin protein, but not deamin-defective mutants, potently inhibits L1 retroposition in human cells, yet deamination events were not detected in L1 retroposition generated in the presence of wild-type A3. An in vitro assay, L Element Amplification Protocol, was used to elucidate the mechanism for A3-mediated inhibition of L1 retroposition. Degradation of deaminated retrotrans intermediates could reduce L retroposition levels and eliminate evidence of A-mediated deamination, explaining why previous studies failed to detect A-mediated deamination. Deamination of single-strand genomic DNA leads to inexact TSD in post-integration, and UGI alleviates A3-mediated L inhibition. The study provides a mechanistic explanation of how the A3 cytid deamin protein inhibits L retroposition.
Researchers investigated the role of the activity-regulated non-canonical Ras-like GTPase Rem2 in visual circuit plasticity, demonstrating that Rem2-/- mice fail to exhibit normal ocular dominance during the critical period. At the cellular level, Rem2 has a cell-autonomous role in regulating intrinsic excitability of layer 2/3 pyramidal neurons prior to changes in synaptic function. Both in vitro and in vivo recordings reveal increased spontaneous firing rates in the absence of Rem2. Rem2 functions at the nexus of a signaling network that senses and responds to changes in neuronal activity to preserve circuit function in the face of changing sensory experience. Defining Rem2-dependent mechanisms will elucidate the molecular mechanisms that instruct activity-dependent modifications of neuralry. Rem2 may regulate the responsiveness of the cortex to visual stimulation through neuronal excitability or synaptic scaling. Findings indicate Rem2 functions as a regulator of experience-dependent plasticity at morphological, cellular, and circuit levels, connecting molecular regulators of morphology with broader function. Rem2 expression is regulated by visual experience and plays a role in activity-dependent processes in the visual cortex following sensory deprivation by investigating ocular dominance. The activity-regulated Rem2 is a candidate to link circuit activity with functional plasticity, a member of Rad/Rem/Gem/Kir family, Ras-related small GTP, whose expression and function are regulated by neuronal activity in response to calcium influx through voltage-gated channels. Data establish Rem2 as key molecule regulating neuronal excitability and circuit function in context of changing experience. Rem2 stabilizes intrinsic excitability and spontaneous firing in visual circuits.
Recent reports have suggested that magnetic fields can affect biological molecules and cells, including claims of a protein acting as a compass to guide magnetic orientation in animals and magnetic control of membrane conductance by attaching ferritin to an ion channel. However, these claims conflict with basic laws of physics, with discrepancies of 5 to 10 orders of magnitude, indicating that if the phenomena occur, they must have entirely different causes than proposed. The paramagnetic nature of protein complexes limits their utility for engineering magnetically sensitive cells. The forces or torques produced are too small for desired effects on molecular orientation or membrane, suggesting alternative mechanisms if phenomena occur. Proposed routes to magnetogenetics, based on pulling or heating ferritin with magnetic fields, are unlikely to succeed without new discoveries about biological matter structure. 

A molecular system was designed to couple magnetic fields to ionic current across cell membranes using a putative mechano-sensitive cation channel fused with ferritin, aiming to enable magnetic torque to open the channel and depolarize cells. Experimental results suggested neural activity modulation by static fields, but evidence is limited, with only 18 of 200 cells responding. The biophysical mechanism is unclear, with ferritin being a large protein complex forming a spherical shell around 12 nm in diameter. There is renewed interest in effects of magnetic fields on cells, including magnetosensation, where organisms sense Earth's magnetic field for navigation, and magnetogenetics, controlling cell activity with magnetic fields. Magnetosensation may rely on magnetite or magnetic field acting on single molecules in biochemical reactions, but biophysical basis remains unresolved.
Researchers have made a significant discovery about the role of the Prospero factor in Drosophila neural progenitors, also known as neuroblasts, which are used as a model for studying self-renewal and differentiation. When neuroblasts enter quiescence, Prospero is transiently detected in the neuroblast nucleus at low levels, followed by the establishment of a unique molecular profile lacking progenitor and differentiation markers. The pulse of low nuclear Prospero precedes entry into neuroblast quies even when the timing of quies is advanced or delayed by changing temporal factors. Loss of Prospero prevents entry into quies, whereas a pulse of low nuclear Prospero drives proliferating larval neuroblasts into quies. Prospero levels distinguish three progenitor fates: absent for self-renew, low for quies, and high for differentiation. Drosila neuroblasts have served as a system for identifying conserved pathways regulating cell proliferation and quies. Nearly all quiescent neuroblasts had reactivated proliferation and re-expressed progenitor markers after induction. Precocious low nuclear Prospero advances the timing of neuroblast quies. Transient low-level nuclear Prospero is sufficient to induce neuroblast quies. The timing of nuclear Prospero correlates with neuroblast entry into quies. Loss of Prospero delays the timing of neuroblast quies, shown by quantifying Ed+ neuroblasts in prosper null embryos. The pulse of nuclear Prospero matches the timing of neuroblast quies, occurring earlier in pdm mutants and later in cas mutants. A pulse of low nuclear Prospero marks neuroblast entry quies, suggesting a functional relationship.
Researchers have developed a single-particle fluorescence microscopy method to study the real-time uncoating kinetics of authentic HIV-1 capsids in vitro after permeabilizing the viral membrane. The uncoating of the metastable HIV-1 capsid is a tightly regulated disassembly process required for release of the viral DNA prior to nuclear import. The study reveals that opening of the first defect in the lattice is the rate-limiting step of uncoating, which is followed by rapid, catastrophic collapse. The caps-binding inhibitor PF74 accelerates capsid opening but stabilizes the remaining lattice, while binding of a polyanion to a conserved arginine in the lattice delays initiation of uncoating but does not prevent subsequent lattice disassembly. Different stages of uncoating can be controlled independently and the interplay between different capsid regulators determines the overall unco kinetics. Single-particle unco kinetics measurement of authentic capsids after removing the protective barrier of the viral membrane allowed observation of the effects of caps-binding molecules on discrete steps in the caps unco process. Ensemble measurements would have obscured the order of events due to dephasing. Caps-binding molecules exhibit differential effects on steps controlling initiation and continuation of unco. The structural heterogeneity of the HIVid is well described, with assembly yielding conical, tubular and other shapes containing structural defects such as gaps in the CA lattice. The single-molecule imaging assay follows unco kinetics of hundreds of capsids, resolving intermediates in disassembly averaged in traditional assays, classifying caps according to unco behaviors. Characteristics of HIV-1 caps unco and interactions with capsid molecules include caps integrity and stability being highly heterogeneous, majority containing defects and uncoating after membrane removal, and in absence of binders unco is all-or-none process after first defect creation. The half-life of HIVid is long to use permeabilized particles as a platform to quantify labeled protein binding to authentic caps at single-particle level, demonstrated by adding labeled CA and following fluorescence signals using dual-color TIR microscopy.
Breast cancer is the most commonly diagnosed malignancy in women, and analysis of breast cancer DNA indicates frequent loss-of-function mutations in components of the cJ NH-terminal kinase (JNK) signaling pathway. Despite JNK signaling promoting cell proliferation by activating the AP transcription factor, its association with reduced tumor development was unexpected. This tumor suppressive function was not mediated by a role of JNK in growth of established tumors, but by a requirement of JNK to prevent initiation. Data identify J pathway defects as driver mutations that promote genome instability and tumor initiation. Researchers have identified peptidylglycine α-amidating monooxygenase (PAM), an enzyme required for generating amidated bioactive peptides, as a crucial factor in ciliogenesis in Chlamydomonas and mammalian cells. AM is necessary for the normal assembly of motile and primary cilia in Chlamdomonas, planaria, and mice. In Chlamydomonas, AM knockdown lines failed to assemble cilia beyond the transition zone, had abnormal Golgi architecture, and altered levels of cilia components. Decreased AM expression reduced motile ciliary density on the ventral surface of planaria, resulting in cytosolic axonemes lacking a ciliary membrane. Primary cilia on neuroepithelial cells in Pam-/- mouse embryos had aberrant architecture. Data suggests AM activity and alterations in post-Golgi trafficking contribute to observed cili defects, providing a conserved link between AM, amidation, and ciliary assembly. AM plays a conserved role during early steps of ciliogenesis, revealing a novel link between amidation and cilium assembly in multiple cell types. AM may have been included in signaling proteins trafficked to ciliary compartment from Golgi in ancestral eukaryote. Results indicate both amidation and membrane roles of AM contribute ciliogenesis. Key steps in ciliogenic pathways may exhibit sensitivity to lack of AM in distinct organisms and tissues. Reduction of AM in C. reinhardtii and planaria resulted in ciliary assembly defects, while complete loss in mice did not abolish primary cilia in neuroepithelial cells, although they were shorter and often only ciliary stubs were evident. The functionality of these short cilia is unknown. Overall, AM is an evolutionarily conserved factor required for ciliogenesis in multiple cell types, including motile cilia in C. reinhardtii, ventral epithelium planaria, and primary cilia neuroepithelial cells in mammals.

Voltage-gated calcium channels play a crucial role in controlling key functions of excitable cells, including synaptic transmission in neurons and contraction of heart and skeletal muscles, by activating at different voltages with distinct kinetics. To understand the molecular mechanisms governing these specific voltage properties, researchers combined structure modeling, mutagenesis, and electrophysiology to analyze the structures, free energy, and transition of activated and resting states of two functionally distinct voltage-sensing domains of the eukaryotic calcium channel CaV1.1. Both voltage-sensing domains displayed typical features of the sliding helix model but greatly differed in ion-pair formation of the outer gating charges. Stabilization of the activated state enhanced voltage dependence of activation, while stabilization of resting states slowed kinetics, providing a mechanistic model explaining how ion-pair formation in separate voltage-sensing domains can realize characteristic properties of voltage-gated cation channels. 

Voltage-gated calcium channels translate membrane depolarization into calcium influx, contributing to cellular excitability and coupling electrical activity to fundamental cell functions such as contraction, neurotransmitter and hormone secretion, and gene regulation. Together with voltage-gated sodium channels, they form a structurally related ion channel superfamily with fourfold symmetry. Advanced models of activated and resting states of CaV1.1 voltage-sensing domains fill an important gap in understanding voltage-sensing mechanisms in eukotic voltage-gated cation channels. All interaction partners suggested by the model showed predicted effects on channel gating when experimentally tested by mutagenesis and electrophysiology, and alterations of kinetic properties recognized experimentally were reliably explained by the computer model. The structures of lowest energy of Ca1.1 voltage-sensing domains display basic features of the sliding helix model based on cryo-EM structure of Ca1.1 and generated using Rosetta computational software to build a homology model of human eukary CaV1. The resting states of eukary Ca and Na channels remained inaccessible to experimental determination but years of work and modeling provide support the sliding helix model of voltage sensor action. Researchers used mutagenesis and electrophysiology to analyze structures, free energy, and transition of activated and resting states of functionally distinct voltage-sensing domains of eukary calcium channel CaV1 to identify molecular mechanisms governing specific voltage properties.
The ability of the adult brain to undergo changes is of particular interest in medicine, especially regarding recovery from injuries or improving cognition. Matrix metalloproteinases have been associated with juvenile-dependent visual cortex plasticity, yet little is known about their role in this process in the adult visual cortex. Activation of matrix metalloproteinases is crucial for facilitating changes in a healthy brain, however, upon brain injury upregulated metalloproteinases promote the spread of a lesion and impair recovery. Researchers examined the effects of metalloprotein inhibition on experience-induced plasticity in healthy and stroke-affected mice. In healthy animals, a 7-day application of metalloprotein inhibitor prevented plasticity, while treatment with metallo inhibitor once but not twice following stroke rescued plasticity normally lost under these conditions. Data imply an optimal metalloprotein activity level is crucial for adult plasticity to occur. Metalloproteinases play a critical role in visual plasticity, suggesting upregulation after cortical lesion compromises experience-induced plasticity, which can be rescued by brief inhibition. Precise regulation of metalloprotein activity is essential for facilitating plasticity in the brain, offering new opportunities in treatment and recovery after stroke. A single treatment after stroke rescued plasticity in paradigms, whereas treatment twice diminished the effect, revealing metalloprotein's role in adult plasticity. Metalprotein activity must be within a narrow window for experience-induced plasticity to occur. The study objective was to examine if metalloprotein is crucial for adult plasticity and if inhibition after stroke may rescue lost plasticity. A combination of in vivo imaging and behavioral tests revealed an optimal metallo level is essential for adult plasticity in healthy and stroke-affected brain. Data demonstrate metallo's vital role for adult cortex plasticity, expanding previous studies from juvenile rats. Under normal conditions, metallo supports development and function, yet a different outcome has been described for diseased brain. Increased metallo was observed within hours of photothrombotically induced stroke and application partially rescued impaired cortex plasticity. Researchers tested if stroke-induced impairment of cortical plasticity can be rescued by inhibiting metal after stroke.
Signal transduction at vertebrate excitatory synapses involves the activation of AMPA receptors, glutamate-gated ion channels composed of four subunits assembled as dimer-of-dimers. Recent advances in cryo-electron microscopy have provided full-length structures of AMPA receptors, alone and in combination with auxiliary subunits, indicating dimers may undergo substantial lateral motions during gating, opening the extracellular layer along the central twofold symmetry axis. Bifunctional methanethiosulfonate cross-linkers were used to calibrate conformations in functional AMPA receptors in the presence and absence of auxiliary subunit Stargaz, revealing the extracellular layer of AMP receptors can get trapped in stable, opened conformations, especially upon long exposures to glutamate. However, Stargin limits conformation flexibility, suggesting under synaptic conditions where brief glutamate exposures and auxiliary proteins dominate, extracellular domains of AMPA likely stay compact during gating. The extracellular domains of synaptic AMPA are unlikely to undergo large structural rearrangements during transmission and work in a compact conformation regime unless faced with long exposures to glutamate in pathological conditions. The presence of auxiliary subunits, such as Stargin, affects conformation flexibility of AMP receptors. Cryo-EM structures of AMPA in complex with auxiliary subunits have been acquired in resting, active, and desensitized states. AMP receptors are tetrameric ligand-gated channels consisting of three layers from distinct domains, with extracellular layers assembling from amino-terminal and ligand-binding domains adopting dimer pairs in resting and active receptors. Assuming the length of bis-MTS cross-linkers reflects structural rearrangements, AMP receptors at synapses exposed to brief glutamate and complex with auxiliary subunits are unlikely to undergo extreme conformation changes. Technical advances in cry-electron microscopy revolutionized the study of membrane proteins, providing a greater supply of structural information, emphasizing the need to relate geometry to dynamics, particularly in the case of AMP receptors with full-length structures in complexes with ligands and auxiliary subunits published.
Researchers have overcome the challenge of isolating single cells from helminth-infected murine intestines, which has been hindered by strong anti-parasite type 2 immune responses that drive mucus production, tissue remodeling, and immune cell infiltration. By optimizing a standard intestinal digestion protocol, millions of immune cells were successfully isolated from the heavily infected duodenum. To validate the accuracy of these cells in representing intestinal responses, a 23-color spectral flow cytometry panel was used, incorporating surface and intracellular markers for type 2 immune responses associated with helmin infections, and findings were confirmed by confocal microscopy. The cell isolation protocol and high-dimensional analysis identified hallmarks of anti-parasite immune responses throughout the course of helmin infection, accelerating single-cell discoveries of local helmin immune responses previously unfeasible. Characterization of isolated cells using spectral cytometry validated the protocol's suitability for single-cell analysis and immunophenotyping. Hallmarks of innate and adaptive-parasite immune responses were characterized throughout the course of helmin infection, validated by confocal microscopy. Observed changes in cell ratios between naïve and infected samples were faithfully reported by both approaches, highlighting the requirement for good cell isolation techniques to investigate intestinal responses against helmin directly. Quantification of specific cell populations, including B220+, Siglec+, CD3+ CD4+, CD+4, and CD64+ cells, using confocal microscopy and spectral flow cytometry confirmed the accuracy of the cell isolation protocol in representing intestinal responses. Difficulties with intestinal digestion during helmin infection have been associated with anti-parite type 2 immune responses driving mucus production, altering epithelium, inducing cell infiltration, and tissue remodeling. Proteasomal protein degradation plays a crucial role in determining protein half-life and is essential for various cellular processes, including metabolism and immunological functions. Despite its importance, the mechanisms regulating proteasome activity are not fully understood. Researchers employed an integrated experimental and modeling approach to develop and validate mechanistic models of proteasomal peptide hydrolysis dynamics. The 20S proteasome is a dynamic enzyme with varying activity over time due to interactions between substrates, products, and proteolytic and regulatory sites. The locations of these sites and interactions are predicted by the model and experimentally supported, suggesting the rate-limiting step of hydrolysis is substrate transport into the proteasome, which varies between standard and immuno-proteomes, affecting degradation and cleavage usage. 

The study combined experimental and computational analyses to elaborate a comprehensive model describing regulatory components of 20S proteomes, interactions between substrate transport, and hydrolysis over time for representative peptides. A dearth of mechanistic analyses of proteome functions and complexity of polypeptide hydrosis precluded detailed analysis. Insights come from short fluorogenic peptides with a single proteas-catalyzed cleavage site, allowing real-time kinetics follow-up. Similar proteasome dynamics were observed using purified 20S proteas and protein homogenates, suggesting the model describes core proteasome features reflecting dynamics in complex cellular environments. Poly-ubiquitin tagging, transport, and peptide-bond hydrolysis regulate protein-life, affecting metabolic processes, yet mechanisms of proteomal action, particularly differences in substrate proteolysis between proteome isoforms, are incompletely understood.

The 20S proteome's catalytic activity varies over time, influenced by structural characteristics and interactions with substrates and products at proteolytic and regulatory sites. Kinetics change over longer scales than structural dynamics and are influenced by these interactions. The study sheds light on proteasome mechanisms using designed assays and modeling, describing regulatory components, substrate transport, and hydrolysis. The model predicts locations of proteolytic and regulatory sites and their interactions, experimentally supported, indicating transport as the rate-limiting step. Efficiency varies between standard and immuno-proteomes impacting total degradation and cleavage usage. The findings advance understanding of proteome structure, function, and dynamics, providing insights into mechanisms regulating proteas activity and differences between proteome isoforms.
The recycling of neurotransmitters is essential for sustained synaptic transmission in Drosophila, where histamine recycling is required for visual transmission. Synaptic histamine is rapidly taken up by laminar glia and converted to carcinine, which is then delivered back to photoreceptors where it is hydrolyzed to release histamine and β-alanine. While histamine is repackaged into synaptic vesicles, the return of β-alanine to laminial cells was unclear. Researchers identified a new β-alanine transporter, Bala, which is expressed and required in retinal cells for maintaining synaptic transmission and phototaxis. Null Bala mutants exhibited lower β-alanine levels and reduced β-alanine accumulation in the retina, suggesting Bala-dependent β-alanine trafficking is required for histamine homeostasis and neurotransmission. Disrupting histamine and carcinine synthesis abolishes photoreceptor synaptic transmission and β-alanine recycling is essential for maintaining histamine homeostasis and photoreceptor synaptic transmission. Establishing and maintaining a β-alanine pool plays a key role in sustaining synaptic transmission. The majority of pigment granules which reduce light exposure are formed in retinal cells of the Dophila compound eye. High β-alanine levels detected in retinal cells suggest pigment cells are critical for β-alanine transport and storage. Mutations in Bala disrupted β-alanine transport and storage in the retina, resulting in reduced photoreceptor synaptic activity and visual behavior. An efficient β-alanine recycling system compensates for rapid β-alanine loss during histamine to carcinine conversion. The current work provides evidence for a complete histamine recycling pathway critical for photoreceptor synaptic transmission where carcinine is synthesized in epithelial glial cells and transported to photoreceptors via the synaptic cleft. The findings suggest a conserved function for β-alanine trafficking in mammals and Dophila and provide genetic evidence retinal cells play a critical role in neurotransmission.
Researchers have made a significant discovery regarding the role of oxidants in aging, finding that chronologically aging yeast cells undergo a sudden redox collapse affecting over 80% of identified thiol-containing proteins. This redox collapse is not triggered by an increase in endogenous oxidants as previously thought, but rather by a substantial drop in cellular NAD, which maintains redox homeostasis. The decrease in NAD levels occurs early in lifespan and sets off a cascade downregulating most cellular processes. Caloric restriction increases NAD levels and delays this cascade. The study reveals a timeline of events leading to system-wide oxidation of the proteome before cell death. Chronological aging represents a stress condition utilizing redox capacity of the thiol proteome, potentially contributing to beneficial downregulation of cellular processes. Early in the aging process, yeast cells undergo an abrupt loss of redox homeostasis, indicated by massive oxidation of thiol-containing cytosolic, nuclear, and mitochondrial proteins. The decrease in NAD and increase in redox potential may not be specific to yeast, but shared with other aging organisms. Yeast undergo the same pattern of NAD decrease, T1 oxidation, and redox collapse when cultivated in 2% glucose. The study used chronologically aging S cerevisiae to determine the onset, extent, and targets of protein oxidation in postmitotic cells, discovering a global redox collapse leading to thiol oxidation in almost 80% of proteins several days prior to death. The findings suggest a correlation between onset and extent of oxidative stress and lifespan. The chronological lifespan of S cerevis represents a model for analyzing aging in postmitotic cells, allowing for uncovering protein targets whose oxidative thiol modifications contribute to physiological alterations in aging and establishing a correlation between oxidative stress and lifespan. The study provides evidence that caloric restriction, a universal lifespan-extending measure, increases NAD levels and delays the cascade of events leading to redox collapse. Overall, the study sheds light on the timeline of redox events in aging postmitotic cells and the role of NAD in maintaining redox homeostasis.
The biological macromolecular world is homochiral and the enforcement of this homochirality is essential for cell survival. A study presents the mechanistic basis of a configuration-specific enzyme, D-aminoacyl-tRNA deacylase, that selectively removes D-amino acids erroneously coupled to tRNAs. The crystal structure of dimer D-aminoacyl-tRNA deacylase from Plasmodium falciparum in complex with a substrate-mimicking analog shows it uses an invariant cross-subunit Gly-Pro dipeptide to capture the chiral centre of incoming D-amino-tRNA. No protein residues are directly involved in catalysis, the unique side chain-independent mode of substrate recognition provides an explanation for the enzyme's ability to act on multiple D-amino acids. The chiral specificity explains how the enriched pool of L-amino-tRNAs escapes this proofreading step. The study provides insights into a fundamental enantioselective process and elucidates a chiral mechanism with a role in preventing D-amino acid infiltration during the evolution of translational apparatus.

D-amino-tRNA deacylase removes D-amino acids mischarged tRNAs and ensures D-amino acids do not get incorporated into proteins. The enzyme acts as freestanding modules, likely operating through resampling by recapturing aminoacyl-tRNAs from EF-Tu. The amino acid site captures all atoms attached to the chiral Cα, with crossunit interactions, particularly a Gly-Pro motif from monomers inserted into the active site of the dimer, playing a central role in recognition. The aminoacyl moiety interacts with residues from both monomers. Considering the actual substrate, D-amino acid linked to tRNA, multiple binding modes are improbable due to the fixed orientation and position of D-amino acid by terminal adenosine binding.

A side-chain-free mechanism provides the basis for nature's design of a single deacylase to deal with D-tRNA, revealing the role of weak bonds in chirality. If L-amino was to bind, it would do so in one of three possible conformations. The study provides insights into enantioselective mechanisms in homochirality enforcement, specifically decoupling D-amino acids from tRNAs. Earlier structural studies on D-amino-tRNA deacylase provided a model based on docking or structures with non-cognate ligands. The reported structures of proofreading domains with substrate-mimicking analogs elucidate the mechanism of L-amino acid rejection from the active site.
The cystic fibrosis transmembrane conductance regulator (CFTR) is a chloride channel that belongs to the superfamily of ATP-binding cassette (ABC) proteins, where ATP-induced dimerization of two cytosolic nucleotide-binding domains (NBDs) opens the pore and dimer disruption following ATP hydrolysis closes it. Spontaneous openings without ATP are rare in wild-type CFTR, but in certain mutants constitute the only gating mechanism stimulated by ivacaftor, a clinically approved CFTR potentiator. Research has correlated energetic coupling between residues across the dimer interface with spontaneous pore opening/closure in single CF channels, showing that spontaneous openings are strictly coupled to NBD dimerization, which can occur even without ATP. Coordinated NBD/pore movements are intrinsic to CFTR, with ATP altering stability but not the fundamental architecture of open and closed conformations. This explains the effects of phosphorylation, mutations, and drugs on ATP-driven and spontaneous activity, providing insights into CF mutation and drug mechanisms. Phosphorylation of the cytosolic regulatory domain by cAMP-dependent kinase gates CF channels by cytosolic ATP. Strict coupling between NBD and pore movements constrains mechanistic models of ATP-dependent gating, understanding driving forces conserved among ABC proteins, and explains modulatory effects of mutations or physiological regulators. The ligand binding site is occluded in open channels, preventing open-state affinity definition, and disruption of ATP hydrolysis forces CF channels to gate at equilibrium following a reversible sequence rather than a cycle subject to the principle of detailed balance. Low-probability ATP-independent pore openings of a CF construct lacking N2 can be linked to NBD dimer formation, but results argue pore opening remaining NBD-TMD machinery undergoes similar movements to NBD dimerization.
Researchers have identified a novel mechanism of assembly and mobility for transmembrane proteins in lipid bilayers, referred to as the "orderphobic effect", which is a pre-transition or pre-melting effect at the first-order transition between ordered and disordered phases in the membrane. Large-scale molecular simulations demonstrate that a protein with hydrophobic thickness equal to that of the disordered phase embedded in an ordered bilayer stabilizes a microscopic order-disorder interface with finite stiffness. When two such proteins approach each other, they assemble due to reduced interfacial energy. The orderobic effect is mediated by proximity to the order-disorder phase transition and the size and hydrophobic mismatch of the protein. The strength and range of forces arising from this effect are significantly larger than those from membrane elasticity. The orderobic effect leverages the power of a phase transition, depending on the ability of the protein to induce a disordered layer in its vicinity, influenced by proximity to the membrane's phase and protein's radius and hydrophobic mismatch. For instance, a transmembrane protein with hydroobic thickness of approximately 2.6 nm favors the structure of the disordered phase and can melt the ordered phase near the protein, resulting in the formation of an order-disorder interface. Enhanced lipid mobility surrounding orderobic proteins may be experimentally tested by single molecule techniques. Biological membranes and transmembrane proteins are more complex, with multiple components allowing more than one order-disorder transition. A dual to the orderobic effect exists, where a transmembrane protein in the disordered phase favors the ordered can nucleate an ordered region and orderdisorder interface. Interfaces separating ordered and disordered regions provide a force for assembly, influenced by hydroobic thickness and area per lipid differences between phases.
Genetically encoded calcium indicators are widely used to investigate neural dynamics, but the relationship between fluorescence signals and action potentials remains unclear. Researchers characterized the action potential-fluorescence transfer function in 48 layer 2/3 pyramidal neurons in primary visual cortex with simultaneous calcium imaging and cell-attached recordings from transgenic mice expressing GCaMPs or GCaMPf. Under optimal conditions, most action potentials can be detected when the soma fills the microscope's field of view, but under typical population imaging conditions, only a minority of action potentials are detected, often less than 10% and 20% for single and double action potentials respectively. This emphasizes the limits of detection under realistic conditions. To construct action-calcium response curves, isolated action potential events were identified and summed over 250 milliseconds. For GCaMPs, isolated events were separated from previous and subsequent events by 100 and 500 milliseconds, and for GCaMPf by 300 milliseconds. Results show that only a minority of action potentials were used to construct action-calcium response curves. The study tested performance of several spike models detecting action potentials under optimal and less optimal conditions, providing insight into the relationship between spiking activity in vivo and fluorescence signals. Genetically encoded calcium indicators are used with two-photon scanning microscopy to report neuronal activity within populations in vivo, enabling measurement from hundreds of neurons at single-cell resolution over multiple sessions. Previous studies established most action potentials can be detected with GCa6 indicators under near-optimal conditions. This work facilitates interpretation of existing and future calcium studies using mainstream transgenic lines, such as the Allen's Brain Observatory Visual dataset. The study's findings highlight the challenges of inferring underlying action potential trains from calcium imaging due to population studies employing a large field of view containing many neurons, compromising signal-to-noise ratio. The results aid interpretation of existing and future imaging datasets and provide a better understanding of the relationship between spiking activity and fluorescence signals in transgenic mice.
Researchers have discovered a unique biological valve in the female genitalia of the Brazilian cave insect Neotla, composed of two plate-like sclerites, a closure element, and in-and-out canals, allowing females to receive multiple seminal packages from the same or different males within a short time. Females of Neotla possess a penis-like intromittent organ used to coercively anchor males and obtain voluminous semen, which is packed in a capsule initiated by seminal injection and serves not only for fertilization but also as nutrition. The valve has two slots for insemination, enabling Neotla to continue mating while the first slot is occupied. This valve, in conjunction with the female penis, is a morphological novelty allowing females to compete for seminal gifts in nutrient-poor habitats through prolonged copulation and multiple injections. The evolution of this valve may have been a prerequisite for the reversal of the introment organ in Neotla. The male-holding organ and long copulation durations of 40-70 hours, compared to 50 minutes in close relatives, can be considered adaptations for escalated competition for nuptial gifts in this group inhabiting oligrophic habitats. Females of Neotla use a mechanism to control seminal flow, allowing to receive two packages from the same or different males within a short time. The valve enables females to receive a second capsule while the first is consumed. Females can selectively use one of two slots for insemination, leaving the other empty, and immediately receive an additional package from the same or other male using the empty slot. The presence of a sperhecal plate with valve may refine understanding of the causes and consequences of reversed selection in this genus. Female-female competition for males to receive seminal substances is considered the driving evolution of the female penis. In a related species lacking genital reversal, seminal transfer for a voluminous capsule is complete in 50 minutes. The female Neotla penis bears spines, anchoring males coercively during copulation, indicating females are responsible for prolonged copulation to obtain more semen.
Synaptic soluble N-ethylmaleimide-sensitive factor attachment receptor (SNARE) proteins play a crucial role in the rapid exocytosis of neurotransmitters in a Munc-18-dependent manner. The functions of different stages in exocytosis and the role of Munc-18 in SNARE assembly are not well understood. Using optical tweezers, researchers observed four distinct stages of assembly in SNARE N-terminal, middle, C-terminal, and linker domains. SNARE mutations differentially affect SNARE assembly, with N-terminal and C-terminal domains responsible for vesicle docking and fusion, and middle domain regulating SNARE assembly and fusion. Munc-18 initiates SNARE assembly and structures t-SNARE, independent of syntaxin N-terminal domain, and stabilizes the half-zippered SN complex dependent on the N-terminal domain. In the absence of Munc-18, SNAP-25 dissociates from tethered syntaxin and VAMP when the t-SN complex unfolds, with no SNARE reassembly observed due to dissociation of SNAP-25. In contrast, in the presence of M-18, 45% of completely unfolded SNARE complexes show full reassembly, suggesting M-18 holds SNAP-25 together with tethered syntaxin or VAMP, facilitating de novo SN assembly. M-18 has three force-dependent activities in SNARE assembly, consistent with multiple modes of binding between M-18 and SNARE complex. M-18 regulates every stage of SNARE assembly, proposing a working model to account for multiple roles in stage-wise zippering and membrane fusion. SNARE mutations compromise interactions between fully assembled SNARE and cognate proteins can support membrane fusion, concluding M-18 acts on folding intermediates of SNs to chaperone assembly.
Researchers investigated how integrin adhesomes self-assemble locally and correctly at diverse cell-matrix adhesion sites by exploring the cytosolic state of integrin-adhesome components and their dynamic exchange between adhesion sites and cytosol. Using fluorescence cross-correlation spectroscopy and fluorescence recovery after photobleaching, they found the integrin adhesome is preassembled in the cytos as multi-protein blocks for adhesion sites. Stationary focal adhesions release symmetrically the same types of protein complexes they recruit, keeping the cytos pool of building blocks spatially uniform. The integrin adhesome is preassembled in the cytos forming multi-protein blocks that facilitate rapid and modular assembly of adhesion sites. Physical associations between adhesion components are spatially uniform around stationary adhesions, indicating sites exchange building blocks with cytos without altering them. In the cytos, blocks cannot form bigger structures due to dependencies between protein interactions. At the plasma membrane, the system can locally self-assemble adhesion sites by passing checkpoints inducing interactions in the integrin adhesome. The integrin adhesome is preassembled in cytos forming blocks for cell-matrix adhesion, size-confined and corresponding to layers across focal adhes anchoring and integrin regulation, force transduction, and actin regulation. Symmetric exchange between adhesion and cytos ensures blocks enter and exit adhesion without alteration. A model proposes switchable formation of adhesion via preassembled blocks where the integrin adhesome is preassembled in cytos as blocks for adhesion. Self-assembly would lose control on identity of co-recruited proteins when recruiting a protein in a block, forming aberrant structures, highlighting the need to integrate interaction-dependencies across the integrin adhesome.
Researchers have identified a novel electrophysiological marker of arousal levels in humans, utilizing intracranial and surface electroencephalogram recordings across four independent datasets. The 1/f spectral slope of the electrophysiological power spectrum, representing the non-oscillatory, scale-free component of neural activity, distinguishes wakefulness from propofol anesthesia, non-rapid eye movement sleep, and rapid eye movement sleep. Notably, the spectral slope discriminates wakefulness from rapid eye movement sleep based solely on the neurophysiological brain state, indicating that reduced states are not only defined by synchronous oscillatory activity. The study demonstrates that the spectral slope becomes more negative in sleep and under anesthesia compared to wakefulness and discriminates wakefulness from non-rapid eye movement and rapid eye sleep. Analysis of two datasets during a full night of sleep revealed that the spectral slope differs significantly between wakefulness and sleep stages. The frequency range of 30-45 Hz is suited to track arousal in both anesthesia and different sleep levels, including rapid eye and non-rapid eye sleep. The slope mirrors rapid changes in excitability over the course of a slow wave, providing a mechanistic link between the slope and population synchrony during slow oscillations. The non-oscillatory part of the power spectrum reliably differentiates wakefulness from reduced arousal states, namely rapid eye, non-rapid sleep, and propof anesthesia. Collectively, the results provide evidence that the spectral tracks changes in levels in propof anesthesia and sleep with high precision, observable on a range of timescales, and correlates with all stages of arousal. The findings describe a common electrophysiological marker that tracks states of reduced arousal, including sleep stages and anesthesia in humans. The spectral slope in the 30-45 Hz range correlates with all stages of arousal, providing empirical evidence for its role in tracking changes in levels. Overall, the study establishes the 1/f spectral slope as a reliable electrophysiological marker of arousal in humans, applicable to various states of reduced arousal, including sleep and anesthesia.
Researchers have discovered that dynamic extensions from chloroplasts, known as stromules, connect with nuclei and play a role in innate immunity. The study demonstrates that stromules extend along microtubules and that microtubule organization directly affects stromule dynamics, with stabilization of microtubules increasing stromule numbers and length. Actin filaments are not required for stromule extension but provide anchor points for stromules, and there is a correlation between the direction of stromules from chloroplasts and the direction of chloroplast movement. Stromule-directed chloroplast movement was observed in steady-state conditions without immune induction, suggesting it is a general function of stromules in epidermal cells. The results show microtubules and actin filaments facilitate perinuclear clustering of chloroplasts during an immune response. A proposed model suggests stromules extend along microtubules, connect to actin anchor points surrounding nuclei, facilitating stromule movement of chloroplasts to nuclei during innate immunity. Microtubules are required for stromule extension and movement, with depolymerization leading to retraction and stabilization increasing frequency. The cytoskeleton supports cellular functions including cytoplasmic streaming, cell division, elongation, polar growth, vesicle trafficking, nuclear positioning, and morphogenesis. The study shows dynamic stromules extend along microtubules, actin filaments stabilize stromules, and chloroplast-nuclear connections during immune response. Microtubule-mediated stromule extension and actin-mediated stromule anchoring are complementary during stromule formation and movement, providing mechanistic insights into interactions with cytoskeleton and stabilizing stromules. A new type of organellar movement along microtubules, stromule-directed, reveals a mechanism for perinuclear clustering during innate immunity. The precise role of actin filaments and microtubules in stromule dynamics in green tissue chloroplasts was not well understood, but analysis of the mechanism of stromule extension and movement in chloroplasts of green tissue and perinuclear clustering during immunity shows microtubules are required for stromule extension and movement. Future investigation will focus on molecular components for stromule dynamics, stromule movement, and importance of perinuclear clustering during immunity.
Researchers investigated whether amino acid sequences of residues that contact across protein interfaces covary during evolution and if such covariance can be used to predict contacts and assemble models of biological complexes. They found that residue pairs identified using a pseudo-likelihood method to covary across protein-protein interfaces in the 50S ribosomal unit and 28 bacterial complexes with known structures are almost always in contact, provided the number of aligned sequences is greater than the average length of the proteins. This method was used to make subunit predictions for 36 protein complexes with unknown structures and models for the tripartite ATP-independent periplasmic transporter, tripart efflux system, pyruvate formate-activating enzyme, and methion ABC transporter. Results suggest interactions can be accurately predicted across protein-protein interfaces given sufficient aligned sequences. Residue-residue contact predictions were made for 36 of 64 complexes with unknown structures and models presented for four well-constrained complexes. Significant residue-residue covariance occurs frequently between physically interacting protein pairs but rarely non-interacting pairs, useful for predicting protein interactions. Application of statistical models to coevolution-based contact prediction has been limited, this study examines residue-residue covariation across protein-protein interfaces using pseudo-likelihood statistical method. Predictions contribute to the determination of structures of biologically important complexes. Strongly co-evolving residue pairs for complexes without structure had at least one prediction with GREML score greater or equal to 0.85.
Researchers investigated how individuals accumulate information from multiple locations in a scene to interpret visual patterns, using a novel gaze-conting paradigm in a visual categorization task. The results showed that participants' scan paths follow an active strategy that incorporates information already acquired about the scene and knowledge of statistical patterns. Notably, categorization performance improved when locations were revealed by an optimal Bayesian active algorithm. Combining a Bayesian observer and active algorithm estimated that a major portion of apparent suboptimality of fixation arises from prior biases, perceptual noise, inaccuracies in eye movements, and the process selecting locations is 70% efficient. The findings suggest participants select eye movements to maximize information about abstract categories requiring integration from multiple locations. The study extends the range of active sensing to tasks with arbitrary latent features, providing fixation-by-fixation analysis of gathering under eye movements by matching the observer model to participants' performance. This allowed dissociation of eye-movement selection from perceptual, motor, and decision processes, identifying sources of sub-optimality and quantifying efficiency in choosing fixation throughout the scene. The approach is amenable to multiple perceptual domains, estimating efficiency of eye movements in high-level tasks on a fixation-by-fixation basis, focusing on pattern categorization different from visual search. Participants' eye movements were more efficient than heuristic strategies employing a subset of elements, about 70% as efficient as the optimal sensor operating with participants' prior, perception, noise, and inaccuracies. An experiment with statistically-controlled visual patterns allowed mathematically tractable quantification of information at each fixation, using gaze-conting categorization tracking eye and revealing small apertures of the image at each location, ensuring foveal vision dominated.
Nuclear transport is a tightly regulated process controlled by the nuclear pore complex, allowing thousands of molecules to cross, including large cargoes such as pathogens, mRNAs, and pre-ribosomes, with little understanding of the requirements for their import, especially the role of multivalent binding to transport receptors via nuclear localisation sequences and the effect of size on import efficiency. Researchers assayed nuclear import kinetics of 30 large cargo models based on capsid-like particles, 17-36 nm in size, with up to 240 nuclear localisation sequences, showing requirements for transport can be recapitulated by a two-parameter biophysical model correlating import flux with energetics of cargo through the nuclear pore. Results reveal key determinants of large cargo import in cells, scaling non-linearly with size, influenced by biochemical and physicochemical properties of cargo surface for small transport. Theoretical model assumes capsids do not interact during transport, but cargoes with nuclear localisation sequences bind nuclear receptors, entering the nucleus rapidly. Studies characterized nuclear receptor-mediated transport, focusing on cargoes with one to five sequences, showing nuclear kinetics follow mono-exponential behavior. Functional nucleocytoplasmic transport can be reconstituted by adding key components: Importinβ, Importα, RanDP, NTF2, and GTP, validated by control experiments using fluorescent dextran and model cargoes. Nuclear transport is a hallmark of eukaryotic cells, central to viability, with more than 200 nuclear pore complexes spanning the envelope in HeLa cells, ≈120 MD in metazoans and roughly half in yeast.
The co-stimulatory molecule CD28 is essential for the activation of helper T cells, and its role in maintaining T cell responses following activation has been investigated. A strain of mice where CD28 is removed from CD4+ T cells after priming was generated to determine the function of CD28 after T cell priming. The results show that continued CD28 expression is important for effector CD4 T cells following infection, with maintained CD28 required for the expansion of T helper type 1 cells and the differentiation and maintenance of follicular helper cells during viral infection. Persistent CD28 is also required for clearance of the bacterium Citrobacter rodium from the gastrointestinal tract. CD28 is essential for CD4 T cell priming and differentiation into effector cell subsets, demonstrating a previously undescribed role for CD28 following cell activation. CD28 expression is required after cell priming for the expansion of Th1 cells and the differentiation and maintenance of Tfh cells. Maintained CD expression is also required for clearance of enteropathic bacteria after oral infection, demonstrating CD expression is required for intact effector CD4 T responses during infection. CD28 signaling is essential for multiple facets of CD4 T activation including proliferation, survival, glucose metabolism, and migration. Consequently, CD-deficient mice have reduced expansion of effector CD4 cells and do not form Th1 and Tfh cells after infection or immunization. Tfh differentiation requires CD28 ligation during interactions of primed cells with B cells, fully differentiated Tfh require CD28 for survival, while Th1 do not require CD28 for maintenance but for expansion following T activation. Mice unable to clear Citrobacterium from the gastrointestinal tract following oral infection demonstrate the importance of CD28 in immune response to bacterial infection.
Helicobacter pylori's helical shape is crucial for efficient stomach colonization, but the molecular mechanisms generating this shape remain unclear. The helical center pitch and radius of wild-type H. pylori dictate surface curvatures with higher positive and negative Gaussian curvatures than straight or curved-rod H. pylori. Quantitative 3D microscopy analysis using N-acetylmamic acid or D-alanine probes showed cell growth is enhanced at sidewall curvature extremes. Immunofluorescence revealed Mre is abundant at negative curvature, while bactin Ccm is abundant at positive curvature. Strains with altered Ccm polymerization properties lose helical shape and positive curvatures. A model is proposed where Ccm and Mre promote PG synthesis at positive and negative curvatures, necessary for maintaining helical shape.

To investigate H. pylori diameter control and sidewall curvature, metabolic probes were used to study PG synthesis patterning. Superresolution and 3D image analysis showed synthesis is enhanced at negative curvature and a range of positive curvatures. Cytoskeletal proteins Mre and Ccm localization were investigated. Mre promotes synthesis at negative curvature due to its preference and role in localizing PG activity. To maintain sidewall curvature with Mre-driven growth, H.pylori enhances growth at the major axis independent of Mre. Bactin Ccm localizes to the helical axis, promoting synthesis at positive curvatures, supplementing Mre-associated synthesis at negative curvatures.

Mre and Ccm contribute to PG patterning, allowing H.pylori to maintain curvatures with Mre-associated synthesis. In E. coli, Mre is enriched at negative curvature, Ccm at positive curvatures where synthesis is enhanced. Both Mre and Ccm maintain PG activity, and patterning plays a role in helical shape maintenance. Computational tools demonstrated H.pylori achieves broader sidewall Gaussian curvature distribution than curved-rod bacteria, using distinct mechanisms to achieve curvatures. The distribution of sidewall Gaussian curvature in H.pylori is higher than in C.crescentus and V.cholera, indicating a unique mechanism for helical shape maintenance. Ccm variants with altered polymerization properties result in loss of helical shape and positive curvatures, supporting the model of Ccm and Mre promoting PG synthesis at positive and negative curvatures for helical shape maintenance.
Cdc48, a ubiquitin-selective chaperone, plays a crucial role in regulating mitochondrial dynamics by orchestrating the function of E3 ligases and deubiquitases. The deubiquitases Ubp12 and Ubp exert opposing effects on mitochondrial fusion and cleave different ubiquitin chains on mitofusin Fzo. Cdc48 integrates the activities of these two deubiquases, which are themselves ubiquitylated, to control the balance between activation or repression of mitochondrial fusion. Cdc promotes proteolysis of U12, stabilizing pro-fusion ubiquity on F1, while loss of U12 stabilizes Ubp and facilitates removal of ubiquitin chains on F1, inhibiting fusion. This cascade ensures tight control of F1 ubiquitylation levels, allowing sufficient for mitochondrial fusion while preventing unnecessary ubiquity that targets F1 for proteasomal turnover. The cascade between Ubp and U2 revises recent results linking U2 and M30, where M30 catalyzes the formation of pro-fusion ubiquitin forms on F1. Cdc negatively regulates U12, which negatively regulates U2, explaining their opposite effects on targets and ubiquitin homeostasis. U12 and U2 cleave different ubiquity forms of F1 that promote or repress mitochondrial fusion, with U12-specificity preceding U2-specificity on F1. Unopposed anti-fusion ubiquity disrupts mitochondrial tubulation. U12 recognizes ubiquity chains on F1 composed of a small number of ubiquitin moieties, proposing U12 does not stabilize substrates due to short ubiquitin chains. Both U12 and U2 deubity F1, binding different forms of ubiquity F1, with U12 binding forms stabilizing F1 and promoting fusion, while U2 recognizing chains targeting F1 for proteas degradation and inhibiting fusion. Ubiquitin is a double regulator of mitochondrial fusion, with U2 and U12 exerting opposite effects on F1 and fusion. Cdc regulates the ubiquity status of F1 through a novel cascade with U12 and U2, fine-tuning the fusogenic activity of F1, allowing control between activation or repression of mitochondrial fusion.
Researchers investigated whether vision is necessary for the development of categorical organization in the Ventral Occipito-Temporal Cortex (VOT) by using fMRI to characterize VOT responses to eight categories presented acoustically in sighted and early blind individuals and visually in a separate sighted group. The results showed that VOT reliably encodes sound categories in both sighted and blind people using a representational structure and connectivity similar to the one found in vision. However, sound categories were more reliably encoded in the blind group using a representational format closer to the one found in vision. Crucially, VOT in blind represents categorical membership of sounds rather than their acoustic features. The findings suggest that sounds trigger categorical responses in the VOT of congenitally blind and sighted people that partially match the topography and functional profile of the visual response despite qualitative nuances in categorical organization between modalities and groups. The categorical organization similar to one found in vision emerged in VOT in absence of visual experience and such categorical response to sounds was enhanced and more stable in congenitally blind people. Studies have shown that in absence of vision the occital cortex enhances response to non-visual processing.
