Representational analysis demonstrated that categorical membership is the main factor predicting representational structure of sounds in VOT in blind rather than lower-level acoustic attributes. VOT reliably encodes categorical membership of sounds from eight categories in sighted and blind using topography, representational format, and representational connectivity similar to one observed in response to images in vision. However, categorical representation of auditory stimuli in VOT exhibits different nuances compared to categorical generated by visual stimuli in sighted. The overlap in responses may come from VOT representing visual attributes in sighted and acoustic in blind due to crossmodal plasticity. Studies involving congenitally blind have shown occital cortex may represent acoustic features such as frequencies. The results demonstrate that VOT encodes sound categories in both sighted and blind with representational structure and connectivity similar to vision and categorical membership is main factor predicting representational structure in blind.
Researchers have discovered a strain of the behavior-manipulating fungal pathogen Entomophthora muscae that infects wild Drosophila and established methods to infect Drosophila melanogaster in the laboratory. Infected flies exhibit moribund behaviors characteristic of E. muscae infection, including climbing upward, extending their proboscis, affixing in place, and raising their wings, clearing a path for infectious spores to launch from their abdomens. The fungus invades the nervous system, suggesting a direct means by which it could induce behavioral changes. The E. muscae-D melanaster system is proposed as a model for developing a mechanistic understanding of parasitic manipulation of host behavior due to the vast molecular toolkit available for D. melanaster. The invasion of the nervous system by E. muscae grants the fungus direct access to host neurons and may be mechanistically important for achieving behavioral manipulation of the host. Four general mechanisms are proposed by which E. muscae achieves behavioral manipulation. To gauge if E. muscae is capable of causing patent infection in hosts outside Drosidae, flies were exposed to E. muscae-infected D. melanaster and observed to die and sporulate, indicating they had been killed by the fungus. Transcription changes in host and fungus were measured at 24 time points over 5 days to gain a comprehensive look into how E. mus infection progresses in D. melanaster at the molecular level. Early infected flies are phenotypically indistinguishable from uninfected flies, complicating the analysis. Similar behaviors are induced in dipterans by distantly related Zygomycete fungi within the Entomophthora muscae complex. The E. muscae-D. melanaster system will enable rapid progress in understanding how E. muscae manipulates host behavior due to the availability of genetic and neurobiological tools in the host.
Researchers have generated transcriptome-wide RNA-binding profiles of 30 general RNA degradation factors in the yeast Saccharomyces cerevisiae to better understand RNA processing and degradation in eukaryotic cells. The profiles reveal the distribution of degradation factors between different RNA classes and are consistent with the canonical degradation pathway for closed-loop forming mRNAs after deadenylation. Modeling based on mRNA half-lives suggests most factors bind intact mRNAs, whereas decapping factors are recruited only for mRNA degradation, consistent with decapping being a rate-limiting step. Decapping factors preferentially bind mRNAs with non-optimal codons, consistent with rapid degradation of inefficiently translated mRNAs. Global analysis suggests the nuclear machinery, including complexes Nrd1/Nab and TRAMP, targets aberrant nuclear RNAs and processes snoRNAs. 

The study used photoactivatable ribonucleide-enhanced crosslinking and immunoprecipitation to generate transcript-wide protein profiles for 30 RNA factors involved in deadenylation, decapping, exosome-mediated degradation, and RNA surveillance pathways, including nuclear surveillance and cytoplasmic nonsense-mediated decay. Bioinformatic analysis revealed how degradation factors vary in binding specificities for different RNAs and preferred locations on transcripts. Comparisons with published profiles of other RNA-binding factors revealed clusters that co-occupy RNAs or co-localize RNAs. The dataset provides a resource for future studies of earyotic RNA pathways, mechanisms, and integration of mRNA metabolism. 

Transcriptome binding for 30 RNA degradation factors in yeast revealed factor enrichment on different RNA classes and behavior for mRNAs and associated antisense transcripts. RNA degradation pathways enable regulation of RNA levels and surveillance of aberrant or poorly functional RNAs in cells. The large variety of RNA factors poses questions on how pathways are selected and whether RNA sequence can influence selection, requiring systematic analysis of RNA profiles of involved factors. Several transcript profiles of RNA factors have been reported, but this study provides comprehensive analysis of RNA-binding of 30 general degradation factors in S cerevisiae.
Researchers have discovered a novel bivalent chromatin structure associated with the rapid induction of camalexin biosynthesis genes in response to pathogen signals in Arabidopsis. The study found that camin biosynthesis genes are marked with two epigenetic modifications, trimethylation of lysine 27 of histone 3 (H3K27me3) and acetylation of lysine 18 histone 3 (H3K18ac), which have opposite effects on gene expression, forming a previously uncharacterized type of bivalent chromatin. Mutants with reduced H3K27me or H3Kac suggested both modifications are required to determine the timing of gene expression and metabolite accumulation at an early stage of the stress response. The H3K27me-HK3ac bivalent chromatin, named kairo, plays a crucial role in controlling the timely induction of gene expression upon stress stimuli in plants. By integrating epigenomic profiles with genome-wide metabolic networks in Arabidopsis, two epigen marks playing opposite roles on gene expression, H3K27me and H3Kac, were enriched at camin biosynthesis and other metabolic pathways. In mutants with defective H3Kme or H3ac, the induction of cam biosynthesis genes and metabolite accumulation was accelerated or delayed. The results provide new evidence for how chemical defense by camin may be regulated at the epigen level. The H3me-HKac bivalent chromatin and its role on cam biosynthesis provide a molecular handle to investigate transcription factors that directly bind to promoters of biosynthesis genes interacting with proteins that deposit or remove epigen marks forming bivalent chromatin upon stress signals. The study found Hme and Hac co-localized on biosynthesis genes forming a previously uncharacterized form of bivalent chromatin and examined the role of Hme-Hac bivalent chromatin on the induction of biosynthesis by combining epigenomics, genetics, molecular biology, and biochemistry. The results indicate the Hme-Hac bivalent chromatin maintains the proper timing of biosynthesis gene upon pathogen signal. Overall, the study reveals the importance of Hme-Hac bivalent chromatin in controlling the induction of cam biosynthesis genes in response to pathogen signals in Arabidopsis.
The epigenetic mechanisms that enable astrocytes to retain neurogenic competence throughout adult life are poorly understood. Research has shown that astrocytes serving as neural cells in the mouse subventricular zone express the histone methyltransferase EZ2, a polycomb repressive factor required for neurogenesis independent of its role in subventricular zone neural stem cell proliferation. Ink4/Arf-deficiency in Ezh-deleted subventricular zone neural stem cells rescues cell proliferation but neurogenesis remains defective. Olig2 is a direct target of EZ2, and repression of this bHL transcription factor is critical for neuronal differentiation. Furthermore, Ezh prevents the activation of genes associated with non-subventricular zone neuronal types. In the human brain, subventricular zone cells including astroglia express EZ2, correlating with postnatal neurogenesis. EZ2 is an epigen regulator that distinguishes neurogenic astrocytes, orchestrating distinct and separable aspects of adult cell biology with implications for regenerative medicine and oncogenesis.

 EZ2 expression is a distinguishing feature of neurogenic astrocytes in the adult brain, required for distinct functions regulating cell proliferation and neuronal specification. To enable self-renewal, EZ2 directly represses the Ink4/Ar locus. Studies establish EZ2 as a feature of astrogl cells in postnatal subvent zone of mouse and human brain, identifying separable roles for EZ2 in neuron production from multipotent stem population. Results provide insights into the complex role of polycomb repressive factor in development and gliagenesis. EZ2 expressed in astroia and neuronal precursors of infant subvent zone suggests key roles in postnatal human neurogenesis. Data indicate EZ2 a critical epigen determinant for maintenance of multipotent astrogl, enabling proliferation and cell specification of adult population.

 EZ2 has a role in subvent zone neurogenesis distinct from function in Ink4Ar repression, rescuing only proliferation not neuronal, enabling investigation of additional roles in neurogenic astro population. Acute deletion of EZ2 in subvent zone neural cells resulted in decreased neurogenesis related to reduced proliferation. Future studies may address re-expression of EZ2 in non-sub zone astrotes enabling neurogenesis or transdifferentiation into neurons for therapeutic purposes. Overall, EZ2 is critical epigen regulator distinguishing neurogenic astrocytes, with implications for regenerative medicine and oncogenesis.
Researchers investigated the ecology and evolution of viruses infecting uncultivated SUP05 bacteria in a marine oxygen minimum zone using single-cell and meta-genomics. They analyzed 127 single-cell amplified genomes from SUP05 bacteria and identified 69 viral contigs representing five new genera within dsDNA Caudovales and ssDNA Microviridae. The results showed that approximately one-third of SUP05 bacteria were infected with viruses, with higher infection frequencies in areas of severe oxygen deficiency. Microviridae clonality suggested recovery of bloom-terminating viruses, while co-infection between dsDNA and ssDNA viruses indicated previously unrecognized modes of cooperation. Analysis of 186 microbial and viral metagenomes revealed that SUP viruses persisted for years but remained endemic to the oxygen minimum zone. The study also identified virus-encoded dissimilatory sulfite reductase, suggesting that SUP viruses reprogram their host's energy metabolism. The findings demonstrate closely coupled SUP virus-host co-evolutionary dynamics with potential to modulate biogeochemical cycling in climate-critical oxygen minimum zones. Single-cell genomics linked SUP viruses and hosts across gradients of oxygen and sulfide over a 3-year interval, revealing patterns of co-infection between ssDNA and dsDNA viruses and auxiliary metabolic genes modulating electron transfer essential to SUP energy metabolism. SUP05 viruses were detected within and below the oxycline, consistent with SUP water column disposition, and contained genes encoding dissimilatory sulfite reductase, indicating a role in modulating the marine sulfur cycle. Fragment recruitment from microbial metagenomes to reference contigs was used as a proxy for abundance, providing insights into SUP lineage-specific virus-host ecology. The results highlight the importance of viruses in modulating microbial communities and ecosystem functions, with implications for understanding biogeochemical cycling in expanding oxygen minimum zones.
Cilia and flagella are assembled and maintained by the process of intraflagellar transport, a highly conserved mechanism involving more than 20 intraflagellar transport proteins. Researchers investigated the putative intraflagellar transport protein TTC26/DY13 to understand its function. Using live imaging and biochemical approaches, they found that TTC26/D13 is an intraflagellar transport complex B protein in mammalian cells and Chlamydomonas reinhardtii. Knockdown of TTC26/D13 in zebrafish embryos or mutation of TTC26/D13 in C. reinhardtii resulted in short cilia with abnormal motility. Surprisingly, intraflagellar transport particle assembly and speed were normal in dy13 mutant flagella, unlike other intraflagellar transport B complex mutants. Proteomic and biochemical analyses showed a set of proteins involved in motility was depleted in the dy13 mutant, indicating different intraflagellar transport proteins are responsible for different cargo subsets. Deletion of dy13 in C. reinhardt still resulted in flagella, indicating TTC26/DY13 is not required for ciliogenesis, but flagella were shorter with pronounced motility defects. Similar phenotypes were observed in zebraf cilia when ttc was depleted. Intraflag transport particle movement was normal in dy13 flagella, but inner dyne arm components were reduced, indicating a specific role for TTC26/D13 in transporting a subset of ciliary proteins. Comparative proteomic analysis supported a function for TTC26/D13 in transporting a subset of cargo involved in flagellar motility. Unlike other intraflag transport proteins, TTC26/D13 is not required for ciliogenesis or assembly of intraflag transport particles, but plays a role in transporting specific motility-related ciliary proteins into ciliaflagella. Mutant cells assembled ciliaflag but they were shorter, coinciding with observations in C eleg dy13, which has abnormal cilia with short length. Most intraflag transport proteins are necessary to assemble ciliaflag in the first place, for example C reinhardt mutants ift46, ift52, and ift88 cannot assemble flagella. Knockdown of IFT56 in zebfish embryos and C reinhardti dy13 cells showed motility defects ciliaflag, further analysis is necessary to clarify the role of IFT56TTC26DY13 in determining cilia length.
Lymphatic endothelial cells (EC) stimulate melanoma metastasis and invasion through a mechanism involving matrix metalloproteinase-14 (MMP14)-dependent Notch3 and β1-integrin activation. Research has shown that exposure of expansively growing human melanoma cells to lymphatic endothelial cells in 3D co-culture facilitates melanoma distant metastasis in mice. The interaction between melanoma cells and lymphatic endothelial cells induces MMP14-dependent Notch3 and β-integr activation, leading to an invasive sprouting phenotype in 3D matrices. Notably, MMP14 is necessary for lymph endothelial cell-induced Notch induction and coincident β-integr activation, and both MMP and Notch are required for lymph endothelial cell-mediated metastasis in zebrafish xenografts. The interaction between melanoma cells and lymphatic endothelial cells leads to increased metastasis of melanoma xenografts in vivo, which is dependent on MMP and Notch. Studies using 2D and 3D lymph endothelial-melanoma co-culture models, in vivo tumor models, transcriptome profiling, in vitro studies, and zebrafish xenograft metastasis assays demonstrate that lymphatic endothelial cells promote melanoma metastasis by inducing a reversible switch from 3D growth to invasive sprouting cell phenotype. In xenograft models, dissemination of metastatic melanoma cells to distant organs is increased by lymphatic endothelial priming. Lymphatic endothelial cells in the tumor microenvironment affect the metastatic melanoma phenotype through molecular crosstalk between the tumor and lymphatic endothelium. Notch receptors 1 and 3 are highly activated in melanoma with pro-tumorigenic functions, and the identified MMP-dependent Notch upregulation may be important in metastatic melanoma progression. Lymph endothelial contact induces β-int activation in metastatic cells, required for 3D sprouting. The upregulation of Notch upon lymph endothelial priming reveals the lymph- melanoma model can identify clinically relevant molecules for cancer dissemination and unrecognized pathways contributing to lymphatic metastasis of melanoma.
MicroRNAs are conserved noncoding RNAs that function as posttranscription regulators of gene expression and miR-9 is one of the most abundant miAs in the brain. Although the function of miR9 has been characterized in neural progenitors, its role in dendritic and synaptic development remains unknown. Researchers developed a transgenic miRNA sponge mouse line allowing inactivation of mi9 in a spatio-temporal-controlled manner and found that mi9 controls dendritic growth and synaptic transmission in vivo. The mi9-mediated downregulation of transcription repressor REST is essential for proper dendritic growth. The study defines a key role for mi9 during postmitotic differentiation of neurons and presents a new reporter mouse line to characterize the activity of mi9 in different cell types in the brain. Inhibition of mi9 results in aberrant dend growth in vivo accompanied by impairment in synaptic transmission. A conditional transgenic mouse model revealed a developmental role of mi9 in postmitotic neurons as a regulator of dend growth and transmission. The study focused on postmit stages of neuronal differentiation and demonstrated a critical role for mi9 in control of dend development. Other miAs influence dend growth suggesting expression is under complex post-transcription regulation. The transgenic mouse line allows inactivation of an mi family in spatio-temporal manner and analysis reveals a role for mi9 on postmit neuronal as regulator of dend through repression of REST. The effects in mi9 mice are cell-intrinsic and mi9 exerts function modeling dend arbors as key of genetic controlling dend development. Synaptic transmission was impaired in mi9 sponge mice.
Researchers investigated the link between social visual difficulties and atypical brain organization in autism spectrum disorder (ASD) using resting-state functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) data from a large sample of toddlers with ASD and non-ASD comparison groups. They found ASD-related functional hypoconnectivity between social brain circuitry, including the default mode network (DM) and visual and attention networks. A subtype of ASD with pronounced social engagement difficulties, identified through eye-tracking, was characterized by marked DM-occipito-temporal cortex (TC) hypoconnectivity. Increased DM-TC hypoconnectivity was related to increased severity of social-communication difficulties in this subtype. Early social-visual hypoconnectivity is a key neurobiological feature of this subtype and may be critical for future social-communicative development. This subtype can be identified with high precision via a simple eye test of social or non-social preferences in early development. Atypical intrinsic brain organization involving circuitry relevant to social engagement was found in early ASD development, with the default mode network functionally disconnected from visual cortices and dorsal attention network. Heterogeneity in social engagement is important for some but not all functional differences in early ASD, supporting the idea that toddler ASD is characterized by hypoconnectivity between DM and visual circuits and attention. Resting-state fMRI measured intrinsic connectivity between neural circuits affected in early ASD, with heterogeneity in social engagement explaining connectivity differences. The findings suggest early social-visual hypoconnectivity is a key neurobiological feature of this ASD subtype, critical for social-communicative development, and a potential target for early intervention.
Drosophila sessile hemocyte clusters are true hematopoietic tissues that regulate larval blood cell differentiation. Virtually all species of coelate animals contain blood cells that display a division of labor necessary for homeostasis, which depends on the balance between proliferation and differentiation accomplished in hematietic organs. In Drosophila melanaster, the lymph gland produces plasmatocytes and crystal cells that are not released until pupation, yet throughout larval development both hemocyte types increase in numbers. Mature plasocytes can proliferate, but it is not known if crystal numbers increase by self-renew or de novo differentiation. New crystal cells in third instar larvae originate through a Notch-dependent process of plasmatocyte transdifferentiation, occurring in sessile clusters and contingent upon the integrity of these structures. The existence of this hematietic tissue, relying on structure-dependent events to promote homeostasis, creates a paradigm for addressing questions in Drosila hematopoiesis and parallels with vertebrate systems.

Macrophages are the most plastic cells in vertebrate hematiet tissue and their specialization in vivo depends on the local microenvironment provided by the tissue they colonize. Similarly, in Drosila larvae the microenvironment by hemocyte clusters is necessary to induce crystal differentiation from plasmocytes, through cell-contact involving Notch-Serr. The study of hematiet organs and function is essential to understand how mature cells arise and their numbers are controlled. Drosila melanaster embryonic hematies produces two types of mature hemocytes: plasmocytes and crystal, plasmocytes are phagocytic compared to vertebrate macrophages. The increase of circulating and sessile crystal numbers during Dila development is lined with an apparent paradox: no mature cell has been dividing during larval and crystal in lymph do not enter circulation in homeatic conditions.

The novel hematiet organ, attached to circulating hemocyte population and relying on structure-dependent events to promote homeost, brings closer to addressing fundamental questions Dila hematies. In homeatic conditions, differentiated hemocytes in lymph do not contribute to circulating and sessile population, nonetheless hemocyte in circulation and sessile patches expands throughout development. Plasmocytes are mitotically active, expanding development by self-renewal, while crystal numbers increase through Notch-dependent plasmocyte transdifferentiation in sessile clusters. The integrity of these structures is necessary for this process, creating a new paradigm for understanding Dila hematopoies and establishing parallels vertebrate systems.
Aneuploidy, a condition where cells have abnormal numbers of chromosomes, is linked to various diseases but also facilitates organismal evolution. However, it remains unclear how cells overcome the deleterious effects of aneupidy until new phenotypes evolve. Laboratory strains are extremely sensitive to aneupidy, but aneupidy is common in wild yeast isolates, which show lower-than-expected expression at many amplified genes. Researchers generated diploid strain panels with cells carrying two, three, or four copies of affected chromosomes and found gene-dosage compensation functions at 10-30% of amplified genes. Genes subject to dosage compensation are under higher expression constraint in wild populations but show elevated rates of gene amplification, suggesting copy-number is buffered at these genes. Aneupidy provides an ecological advantage to oak strain YPS9 by amplifying a causal gene that escapes dosage compensation. Dosage compensation buffers gene amplification through aneupidy to provide a natural route to rapid phenotypic evolution.

Chromosomal amplification is common in non-laboratory yeast, which are inherently tolerant of anupidy and display active gene-dosage compensation for specific amplified genes. Genes subject to dosage compensation are buffered against copy-number variation and show elevated rates of amplification. Mutations or adaptation could reduce expression at toxic genes, or wild strains downregulate in proportion to gene dose. Researchers generated isogenic aneuploid-euploid pairs and found up to 40% of amplified genes in naturally aneup strains show lower expression due to heritable polymorphisms. Reduced expression is due to polymorphisms downregulating amplified genes selected during adaptation to aneupidy.

Significant phenotypic differences between laboratory and non-laboratory aneup strains led to investigation of amplified gene expression. Researchers interrogated 220 genes across six aneuploids and found Class 2 genes could be misclassified, raising the possibility of genetic variation in dosage. Expression of dosage-compensated genes is controlled, many genes escape compensation and contribute to phenic variation. Whole and partial chromosome amplification is common in selection experiments. Results provide insight into how cells tolerate duplication of toxic genes long enough for new phenotypes to evolve, showing dosage compensation buffers aneupidy to provide a transient route to evolution.
Researchers have characterized xenopsin, a recently classified subtype of opsin, in the flatworm Maritrella crozier, and found it is expressed in ciliary cells of eyes in the larva and in extraocular cells around the brain in the adult. These extraocular cells house hundreds of cilia in an intra-cellular vacuole, similar to jawed vertebrate rod photoreceptors, which enclose their cilia within their own plasma membrane. Functional assays in human cells show Maritrella xenin drives phototransduction primarily by coupling to Gα. The discovery of xenin+ ciliary cells in the eyes of protostome larvae challenges views on opsin and photoreceptor evolution, deviating from the invertebrate trend of extraocular ciliary photoreceptors and ocular rhabdomeric/microvillar photoreceptors. Xenin expression is both ocular and extraocular, and its ability to drive phototransduction in live cell assays and expression in ciliary cells in Maritrella add molecular and functional evidence for the presence of ciliary photoreceptors in flatworms. Metazo ciliary photoreceptors are morphologically diverse, especially in the outer segment where ciliary membranes increase cell surface area through stacking or greater numbers of cilia. Xenin's primary coupling to Gα is supported by two pieces of evidence: activation of a Gα signal cascade in human cells and xenin and Gα localization in the putative ciliary photoreceptor in flatworm. Xenin protein expression on cilia cells that form eyes supports the possibility that the VPx motif is involved in transporting xenin to cilia. The findings highlight similarities between xenin and c-ops, revealing a novel type of opsin-expressing cell, and provide the first molecular and functional evidence of ciliary photoreceptors in flatworms. Xenin's ability to drive phototransduction and expression in ciliary cells provide indirect evidence for the presence of ciliary photoreceptors in flatworms, contributing to the understanding of opsin and photoreceptor evolution.
Spatial and temporal cues are required to specify neuronal diversity, but how these cues are integrated in neural progenitors remains unknown. Drosophila progenitors, or neuroblasts, are a good model to study this as they are individually identifiable with relevant spatial and temporal factors known. Researchers tested whether spatial and temporal factors act independently or sequentially in neuroblasts by using Targeted DamID to identify genomic sites of the Hunchback temporal factor in neuroblasts NB-5 and NB-7 that make different progeny. Hunch targets were found to be different in each neuroblast, ruling out the independent specification model. Each neuroblast had distinct open chromatin domains which correlated with H-bound loci in each neuroblast. The Gsb/Pax3 spatial factor, expressed in NB5 but not NB7, had genomic sites correlated with open chromatin in NB5 but not NB7. This data supports a model in which early-acting spatial factors establish neuroblast-specific chromatin, leading to neuroblast temporal factor binding and production of different neurons in each neuroblast lineage. Over 100 loci differentially bound by Hb in NB5 or NB7 were identified as candidates for lineage-specific neuronal specification. Drosila is an excellent system to investigate spatial and temporal integration during neurogenesis due to a deep understanding of neural progenitor lineages and mechanisms involved in patterning. Spatial and temporal cues integrate to generate lineage-specific neuronal diversity in Drosila and mammalian progenitors but how spatial and temporal transcription factors integrate is unknown. Two mechanisms were hypothesized: independent and sequential specification. In the independent model, transcription factor binding is identical in all neuroblasts, whereas in sequential, binding occurs at different loci in each neuroblast. Hb genomic targets were determined in NB5 versus NB7 to discriminate between these models. Spatial and temporal factors are integrated in Drosila embryonic neuroblasts and optic lobe neuroblasts and likely in mammalian lineages. Temporal transcription factors sequentially expressed in neural progenitors regulate this process in Dros and mammals.
Researchers utilized photo-switchable cross-linkers as molecular tweezers to investigate the pore-opening mechanism of the ATP-gated P2X ion channel, which functions by opening a transmembrane pore in response to extracellular ATP. Recent crystal structures in apo and ATP-bound states revealed molecular motions of the extracellular domain following agonist binding, but the mechanism of pore opening remains unclear. The study employed photo-switchable crossers to monitor inter-residue distances in the transmembrane domain of the P2 receptor during activation, providing experimentally based constraints combined with computational studies for high-resolution models of the channel in open and closed states. Results showed the outer pore expansion is significantly reduced compared to the ATP-bound structure, with the inner and outer ends of adjacent pore-lining helices coming closer during opening through a hinge-bending motion. A decrease in distance between the inner and outer ends of pore-lining TM helices, likely through bending at a critical gating hinge, is essential for activation of the P2 receptor. Breaking one TM2-TM interface is sufficient for gating without altering ATP sensitivity, indicating the light-induced expansion is a local rearrangement of the TMD. The study applied photo-switchable derivatives to investigate the mechanism of pore gating in P2 receptors, providing the first application to explore the motion of rat P2X receptor by cross-linking engineered cysteine residues in TMD with photo-switchable azobenzene tweers. A set of structural constraints with Molecular Dynamics provided high-resolution models of the channel in open and closed states in physiological environment. Data demonstrated a 2.4-fold decrease of ATP sensitivity for the G342 mutant, consistent with the fact that alanine rigidifies α-helices, indicating TM helices contain a critical hinge facilitating vertical bending, allowing inner and outer ends of TM2 to come closer during activation.
Cytokinesis, the process of cell division into two daughter cells, is powered by constriction of an actomyosin contractile ring and has been assumed to occur through similar mechanisms in all animal cells. However, recent evidence suggests cytokinetic regulation in individual cell types exhibits more variation than previously realized. In the four-cell Caenorhabditis elegans embryo, each blastomere has distinct fate specified by conserved pathways. Using fast-acting temperature-sensitive mutants and acute treatment, researchers identified cell-type variation in cytoketic requirements for for forminCYK-1-dependent filamentous-actin cytoskeleton. In one cell, P2, this variation is cell-intrinsically regulated, whereas in another, EMS, it is cell-extrinsically regulated, dependent on Src-1 signaling and direct contact with neighbor P2. Both cell-intrinsic and extrinsic mechanisms control cytoketic variation in cell types, protecting against division failure when the contractile ring is weakened.

Researchers used thermogenetics, drug treatment, and embryo microsection to probe mechanisms of cell-based variation in cytokinesis among blastomeres in four-cell C elegans embryo, finding cell-type differences in functional and temporal activity for forminK1 but not myosin-I. In one-cell embryos, forminK1 inactivation by thermal upshift to restrictive temperature before mid-constriction resulted in 100% cytokinesis failure. Fast-acting temperature-sensitive alleles were used to inactivate contractile ring proteins myos-II and diaphanous forK1 in one-cell, identifying cell-type variation in four-cell embryos for forminK1 but not myos-II.

Data show cell-intrinsic and extrinsic mechanisms contribute to cytoketic diversity, promoting robust cytokinesis when contractile ring is weakened in specific cell types within four-cell C elegans embryo. Each cell has unique identity and can be scored for contractile constriction during cytokinesis. Thermogenetics combined with cell-specific in vivo and ex vivo analysis of cytokinesis in each blastomere of four-cell C elegans embryo studied mechanisms of cytoketic variation. Cell-type differences in functional and temporal forminK activity but not myos-I were found among blastomeres. Results indicate both cell-intrinsic and extrinsic mechanisms promote cell-type cytoketic diversity and protect against division failure when contractile ring is weakened in specific cell types within four-cell C elegans embryo.
Bacterial flagella play a crucial role in mediating host-microbe interactions through tissue tropism during colonization and activating immune responses. The flagellar shaft of some bacteria, including human pathogens, is encased in a membranous sheath of unknown function. Researchers have hypothesized that the sheath may allow bacteria to evade host responses to immunogenic flagellin subunit, but this structural feature has remained an enigma. A recent study demonstrates that rotation of sheathed flagellum in mutualist Vibrio fischer and pathogen Vibrio cholera promotes release of potent bacteria-derived immunogen lipopolysaccharide found in flagellar sheath. The study reveals a new role for flagellar sheath in triggering, rather than circumventing, host responses in squid-vibrio symbiosis. Using squid-vibrio symbiosis as a tool, researchers probed the effect of enigmatic flagellar sheath on host recognition, uncovering a previously unrecognized role for rotation of sheathedella in mediating release of immunogenic lipopolysaccharide. The flagellar sheath has been problematic to study due to lack of mutations resulting in loss of sheath in otherwise sheathed flagella. The functional role of sheath, even in relation to bacterial motility, remains poorly characterized. Observation of this phenomenon furthers understanding of enigmatic structure and contribution to bacterial virulence. Further work will build upon discoveries, clarifying mechanisms underlying signaling during squid-vrio symbiosis, including characterizing composition of lipopolysaccharide released by Vibrio fischer during flagellar rotation and identifying squid receptors recognizing Vibrio lipopolysaccharide. Flagellar activation of immunity has been limited to responses to protein flagellin, despite flagellar sheath flagin monomers being prevalent in secretome of Vibrio cholera and Vibrio fischer. Limited tools to study flagellar sheath led researchers to use model symbiosis between Vibrio fischer and Hawaiian bobtail squid to probe sheathedellum role in inducing symbiont-specific response. Rotation of sheathed flagell in both Vibrio fischer and Vibrio cholera promotes release of lipopolysaccharide, found in flagellar sheath, presenting new implications for study of bacterial pathogens with sheathed flagella, raising biophysical questions of sheathed-flagell function.
Genome instability is associated with mitotic errors and can lead to deleterious rearrangements, but also genetic novelty, and many questions regarding its genesis and evolutionary role remain unanswered. Researchers have described extreme chromosomal restructuring during genome elimination, a process resulting from hybridization of Arabidopsis plants expressing different centromere histones. Shattered chromosomes are formed from the genome of the haploid inducer, consistent with catastrophes affecting a single, large compartmentalized within a micronucle. Analysis of breakpoint implicates breaks followed by repair through non-homologous end joining or stalled repair. Mutation of non-homologous end joining factor DNA Ligase 4 results in enhanced haploid recovery, and heritability and stability of rearranged chromosomes suggest potential for enduring genomic novelty. These findings provide a tractable natural system towards investigating causes and mechanisms of complex rearrangements similar to those associated with human disorders.

The phenomenon studied depends on chimeric CEN3, but a similar effect was observed when the haploid inducer expresses CEN3 of a close species. Results using DNA lig4 mutants suggest non-homolog end joining plays a role in repair of haploid chromosomes contributing to diploid and aneuploid progeny. When non-hom end joining is inhibited, haploid frequency increases. In silico reconstruction indicates non-hom end joining is involved in repairing breaks on shattered chromosomes, contributing to formation or persistence of aneuploid and diploid progeny. Unrepaired double-stranded breaks increase haploid genome elimination similar to observations in mouse-human hybrid elimination.

Consequences of mitotic malfunction on genome integrity can be dire, missegregated chromosomes lead to aneuploidy, extensive and catastrophic restructuring resulting in sequestration in micronuclei, endonolytic damage, defective repair, and rescue. Researchers hypothesize missegregated chromosomes enter degradative pathway initiated by endonucleolytic breaks, occasionally rescued. Results provide evidence for occurrence of chromosomal restructuring when diverged individuals hybridize, identifying centrom-based mechanism for genomic instability. Altered CEN3 strain hybridized to recessive glabrous-1 mutant results in ploidy from centrom-mediated uniparent genome elimination. Non-hom end joining pathway plays a role in haploid chromosomes contributing diploid aneup progeny and unrepaired breaks increase haploid genome elimination. Findings indicate non-hom joining contributes formation persistence aneup and diploid and haploid progeny.
Epstein-Barr virus (EBV) infection is causally linked to several human cancers and expresses viral oncogenes that promote cell growth and inhibit apoptotic response to uncontrolled proliferation. The EBV oncoprotein LMP1 constitutively activates NFκB and is critical for survival of EBV-immortalized B cells. However, during early infection, EBV induces rapid cell proliferation with low levels of LMP and little apoptosis. To define the mechanism of survival in the absence of LMP/NFκ early infection, researchers used BH3 profiling to query mitochondrial regulation of apoptosis and defined a transition from uninfected cells (BCL-2) to early-infected (MCL-1/BCL) and immortalized cells (BFL1). This dynamic change in cell mechanisms relies on regulation of MCL1 mitochondrial localization and BFL1 transcription by the viral EBNA3 protein. EBNA3 regulates the BH3 profile of EBV-infected cells and suppresses apoptosis by promoting MCL1 mitochondrial localization and increasing BFL mRNA transcription. Transcription of BFL is regulated by EB transcription factor EB2 and LMP-induced NFκ. Mature human peripheral B cells depend on BCL2 for survival, while EB latent infection suppresses apoptotic priming during outgrowth by initially upregulating MCL, followed by B1. Mechanistically, a new role for viral protein EB3 in apoptosis resistance during early and late EB-induced cell proliferation was identified. Uninfected human peripheral CD19 B cells depend on B2 for survival, and EB upregulates additional proteins to suppress apoptosis by B2 antagonism. BH profiling predicted EB-infected cells are less primed for apoptosis due to combined effects of M1 and B2 during proliferation, with protection by B1 in immortalized cells. Constitutive NF signaling from L1, a TN homologue, is responsible for suppression in latently proliferating cells, but the initial burst of cell proliferation is driven by EB transcription factors in absence of L or NF activity. The mechanism by which EB3 enhances M1 function at the mitochondria provides insight into apoptotic regulation during B maturation and lymphagenesis. The early phase of EB infection where EBs are expressed with little Ls is known as latency II. The study defines a new role for EB3 in suppression of apoptosis with implications for EB lymphagenesis.
Plants produce diverse metabolites to cope with environmental challenges, driving the diversification of specialized metabolites within and between species. Researchers are beginning to understand how new alleles controlling metabolite diversity arise and how their geographic distribution is structured by ecological and demographic pressures. A study measured variation in specialized metabolites across 797 Arabidopsis thaliana accessions, showing a combination of geography, environmental parameters, demography, and genetic processes influence chemotypes and distribution. Causal loci in metabolism contain frequent independently generated alleles, suggesting within-species convergence, providing a new perspective on selective forces shaping allelic variation and local adaptation. Genetic variants influencing plant chemical variation derive from gene duplication in enzyme encoding genes for metabolism, with mechanistic studies focusing on bialle phenotypic variation linked to loss-of-function variants. The Arabidopsis glucosinolate system is an optimal model to study species-wide processes driving metabolite variation due to known biosynthetic pathways and causal loci for variation. High allelic diversity at these loci raises questions on how pathways pass through speciation boundaries, confounded by observations of similar chemotyp variation in Brassica species. Understanding genetic, demographic, and environmental factors shaping trait variation within a population is key to understanding evolution. This study used aliphatic glucosinolates in Arabidopsis thalian seeds to query genetics, geography, environment, and demography intersecting chemotyp variation across Europe. The intersection of environmental pressure, demography, and genomic complexity gives rise to metabolic variation, using the glucosinolate pathway as a model to understand genomic variation, demography, and environmental pressure shaping metabolism within species.
Apoptosis and its molecular mediators, caspases, have long been regarded as tumor suppressors, with evasion of apoptosis being a hallmark of cancer. However, recent work suggests that apoptotic caspases can also promote proliferation and tumor growth under certain conditions. In a well-studied neoplastic model in Drosophila, oncogenic mutations of the proto-oncogene Ras (RasV12) maintain tumorous cells in an 'undead-like condition and transform caspases from tumor suppressors into promotors. Instead of killing cells, caspases promote the generation of intra and extracellular reactive oxygen species (ROS), which recruit and activate macrophage-like immune cells, signaling back to tumorous epithelial cells to activate oncogenic JNK. JNK further amplifies casase activity, constituting a feedback loop. Interfering with this loop reduces neoplastic behavior and improves organismal survival. Ras-modified casases initiate a feedback loop involving tumorous epithelial and macroage-like cells necessary for uncontrolled growth and invasive behavior.

Oncogenic Ras suppresses the apoptotic activity of casases, keeping scri−/−Ras12 cells in 'und-like condition. Casases induce apoptosis in few cells, but largely promote intra and extracellular ROS required for growth and invasion of neoplastic cells. This work extends models of Eiger and JNK signaling conversion from anti-tumor to pro-tumor by oncogenic Ras, identifying casases as essential components. Although apoptotic casases are tumor suppressors, under conditions like presence of oncogenic Ras12 in scri mutant cells, they adopt tumor-promoting roles. Oncogenic Ras switches caspase activity into tumor-promoting, maintaining scri−/−Ras12 in 'und-like state. Inhibition blocks tumor growth and invasion. The tumor-promoting function is dependent on intra and extracellular ROS required for neoplastic scri−/−Ras clones.

Duox-generated ROS activate hemocytes, JNK signaling, and hyperplastic overgrowth in 'und Ai model. ROS examined in neoplastic scri−/−Ras12 tumor in Dophila show similar function. Eiger and JNK signaling have proliferation and growth-promoting functions in 'und Ai model, prompting investigation of ROS and caspase roles in scri/−Ras growth in Dophila. Oncogenic Ras transforms caspases from tumor suppressors to promotors, generating intra and extracellular ROS, recruiting macrophage-like cells, activating JNK, and amplifying caspase activity. Interfering with this loop reduces neoplastic behavior and improves survival, indicating a tumor-promoting function of apoptotic caspases in presence of oncogenic Ras.
Perception of social interactions compresses subjective duration in an oxytocin-dependent manner. Communication through body gestures permeates daily life and efficient perception of messages reflects one's social competency, which is manifested temporally as shortened subjective duration of social interactions. Motion sequences showing agents acting communicatively are perceived to be significantly shorter in duration compared to those acting non-communicatively. The strength of this effect is negatively correlated with one's autistic tendency. Intranasal oxytocin administration restores the temporal effect in socially less proficient individuals, whereas administration of atosiban, a competitive antagonist of oxytocin, diminishes the effect in socially proficient individuals. These findings indicate perceived time is highly idiosyncratic and ingrained with one's personality, suggesting oxytocin is involved in mediating time perception of social interaction, supporting its role in human cognition. Autistic traits have been associated with reduced oxytocin levels and can be ameliorated with intranasal oxytocin administration. The link between autistic-like tendency and duration of social interactions raises the question of oxytoc's role. Antagonizing oxytoc's effect diminishes temporal compression in socially proficient individuals. The mechanism involves oxytocin, with socially less proficient individuals having lower endogenous oxytoc levels. Application of oxytoc promotes social-induced temporal compression in socially proficient individuals. Results reaffirm perceived social interactions influence on subjective duration, highlighting idiosyncrasy tied to social proficiency, a stable personality trait. Oxytoc plays a critical role in time perception of social interaction, supporting its involvement in human cognition.
The interaction between the SARS-CoV-2 virus Spike protein receptor binding domain (RBD) and the ACE2 cell surface protein is required for viral infection of cells. Mutations in the RBD present in SARSV-2 variants of concern have emerged worldwide, such as the B.1.1.7 lineage with a N501Y mutation that enhances binding to ACE2. There are also ACE2 alleles in humans with mutations in the RBD site. A detailed affinity and kinetics analysis of the effect of five RBD mutations (K417N, K417T, N501, E484, and S477) and two ACE2 mutations (SP and KR) on the RBD/ACE interaction was performed. The effects of individual RBD mutations and combinations found in new SARS-CoV2 Alpha, Beta, and Gamma variants were analyzed. Most of these mutations increased the affinity of the R/ACE interaction, except for KN/T which decreased affinity. The N501 and S477 mutations enhance transmission primarily by enhancing binding, the KN mutations facilitate immune escape, and the E484 mutation enhances binding and immune escape. Studies of enveloped viruses, including SARSV, suggest increases in affinity of viral ligands for cellular receptors can increase infection and disease severity. The effect of increased affinity for SARS2 RBD of the KR and SP ACE mutants is less clear. Carriers of ACE variants may be at greater risk of infection and severe disease as RBDACE binding is limiting for SARS transmission. The effects of ACE variants are primarily relevant to carriers of these mutations. The SARS2 virus enters cells following interaction between the Spike protein and ACE on cell surfaces, with the RBD binding the membrane-distal portion of ACE. Precise information is needed to assess the extent RBD mutations enhance ACE binding or immune evasion. A detailed affinity kinetic analysis of the interaction between RBD and ACE at physiological temperature was undertaken, taking care to avoid common pitfalls. Combinations of RBD mutations including present in VOC20202, Alpha, Beta, and Gamma variants were examined. The results suggest the N501 and S477 mutations enhance transmission, KN mutations facilitate immune escape, and E484 enhances binding and escape. The study provides insight into the effects of RBD and ACE mutations on viral infection and transmission.
Researchers have generated a large-scale map of genetic interactions in metazo cells, specifically in Drosophila cells, to understand how gene-gene interactions shape phenotypes and modify the effects of mutations during development and disease. The study utilized combinatorial RNA interference and automated single-cell phenotyping to generate a genetic map for 21 phenotypic features, devising a method that combines interactions on multiple phenotypes to reveal directional relationships. This approach reconstructed the sequence of protein activities in mitosis and revealed the Ras pathway interacts with the SWI/SNF chromatin-remodelling complex, an interaction conserved in human cells. The map comprises 136 Drosila genes implicated in cellular processes including signaling, chromatin, and cell regulation, with quantitative scoring of 21 phenotypes in cultured cells using automated imaging. Gene functions were inferred based on statistical interactions, predicted complexes, and clustered by similarity. An epistatic relationship between Ras/MAP signaling and SWI/SNF regulators of chromatin remodeling was demonstrated in vivo. Directional genetic interactions were identified at scale and mapped to other genomic sets to identify regulatory relationships. The multivariate phenotyping approach revealed a circuit of temporal and causal relationships of complexes during the M-phase of the cell cycle. Inspection highlighted an unexpected link between chromatin remodeling and RAS signaling with interactions between the SWI/SNF complex and SWI-associated chromatin remodeler. The study presents a powerful approach for reconstructing regulatory networks and provides a resource for interpreting functional consequences of genetic alterations, offering the largest map of multi-phenotype interaction profiles in metazo cells to date.
Heterostly is a widespread floral adaptation that promotes outbreeding, yet its genetic and evolutionary basis remains poorly understood. In Primula, heterosty is controlled by the S-locus supergene that determines the reciprocal arrangement of reproductive organ incompatibility between two morphs. Researchers have identified the Primula CYP73450 gene, encoding a putative brassinosteroid-degrading enzyme, as the locus that determines style-length dimorphism. CYPA50 is present only on the short-styled S-morph haplotype, specifically expressed in S-morph styles, and its loss or inactivation leads to long styles. The gene arose via a specific duplication to the Primulaceae lineage and shows an accelerated rate of molecular evolution. The combined evidence indicates CYP50 represents the locus in the Primula heterostly supergene, whose expression in S-morph styles causes brassinoster degradation, limiting cell expansion and style elongation. Evolutionarily, CYP50 arose via lineage-specific duplication and gain of style-specific expression. The proposed duplication and gain of style-specific expression of CYP50 in the ancestor of Primulaceae likely represents the dominant shortening mutation. CYP50 is an example of an identified causal locus in a supergene underlying adaptive polymorphism, opening the way for understanding the evolutionary origin of the Primula S-loc as an iconic case of complex supergene. Heterostly evolved once in the genus Primula and the causal genes should co-segregate with morphotypes in all heterostylous species. The CYP50 presence/absence polymorphism strictly cosegregates with the morph in all heterostyl species sampled across the phylogeny and the gene is absent or inactivated in all cases of derived homostly. This supports the conclusion that CYP50 is the locus of the Primula heterosty supergene determining style-length polymorphism. Heterly in Primula is controlled by the S-loc supergene comprising five linked genes determining style, anther height, pollen size, and male and female incompatibility.
Kisspeptin and neurokin B neurons in the hypothalamic arcuate nucleus are essential for pubertal development and fertility, with kisspept neurons co-expressing kiss1, neurokin B, dynorphin, and glutamate to provide an episodic excitatory drive to gonadotropin-releasing hormone neurons. High-frequency photostimulation of kisspept neurons evokes local release of excitatory neurokin B and inhibitory dynorph peptides, synchronizing kisspept neuronal firing and causing robust excitation of gonadotropin-releasing hormone neurons through a mechanism involving glutamatergic input to preoptic kisspe neurons. Kisspept neurons play a dual role in driving episodic secretion of gonotropin-releasing hormone through differential release of peptide and amino acid neurotransmitters to coordinate reproductive function. Nerve fibers expressing kisspein are localized in the internal zone of the median eminence, and simultaneous activation of kisspept neurons generates LH release in vivo via kisspept-mediated depolarization of gonotropin-releasing nerve terminals. High-frequency photostimulation of kisspe neurons induces a neurokin B-mediated slow excitatory postsynaptic potential modulated by co-released dynorph and synchronization of kisspe neurons. Activation of kisspe neurons co-releases glutamate, exciting rostral kisspe neurons and resulting in robust excitation of gonotin-releasing neurons. Kisspe neurons communicate with kisspe neurons via glutamate, stimulating burst-firing and releasing kisspein to excite gonotin-releasing neurons via the GPR signaling cascade. Kisspe, GPR, neurokin, and gonotin-releasing hormone are required for normal fertility. High-frequency photostimulation of kisspe neurons releases neuro B, depolarizing and recruiting other kisspe neurons, with dynorph co-released and acting presynaptically to modulate neuro release, governing synchronous activity and promoting kisspe release to stimulate gonin-releasing hormone release. Kisspe neurons play a dual role driving pulsatile secretion of gonin-releasing hormone through kiss release into the external median eminence and excite kiss neurons via glutamate to generate the preovulatory surge through a di-synaptic pathway.
Bone morphogenic protein (BMP) signalling plays a crucial role in maintaining pluripotency and favours mesodermal over neural fates upon differentiation, but the mechanisms by which BMP controls differentiation are not well understood. BMP regulates differentiation by blocking downregulation of Cdh1, an event that accompanies the earliest stages of neural and mesodermal differentiation. Loss of Cdh is a requirement for differentiation of pluripotent cells and suppression of Cdh activity rescues the BMP-imposed block to differentiation. BMP acts prior to and independently of Cdh to prime pluripotent cells for mesod differentiation, thus helping to reinforce the block to neural differentiation. Differentiation depends on exposure to extrinsic cues and morphogen events that control receptivity to those cues. BMP, a key pluripency signal, feeds into this mechanism by maintaining Cdh expression which interferes with efficient neural and mesermal differentiation. Higher levels of BMP impose a posterior identity that primes cells for meserm and is incompatible with neural fate, explaining why BMP protects pluripency and promotes mesod differentiation. The loss of Cdh activity is identified as a limiting event for earliest stages of neural differentiation and BMP controls differentiation by maintaining Cdh expression. BMP target Id1 imposes proximal posterior identity on undifferentiated cells, priming them towards meserm. The ability of Id to block neural differentiation is due to a shift in positional identity of pluripotent cells in absence of overt differentiation. BMP signalling protects pluripency, suppresses neural specification, and required for meserm formation in vivo. BMP signalling contributes to maintenance of pluripency, but underlying mechanisms remain unclear, proposing BMP suppresses differentiation by maintaining Cdh expression. Suppression of Cdh rescues the BMP-imposed block to multilineage differentiation. BMP favours mesermal over neural fates by maintaining Cdh which interferes with efficient neural and mesermal differentiation and primes cells for meserm. The transition from pluripency to lineage commitment remains poorly understood, BMP signalling protects pluripency and suppresses neural specification, but mechanisms remain unclear. BMP required for meserm formation and maintains pluripency through Cdh expression.
Researchers have identified a novel mechanism by which a protein called Cep120 inhibits centriole maturation in quiescent cells, shedding light on how asymmetric properties of centrioles in the centrosome are established and maintained during quiescence. Cep120, a daughter centriole-associated ciliopathy protein, plays a critical inhibitory role at daughter centrioles, and its depletion in quiescent mouse and human cells leads to accumulation of pericentriolar material components including perentrin, CdkRap2, ninein, and Cep170. Elevated levels of these components result in increased microtubule-nucleation activity at the centrosome, causing aberrant dyne-dependent trafficking of centrosomal proteins, dispersal of centri satellites, and defective ciliary assembly and signaling. Cep120 helps maintain centrosome homeostasis by inhibiting untimely maturation of the daughter centri and defines a new molecular defect underlying pathogenesis of ciliopathies such as Jeune Asphyating Thoracic Dystrophy and Joub syndrome. Loss of Cep120 initiates changes in protein localization and abundance resulting in defective ciliary assembly, starting with changes in the maturation state of the daughter centri where Cep is preferentially enriched, disrupting ciliary signaling requiring CS-dependent delivery of essential factors to the centrosome. Cep regulates asymmetric distribution of PCM proteins at centrosome residing on the outer wall of centolar microtubules. Loss of Cep during quies is detrimental to ciliary function due to aberrant CS trafficking of proteins to centrosome, disrupting localization due to changes in PCM, microtubule organization and dyne-dependent trafficking of centrosomal proteins. Dispersal of satellites upon Cep loss disrupts cilia formation and function during quies. Findings establish sensitive control of centrosome homeostasis and relationship to primary cilium assembly and signaling, uncovering a molecular defect that underlies pathogenesis of ciliopathies such as Jeune and Jert syndrome.
Neuron-glia interactions play a critical role in the maturation of neural circuits, however, little is known about the pathways that mediate their communication in the central nervous system. Researchers investigated neuron-glia signaling in the developing retina and found that retinal waves induce calcium transients in Müller cells. During cholinergic waves, calcium transients were blocked by muscarinic acetylcholine antagonists, whereas during glutamatergic waves, calcium transients were inhibited by ionotropic glutamate antagonists, indicating the responsiveness of Müller cells changes to match the neurotransmitter used to support retinal waves. An optical glutamate sensor showed the decline in Müller cell calcium transients was caused by a reduction in the amount of glutamate reaching Müller cells. These studies indicate neurons and Müller cells exhibit correlated activity during a critical period of retinal maturation enabled by neurotransmitter spillover from retinal synapses.

Whole-cell recordings of retinal ganglion cells and two-photon imaging of calcium indicators or glutamate sensors expressed in Müller cells showed neurotransmitters released during retinal waves induce calcium transients in Müller cells. The spatio-temporal characteristics and molecular pathways by which neurons and Müller cells communicate in the developing retina were elucidated, finding retinal waves correlated with calciumients in a large proportion of Müller cells. The mechanism of interaction between neurons and Müller cells observed during cholinergic retinal waves is similar to previous findings in visual cortex and hippocampus where cholinergic stimulation leads to astrocyte transients via muscarinic accholine receptors. 

Müller cells express multiple neurotransmitter types throughout the period, allowing neuronal control of Müller activity to be sustained as synchronization shifts from cholinergic to glutamatergic signaling. The proportion of Müller cells responding to waves with calcium transients decreased significantly despite the presence of retinal waves. Müller cells express a variety of neurotransmitter receptors, including glutamatergic and cholinergic, and calcium transients at different ages could be evoked by different neurotransmitters during retinal waves. Researchers explored which transmitters modulated neuron-Müller signaling at developmental ages and found correlated activity between neurons and Müller cells during critical retinal maturation enabled by neurotransmitter spillover from retinal synapses.
Establishing the time since death is critical in every death investigation, yet existing techniques are susceptible to a range of errors. For example, forensic entomology, widely used to assess the postmortem interval (PMI), can have errors ranging from days to months. Microbes may provide a novel method for estimating PMI that avoids these limitations. Researchers have shown that postmortem microbial community changes are dramatic, measurable, and repeatable in a mouse model, allowing PMI to be estimated approximately within 3 days over 48 days. The results provide a detailed understanding of bacterial and microbial eukaryotic ecology within a decomposing corpse, suggesting microbial data can be developed into a forensic tool for estimating PMI. The study aimed to assess long-held hypotheses about the role of microbes in corpse decomposition and test whether changes in microbial communities are predictable over the decomposition timeline, crucial for using microbes as a 'clock' to estimate PMI. Data allowed understanding of the underlying microbial ecology of decomposition and feasibility of using microbes as evidence, testing whether community change is measurable and directional during decomposition for accurate estimates of past events such as PMI. Previous research demonstrated skin and mouth bacterial communities followed a consistent trajectory of change during decomposition of swine corpses over 5 days. This study expanded on initial results by characterizing microbial change in external and internal sites in destructively sampled mouse corpses over 48 days, providing a proof-of-principle demonstration that microbial ecology has potential as a forensics tool for PMI estimation. A laboratory experiment characterized temporal changes in microbial communities associated with mouse corpses decomposing on soil under controlled conditions for 48 days, using culture-independent high-throughput DNA sequencing approaches for each sample, distinguishing bacteria, archaea, and microbial eukotes. The approach differs from prior work where regression models explained variation after being fit to all data, instead providing a demonstration that microbial ecology can estimate PMI. The study shows microbial community changes are dramatic, measurable, and repeatable, allowing PMI estimation within 3 days over 48, suggesting microbial data can be developed into a forensic tool, providing a novel method for estimating PMI that avoids limitations of existing techniques.
The Hox genes are a set of developmental genes that display conserved expression patterns in the embryo and define identities along the anterior-posterior axis in bilaterian animals, exerting their function through interaction with TALE transcription factors. Hox and TALE members are present in some but not all non-bilateria phyla, raising the question of how Hox-TALE interactions evolved to provide positional information. Using proteins from unicellular and multicellular lineages, it was found that these networks emerged from an ancestral motif present in Hox and other protein families. Hox-TALE networks experienced extensive molecular innovations crucial for differentiating Hox functions along body plans. The results highlight how homeobox families evolved during eukote evolution to constitute a patterning system in Eumetazoa. Hox-TALE networks constituted an ancestral regulatory module later exploited for patterning in Bilateria, effective when interaction modes existed with duplicated Hox family members, allowing diversifying patterning functions along the axis. This system was co-opted by various contexts of embryogenesis in eumazoan phyla for axis or tissue. Hox genes are expressed along the anteroposterior axis of bilian animals, providing positional information during embryogenesis, contributing to morphological diversification of bilian organisms during evolution. Although networks rely on intricate properties, they originated from an ancestral mode kept in other homebox families. The study describes molecular cues underlying H-TA patterning in Bilateria established stepwise during eukote evolution. Developmental genes display dissimilar patterns or be absent in non-bilateria, showing genetic mechanisms are not universal. Cnidaria has a bona fide Hox repertoire, others lack H genes, raising the question how Hox acquired axial patterning during metazo evolution. In Bilateria, H patterning relies on PBC and Meis proteins, present in non-bilateria. Hox-T networks emerged from ancestral motif in H and related families, experiencing innovations for H functions along body plans, highlighting evolution of homeobox during eukote to patterning in Eumazoa.
Researchers utilized super-resolution microscopy to examine the impact of fluorophore size and photostability on AMPA receptor trafficking within and outside post-synaptic densities in rats. AMPA receptors were labeled with organic dyes, small quantum dots, or big quantum dots via different-sized linkers. Results showed over 90% of AMP receptors labeled with dyes or small quantum dots diffused in confined nanodomains within post-synaptic densities, stable for 15 minutes or longer, while less than 10% were extrasynaptic and highly mobile. Conversely, 5-10% of big quantum dot-labeled AMP receptors were in post-synaptic densities and 90-95% were extrasynaptic. The findings suggest AMP receptors rapidly enter stable nanodomains in post-synaptic densities with lifetimes over 15 minutes and do not accumulate in extrasynaptic membranes. A total of 126 synapses were observed using small quantum dots to study AMPA receptor dynamics over 15-60 minutes. Similar results were obtained using sparsely labeled small quantum dots and organic dyes on live neurons, showing AMPA receptors predominantly localized at synaptic regions. Typically one nanodomain per synapse was observed, although multiple nanodomains existed. Labeling with big quantum dots correlated with the number of days in vitro, while small quantum dots showed no accumulation of large extrasynaptic receptor pools and constrained diffusion in post-synaptic density nanodomains. AMP receptors tagged with smaller fluorophores were mostly immobilized within post-synaptic densities, 84-97%, and 5-16% freely diffused in the dendrite membrane. Big quantum dots were too large to enable AMP receptors to enter the synaptic cleft. Determining receptor location has been challenging due to synapse nanoscale size, two-dimensional imaging, and inability to distinguish intracellular versus extracellular.
Researchers have identified the mechanism underlying the earliest response of central neurons to hypoxia, an increase in voltage-gated sodium current, as SUMOylation of NaV1.2 channels. Hypoxia activates the Small Ubiquitin-like Modifier pathway in cerebellar granule neurons, leading to SUMOylation of NaV1.2 channels, which increases voltage-gated sodium current. The time course of SUMylation of single NaV1.2 channels at the surface and changes in voltage-gated sodium current coincide and are prevented by mutation of NaV1.2-Lys38 or application of a deSUMOating enzyme. Within 40 seconds, hypia-induced linkage of SUMO to channels is complete, shifting voltage dependence of channel activation, allowing depolarizing steps to evoke larger sodium currents. The SUMO pathway and NaV1.2 are potential targets for neuroprotective interventions due to their role in hypoxic brain damage. Hypia-mediated SUMylation of Na1.2 is observed in the presence of TTX, indicating sodium flux is not a prerequisite for channel SUMylation. SUM1 and SENP increase and decrease voltage-gated current under normoxic conditions. The response to hypia is ablated by µ-Conotoxin-TII, a blocker of Na1.2 channels, supporting rapid SUMylation of Na1.2 at the plasma membrane in response to hypia. Hypia directly increases interaction of native SUM1 and Na1.2. The findings suggest Na1.2 channels and SUM pathway may be valuable targets for reducing damage due to acute hypoxic insults to central neurons. Acute hypia induces SUMylation of Na1.2 on Lys38 in rat Na1.2 expressed in CHO cells with the β subunit under normoxic and hypoxic conditions. Data shows mean ± S.E.M for 10-15 cells per group. The study demonstrates SUMylation of Na1.2 at the plasma membrane underlies the increase in voltage-gated sodium in response to acute hypia in neurons and CHO cells.
Researchers have developed a mechanistic model to understand the thermal response of Ross River virus, a mosquito-borne pathogen prevalent in Australia and the Pacific Islands, to predict its transmission patterns. The model reveals that transmission peaks at moderate temperatures of 26.4°C and declines to zero at thermal limits of 17.0 and 31.5°C. The findings indicate that transmission is year-round in the tropics but seasonal in temperate areas, resulting in a nationwide peak in human cases. Climate warming is expected to increase transmission in temperate areas but decrease it in tropical areas where temperatures are already near the thermal optimum. The study emphasizes the importance of nonlinear models to infer the role of temperature in disease dynamics and predict responses to climate change. As the climate warms, understanding temperature effects on mosquito-borne disease transmission is critical, particularly with new pathogens emerging worldwide. Identifying thermal optima and limits by characterizing nonlinear responses can accurately predict geographic, seasonal, and interannual variation in disease. Thermal responses vary among diseases and vector species, yet mechanistic models based on empirical unimodal responses are lacking for many diseases and vectors. The study parameterized a temperature-dependent model for Ross River virus transmission using data from two vector species, Cx annirostris and Ae vigilax. The results support a general pattern of intermediate thermal optima for transmission where optimal temperature is driven by adult mosquito lifespan but upper and lower limits are uncertain. The strongest signal of temperature on disease is expected in temperate regions where mean temperature varies along the rising portion of the curve. Upper thermal limits of mosquito-borne transmission are determined by vector life traits with symmetrical performance curves like fecundity and survival rather than rate-based traits with asymmetrical curves like biting or pathogen development. The study highlights the significance of nonlinear models in predicting disease dynamics and responses to climate change.
Motor cortical beta oscillations have been observed for decades, yet their behavioral correlates remain unresolved. To investigate how changes in population activity, beta oscillations, and behavior are linked, researchers recorded multi-scale neural activity from motor cortex while macaques performed a neurofeedback task. Subjects brought their beta oscillatory power to an instructed state and executed an arm reach. Reaches preceded by reduction in beta power exhibited faster movement times than reaches by increase in power. Population activity was found to shift from a movement state during oscillations neuro-induced or naturally during tasks. This establishes a neural basis for slowed movement following periods of oscillatory activity. Notably, this shift in population seen in naturally occurring oscillations during tasks, suggesting oscillations reflect common signature of spiking patterns different contexts. The study ties existing works on behavioral correlates of oscillations with hypotheses of motor encodes onset through population-level activity. Volitionally increasing and decreasing cortical power with neurofeedback precedes slower and faster movement times, supporting the hypothesis that oscillations linked to patterns slowing new movements. Results add to previously findings of elevated power prior and after well-trained movements. Behavioral effect from neuro-induced oscillations matches hypotheses claiming movement-slowing role during natural movements. Individual responses during activity were different across tasks. Underlying motor population shifts from threshold when producing oscillations in task or naturally in task. Researchers analyzed units exhibiting activity during neuro-induced oscillations compared natural during reaching, despite differences at unit level, population show oscillations promote consistent movement-slow state both tasks, matching observed behavior. The study recorded neural activity from motor cortex while macaques performed a novel neurofeedback task and found that changes in population activity, beta oscillations, and behavior are linked. The findings suggest that beta oscillations reflect a common signature of spiking patterns even in different contexts and provide a neural basis for slowed movement following periods of oscillatory activity. The results support the hypothesis that beta oscillations are linked to neural patterns that slow the onset of new movements and add to previously reported findings of elevated power prior to and after well-trained movements. The study ties existing works on behavioral correlates of oscillations with hypotheses of how the motor cortex encodes movement through the lens of population-level activity, providing new insights into the role of beta oscillations in motor control.
TRAF2, a component of the TNF superfamily signaling complexes, plays a crucial role in regulating immune cell homeostasis. TR2 deficient mice die at birth, limiting understanding of its role in adult tissues. The function of TR2 RING is debated, with some claiming it cannot function as a ubiquitin E3 ligase, while others argue it requires sphingosine-1-phosphate, generated by sphingine kinase 1, to function as E3 ligase. Keratinocyte-specific deletion of Traf2, but not sph1 deficiency, disrupts TNF mediated NF-κB and MAPK signaling, causing epidermal hyperplasia and psoriatic inflammation. This inflammation is driven by TNF, cell death, non-canonical NF-κB, and the adaptive immune system, representing a clinically relevant model of psoriasis. TR2 has tissue-specific functions independent of sph1. Absence of TR2 in keratinocytes in vivo results in psoriasis-like epidermal hyperplasia and inflammation. Deleting Tnf and Nf2 genes reverses the inflammatory phenotype, highlighting TR2's role in protecting keratinocytes from death and downregulating inflammatory responses. TR2-deficient keratinocytes produce inflammatory cytokines via non-canonical NF-κ pathway, recruiting immune cells, including neutrophils and IFN+ cells, generating a psoriasis-like phenotype with slower onset. Primary keratinocytes isolated from T2KO and Sk1 mice show TN signaling delayed and weakened in T2 deficient, but not Sk deficient keratinocytes. T2 deficient keratinocytes are sensitive to TN-induced death, associated with cFLPL reduction and caspase cleavage. Concomitant deletion of Tnf in T2 deficient keratin breaks the cycle, preventing apoptosis and psoriasis onset. TR2 regulates TN and NF-κ signaling to suppress apoptosis and inflammation independently of sphing kinase 1.
Synaptic vesicles are specialized organelles that store and secrete non-peptide neurotransmitters at synapses, and following exocytosis, their membranes are rapidly recaptured by endocytosis and recycled for the generation of new fusion-competent vesicles. Research has shown that clathrin-mediated endocytosis plays a key role in this endytic traffic, but the mechanisms underlying process is poorly understood. A recent study used synapses of dynamin 1 and 3 double-knockout neurons, where clathrin-mediated endocytosis is impaired, to gain insight into this process. The results showed that massive formation of large endocytic vacuoles, or bulkosomes, was not defective but rather enhanced in the absence of dynamin 1 and 3. The conversion of bulkosomes into synaptic vesicles was not accompanied by the accumulation of clathrin-coated buds on their surface and proceeded even after clathrin knockdown, suggesting its independence of clathrin. These findings support the existence of a pathway for synaptic vesicle reformation that bypasses the requirement for clathrin and dynamin 1 and 3 that operates during intense synaptic activity. The study found that neither dynamin 1 nor dyn 3 is required for bulk endocytosis and their absence delays but does not abolish synaptic vesicle formation from internal membranes. The results are based on studies of dynamin 1 3 double-knockout synapses and ultrastructural analysis of the time course of synaptic vesicle recovery from bulkosomes in presence or near depletion of clath. The study contradicts previous evidence that bulk endocytosis in response to strong stimulus is dynamin 1 3 dependent and proposed an important role for clath in synaptic vesicle budding from endosomes.
