Overall, the study demonstrates a clathrin and dynamin-independent pathway of synaptic vesicle recycling mediated by bulk endocytosis that operates during intense synaptic activity.
Researchers conducted a randomized, single-blind, crossover study to identify and validate biomarkers of glucocorticoid action in 10 subjects with primary adrenal insufficiency. The study involved physiological glucortic exposure and withdrawal, with no serious adverse events reported except mild physical and mental fatigue and salt craving. Integrated multi-omic analysis of peripheral blood mononuclear cells, adipose tissue, plasma miRNA, and serum metabolomics revealed a transcriptomic profile and multi-omic cluster predictive of glucortic exposure. A microRNA, miR-122-5p, correlated with genes and metabolites regulated by glucortic exposure, was identified and replicated in independent studies with varying glucortic exposure. The findings provide a basis for the discovery of biomarkers to measure glucortic effects, allowing for individualized and optimized glucortic therapy, and shed light on diseases related to unphysiological glucortic exposure, such as cardiovascular disease and obesity. The study utilized a multi-omic strategy combining gene expression in circulation and metabolic tissue, integrated with microRNAs and metabolites to identify putative biomarkers, which were then replicated in independent groups with different glucortic exposure. A transcriptomic profile similar in peripheral blood monuclear cells and adipose tissue was validated by comparison to published data, and miR122-5p correlated with circulating transcriptome and metabolome, suggesting a functional role in glucortic action. Two subjects did not show the miR122-5p response, experiencing mild fatigue during glucortic withdrawal. The study was funded by the Swedish Council, Swedish government, Endocrin Association, Wellcome Trust, Medical Council UK, Eva’s Foundation, Copenhagen Hospital, and Danish Rheumat Association.
The dynamics of mTORC1 activation in response to amino acids involves a complex process where amino acids activate mTOR1 via a complex containing RAG GTPases, RAGULATOR, and vacuolar ATPase, leading to the translocation of mTOR1 to lysosomes, an obligatory step for activation. Live imaging of the mTOR1 component RAPTOR and a cell-permeant fluorescent analogue of di-leucine methyl ester revealed that translocation to lysosomes is a transient event, occurring within 2 minutes of amino acid addition and peaking within 5 minutes. This event is temporally coupled with fluorescent leucine appearance in lysosomes and sustained in comparison to amino acid stimulation. Sestr2 and vacuolar ATPase are negative and positive regulators of mTOR1 activity. Phosphorylation of canonical mTOR targets is delayed compared to lysosomal translocation, suggesting a dynamic and transient passage of mTOR1 from the lysosomal surface before targeting its substrates elsewhere. Leucine has a prominent role in mTOR activation and leuc methyl ester that enters cells and accumulates in lysosomes can activate mTOR on its own. The lysosome is a crucial for m activation but the dynamics of this process and how mTOR1 activity is connected to lysosomal residence may be more complicated than current models. Expressing subunits of mTOR tagged with GFP to follow localization in live cells revealed a sustained translocation of the complex owing to its property to continuously accumulate within the lysosomal lumen. Amino acids ultimately activate mTOR in the interior and more than one type of sensing and acid is involved, with leuc sensed by Sestr1/2 and arginine entering lysosomes via SLC38 important for mTOR activation.
Research has shown that the modification of antigens with specific glycan structures can alter their intracellular routing in dendritic cells, influencing the priming of T cells. The study focused on the modification of Ovalbumin (OVA) with the glycan-structure LewisX (LeX), which directs OVA to the C-type lectin receptor MGL. This modification favored Th1 skewing of CD4+ cells and enhanced cross-priming of CD8 cells. Notably, cross-presentation of native OVA requires high antigen dose and TLR stimuli, whereas Le modification reduces the required amount 100-fold and obviates its dependence on TLR signaling. The OVA-LeX-induced enhancement of T cell cross-priming is M1-dependent, demonstrated by reduced CD8 effector T cell frequencies in MGL-deficient mice. MGL-mediated crosspresentation of OVA-LeX neither required TAP transporters nor Cathepsin and was observed after prolonged intracellular storage in Rab11+LAMP+ compartments. Glycosylation of antigens has a major influence on antigen uptake and compartmentalization, affecting presentation and the strength of the immune response. Modification of OVA with LeX glycans directs OVA towards MGL, skewing naive CD4 cell differentiation towards IFN-producing Th1 cells. Immunization with OVA-LeX increased CD8 T responses in vivo, with expression of murine MGL on BM-DCs and CD11+ sps analyzed by flow cytometry. Conjugation of LeX to OVA alters routing from MR and TAP-dependent cross-presentation into a TAP and Catheps-independent pathway, devoid of TLR dependence, enhancing CD8 cell priming and Th1 skewing CD4 cells. Crosspresentation is associated with prolonged storage in Rab11LAMP vesicles. Targeting OVA to MGL through Le conjugation enhances crosspresentation, increasing OVA-specific CD8 effector cells in vitro and in vivo. Importantly, MGL-dependent crosspresentation occurs at low antigen dose and independently of TLR, without TAP and Catheps. Using M1KO mice lacking MGL, it was ascertained M1 is the lectin involved in boosting antigen-specific effector cells, with no enhanced frequencies of OVA-specific IFN and TNα-producing cells detected upon immunization with Le-conjugated OVA. Overall, controlled glycosylation of antigens can influence intracellular routing, the nature and strength of immune responses, and should be considered for optimizing vaccination strategies.
Burkholderia thailandensis utilizes acyl-homoserine lactone-mediated quorum sensing to regulate hundreds of genes, including those involved in cell-cell-dependent type VI secretion (T6) toxin-immunity systems. Research demonstrates that T6 is required to constrain the proliferation of quorum sensing mutants in cocultures of a BtaI quorum-sensing receptor mutant and its parent. However, the BtaI mutant is not constrained and outcompetes its parent in broth culture due to the lack of cell contact and metabolic cost associated with quorum gene activation. The increased fitness of the wild type over the Bta mutant during surface growth is dependent on an intact TSS-1 apparatus. Quorum sensing activates B thailand TSS1 growth inhibition, serving to police and constrain quorum-sensing mutants, defining a novel role for TSS in intraspecies control. Evidence shows QS control of T6-mediated toxicity and immunity allows QS competent B thailand colonies to police against Bta QS mutants. In planktonic growth where T6 does not function due to limited contact, Bta mutants have a substantial advantage over wild cells due to the cost of Bta-dependent activities. A mechanism is described where wild populations mitigate the cost by hindering QS mutant growth via regulation of cell-dependent toxin and immunity, a form of microbial policing where QS-proficient bacteria outcompete signal nonoperators in the absence of T cell growth inhibition. Policing of social deviants has been reported in Myxococcus xanthus and Pseudomonas aeruginosa, with this study describing a novel mechanism for limiting quorum mutant growth in bacterial groups. Several functions have been ascribed to T6, with B thailand T-mediated killing active against Proteobacteria and important for interspecies competition. Results demonstrate TSS-mediated killing does not occur in liquid cocultures. Quorum sensing often controls factors benefiting cells in groups and cooperative activities, commonly mediated by acyl-hoserine lactone signals made by LuxI synthases that diffuse in and out of cells. The parent with an intact QS system expresses quorum-activated TSS toxin and immunity, whereas the Bta mutant does not fully induce TSS effectors and factors.
Research has shown that long-range coupling within prefrontal-hippocampal networks is crucial for cognitive performance and emerges early in life, with discontinuous hippocampal theta bursts proposed to drive the generation of neonatal prefrontal oscillations. To elucidate the cellular substrate of these interactions, glutamatergic projection neurons in the CA1 area of either dorsal or intermediate/ventral hippocampus were selectively targeted at neonatal age using optogenetic manipulation. Results indicate that despite stronger theta and ripples in the dorsal hippocampus, the prefrontal cortex is mainly coupled with intermediate/ventral hippocampus by phase-locking neuronal firing via direct axonal projections. Theta band-confined activation by pyramidal neurons in intermediate/ventral but not dorsal CA1 boosts prefront oscillations. The temporal delay of 10-20 ms between prefront and hippocal spike trains and the directionality of information flow between the two areas suggest hippocal drives the oscillatory entrainment of local circuits in the prefrontal cortex. Data provide causal evidence that theta-specific activation of pyramidal neurons in the CA1 area of intermediate and ventral elicits broad oscillations in the prefrontal cortex via axonal projections. Tight coupling synchrony and directed flow from hippocal CA1 to prefrontal suggest the hippocampus acts as a drive for prelimbic activation at neonatal age. Differences between dorsal and intermediate/ventral communication argue for a driving force along the septo-temporal hippocamp axis. The study reveals that sharp wave ripples from intermediate/ventral hippocal correlate with pronounced firing and entrainment in the prefrontal cortex of neonatal mice. Anatomical substrate of coupling between hippocal subdivisions and prefrontal cortex was identified by monitoring projections from CA1 to prefrontal cortex. Optogenetic manipulation of glutamatergic neurons in CA1 of intermediate/ventral hippocampus boosts prefrontal oscillations in neonatal mice, demonstrating the cellular origin of long-range coupling in the developing brain.
Dynamic post-translational modification of RNA polymerase II (RNAPII) plays a crucial role in coordinating the co-transcriptional recruitment of enzymatic complexes that regulate chromatin states and processing of nascent RNA. The C-terminal domain (CTD) of the largest RAPI subunit is composed of multiple heptapeptide repeats with a consensus sequence that includes serine residues, which undergo extensive phosphorylation. Serine-5 and Ser-7 phosphorylation mark transcription initiation, while Ser-2 phosphorylation coincides with productive elongation. However, in vertebrates, the CTD has non-canonical substitutions of Ser-7 into Lys-7, which can be acetylated. This study describes the discovery of mono- and di-methylation of CTD Lys-7 residues (Kme1 and Kme2), observed during the earliest stages of transcription, preceding or accompanying Ser-5 and Ser-7 phosphorylation. In contrast, Kac is associated with RNAPI elongation, Ser-2 phosphorylation, and mRNA expression. An unexpected balance between RNAPI K7 methylation and acetylation at promoters fine-tunes gene expression levels.

RNAPI binds to promoter regions in a hypophosphorylated state before the CTD becomes phosphorylated at Ser-5 and Ser7, marking the earliest stages of transcription. Productive elongation is characterized by an increase in phosphorylation of Ser2 throughout gene bodies. Novel antibodies specific to CTD-Kme1 and CTDme2 show these modifications precede or accompany phosphorylation of S5 and S7, upstream of S2 phosphorylation. CTD methylation is present at the promoters of genes productively transcribed into mature RNA, defining the earliest stages of the transcription cycle. Mutation of CTD7 residues is compatible with viability of mouse fibroblasts, and global serine phosphorylation and RNAPI localization are independent of the presence or absence of K residues. Acylation of CTD7 is associated with inducible expression.

Mono and di-methylation of CTD7 is detected primarily in hypophorylated forms of RPB, suggesting CTD methylation is associated with early stages of transcription. The extent of productive transcription at active genes with RNAPI recruitment to promoters is modulated at the level of CTD7 modifications. Methylation is associated with less productive transcription, while K acylation is most enriched at the highest expressed genes. The detection of K1 and K2 increases with the number of K residues in mammalian CTD cell lines, flanked by different amino acids, supports this conclusion. Overall, the findings suggest a balance between RNAPI K7 methylation and acylation at promoters fine-tunes gene expression levels, with methylation associated with early transcription stages and acylation with elongation and expression.
Skeletal muscle satellite cells remain quiescent in their niche but exit quiescence upon muscle injury to proliferate and repair tissue and self-renew to replenish the satellite population. To understand the mechanisms maintaining satellite quiescence, researchers identified differentially expressed transcripts during satellite activation following injury and found RNA binding proteins, including Tristetrapin, to be significantly changed. Tristrapin promotes decay of MyoD mRNA, a transcriptional regulator of myogenic commitment, via binding to the 3' untranslated region. Upon activation, p38/β MAPK phosphorylates and inactivates Tristrapin, stabilizing MyD mRNA. Satellite-specific knockdown of Tristrapin preciously activates cells in vivo, enabling MyD accumulation, differentiation, and cell fusion into myofibers. Regulation of mRNAs by Tristrapin functions as a critical post-transcriptional regulatory mechanism controlling satellite homeostasis. MyD upregulates its transcription, and muscle injury triggers a rapid feed-forward loop resulting in MyD expression driving satellite cells to commit to myoblasts. TTP may be involved in downregulation of MyD mRNA and other TTP-target transcripts by myoblasts during quiescent population formation or reacquisition following injury. Inhibition of TTP by p38/ MAP initiates a circuit committing satellite cells to myogenic lineage. Muscle regeneration requires rapid exit from quies but also renewal of quiescent population upon completion. TTP suppresses transcripts for activation as a mechanism for immediate exit quies. Upon injury, p38 MAP is activated, phosphorylating TTP. Transcripts encoding proteins regulating mRNAs, including mRNA binding proteins in skeletal diseases, support post-transcription regulation of RNA in muscle homeostasis. The involvement of p38 MAP signaling in satellite activation, division, and loss of function during aging indicates TTP may be required for quies. TTP is present in quiescent satellite, inhibited by p38 MAP signaling, and reduces MyD mRNA half-life. Loss of TTP from muscle should preciously activate satellite, promoting MyD expression and aberrant cell entry. The p38-dependent phosphorylation of T upon activation suggests T plays a role maintaining quies. T inactivation preceding MyD upregulation is consistent with T suppressing pro-myogenic targets.
Researchers investigated the role of interleukin-10 receptor (IL-10R) in regulating lamina propria macrophage function during infant development in mice and its impact on preventing colitis. IL-10 deficient mice did not exhibit signs of colitis during the first two weeks of life, but developed intestinal inflammation and macroage dysfunction during the third week, concurrent with weaning and diversification of the intestinal microbiota. However, IL-10 did not directly regulate microbial ecology during infant development. Macrophage depletion with clodronate inhibited colitis development, while absence of IL-10 on macroages sensitized mice to colitis, indicating IL-10 regulation of macroage function during the postnatal period is crucial for preventing murine colitis. Time-series experiments using IL-10 deficient mice evaluated histology, inflammatory gene expression, cellular composition, and luminal bacterial communities from birth to 14 weeks. Clodronate depletion investigated the role of colonic macrophages in colonic inflammation in IL-10 deficient mice, suggesting a direct effect of IL-10 signaling on microbiome is unlikely to be responsible for colitis. Data demonstrated a requirement for macroage-intrinsic IL-10 signaling during a critical developmental transition, which has implications for understanding colitis initiation in infants with IL-10 signaling deficiencies. The IL-10/IL pathway is essential for intestinal mucosal homeostasis and prevention of inflammation in infancy. Modeling infant-onset colitis in mice lacking functional IL-10 signaling could lead to advances in understanding etiopathogenesis of these diseases, as IL-10 deficient and wild-type mice exhibit differences in microbial diversity and composition. The study highlights the importance of IL-10 in regulating macrophage function during early development to prevent colitis.
The Tudor domain protein SIM-1 plays a crucial role in promoting siRNA production at piRNA-targeted mRNAs in Caenorhabditis elegans, acting downstream of piRNA production and upstream of mutator complex-dependent siRNA biogenesis. SIM1 localizes to distinct subcellular foci adjacent to P granules and Mutator foci, two phase-separated condensates that are the sites of piRNA-dependent mRNA recognition and mutator-dependent siRNA amplification. This suggests a role for multiple perinuclear condensates in organizing the piRNA pathway and promoting mRNA regulation by the mutator complex. SIM1 is required at a step between the piRNA pathway to the mutator, functioning to bridge the gap between primary and secondary phases of the C eleg piRNA silencing pathway. The protein localizes to cytoplasmic foci near Pules, granules, and Mutator foci, implicating a series of distinct perinuclear condates in the regulation of mAs by the piRNA and mutator. SIM1 acts downstream of PRG-1 to mediate the production of secondary siAs by the mutator, suggesting a role for multiple perinuclear condates to promote mRNA regulation by the piRNA and mutator. In C elegans, a small RNA amplification pathway dependent on the mutator synthesizes secondary siAs from pi-targeted mRNAs to trigger heritable silencing. Several proteins localize at or near P granules, including GLH-1-2-3-4, DDX19, LAF1, MUT14, and VBH1, potentially serving a similar function to RDE12 in shuttling pi-targeted mAs into SIM1 foci and to Mut foci. Numerous proteins have been identified in C elegans required for piRNA transcription, trimming, and modification. The mutator complex, which includes an RNA-dependent RNA polymerase, synthesizes high levels of siAs through the activity of an RNA-dependent RNA polymerase, regulating transposons and other germline genes in C elegans. The steps between mRNA recognition by piRNA and si amplification by the mut complex were unknown, but the identification of SIM1 provides insight into the regulation of pi-target genes mediated by the mut complex. Overall, SIM1 is a factor that acts downstream of PRG1 to mediate the production of secondary siAs by the mutator, and suggests a role for multiple perinuclear condensates to promote mRNA regulation by the pi and mut complex.
Researchers have investigated the properties of Purkinje neurons in zebrafish larvae, which are central to cerebellar function and exhibit bistability when recorded in vitro or in vivo under anesthesia. By recording intracellularly from Purje neurons in unanesthetized larval zebish, the study demonstrates bistability in these neurons, with tonic firing seen in depolarized regimes and bursting at hyperpolarized membrane potentials. Purje neurons can switch from one state to another spontaneously or with current injection. GABA or NMDA receptors are not required for bursting, but activation of AMPA receptors by climbing fibers is sufficient to trigger bursts. The initiation of motor spiking is correlated with increased incidence of EPSPs and depolarization. Developmentally, bistability is observed soon after Purje neuron specification and persists at least until late larval stages. Two kinds of glutamatergic excitation are observed: strong excitation from climbing fibers and weak excitation from parallel fibers, both mediated by AMPA receptors. The study demonstrates bistability in Purje neurons of zebish larvae and shows that state changes are dependent on AMPA-mediated climbing fiber inputs. Purje neurons can be in tonic or bursting mode and toggle between modes. The conservation of structural and functional properties of the cerebellum suggests bistability is a conserved feature of Purje neurons from fish to mammals. The intracellular recordings from zebish Purje neurons confirm narrow spikes are sodium-dependent potentials, broad spikes are calcium-mediated, and large events are EPSPs. The study provides evidence that Purje neurons exhibit bistability in vivo and AMP receptor-mediated synaptic excitation drives state-dependent bursting in Purkin neurons of zebfish larvae.
Researchers have developed a multiscale model to study the principles of self-organization and adaptation of the actin cytoskeleton during clathrin-mediated endocytosis, demonstrating that a minimal branched actin network is sufficient to internalize endocytic pits against membrane tension. The model shows that around 200 activated Arp2/3 complexes are required for robust internalization, which is consistent with experimental findings that approximately 200 Arp/3 complexes assemble at sites of clathrin-mediated endocytosis in human cells. Simulations predict that actin self-organizes into a radial array with growing ends oriented toward the base of the pit, with long actin filaments bending between attachment sites in the coat and the base of the pit, storing elastic energy that contributes to endocytic internalization. Elevated membrane tension directs more filaments toward the base of the pit, increasing actin nucleation and bending for increased force production, allowing spatially constrained actin assembly to utilize an adaptive mechanism enabling endocytosis under varying physical constraints. Cells with low membrane tension can carry out endocytosis with low Arp2/3 complex activity, while actin network self-organization arising from the Hip1 distribution around the pit and filament bending allows the endytic actin network to change organization and force-producing capacity under elevated loads. The integrated model combines membrane mechanics with filament-based actin polymerization to develop a multiscale model of actin in mammalian endocytosis, demonstrating that a minimal actin network composed of actin, Arp2/3 complex, and capping protein can generate force to internalize endocytic pits against mammalian plasma tension. The actin self-organization and bending arise from the distribution of actin-coat attachments around the curved endocytic pit, allowing the actin network to adapt to changing loads, and incorporating the experimentally determined Ar2/3 molecule number into the multiscale model reveals the spatial organization of actin and force capacity of the endytic network.
Macropinocytosis is a cellular mechanism that allows cells to take up extracellular liquid into large vesicles, critically dependent on the formation of a ring of protrusive actin beneath the plasma membrane, which develops into a macinocytic cup. Research on Dictyostelium shows that macinocytic cups are organized around intense patches of PIP3, active Ras, and active Rac, with these signaling patches associated with a ring of active SCAR/WAVE at their periphery. The formation of these patches does not depend on the enclosing F-actin ring and becomes enlarged when RasGAP1 is mutated, indicating Ras plays an instructive role. New macinocytic cups predominantly form by splitting from existing ones, suggesting cup-shaped plasma structures form from self-organizing patches of active Ras/PIP, recruiting a ring of actin nucleators to their periphery. 

Macropinos develop from cup-shaped projections of the plasma membrane, driven outwards by actin polymerization, containing a central patch of activated Ras and PIP throughout their life, associated with a ring of active SC at the edge. The ring of active SC is proposed to be recruited by the patch, driving a hollow of F-act to extend the walls of the macinocytic cup. Using lattice light microscopy, spatial and temporal control of actin regulators such as SCAR and WASP with respect to signaling molecules including PIP and Ras were mapped, leading to a new hypothesis for the formation of cups from the plasma. F-act in de novo macinosomes is closely associated with signaling, as illustrated by active Ras, with circular ruffles persisting on the surface before closing or regressing into the cell. Closure can be abrupt, involving the inward collapse of the rim. 

Ras activity stimulates macinocytosis in mammalian and Dictostel cells, with macinocytic cups associated with an intense domain of PIP essential for function. However, recent work shows ForG contributes to the base of the macinocytic cup but not the extending lip, suggesting SC might be moved to the periphery of Ras/PIP patches by myosin-1 motors. Early work showed myos-1 is genetically important for macinocytosis in Dictostel. Overall, macinocytic cups in Dictyostel are organized around intense patches of PIP, active Ras, and Rac, associated with a ring of active SC/WAVE at their periphery, with Ras playing an instructive role and new cups forming by splitting from existing ones.
The cohesin ring plays a crucial role in holding newly replicated sister chromatids together until their separation at anaphase, and its loading onto DNA is dependent on the Scc2/Scc4 complex. Proper cohesin loading is essential for cell division, and defects in this process can lead to chromosome missegregation and aberrant transcriptional regulation, resulting in severe developmental defects in multicellular organisms. The structure of Scc in complex with an N-terminal fragment of S2 has been determined, revealing that S4 is a TPR array that envelops an extended S2 peptide. A conserved patch on the surface of S4 is required to recruit S2/4 to centromeres and build pericentromeric cohesion, demonstrating the role of S4 in determining cohes localization. The findings establish a molecular basis for S2/4 recruitment to centromeres and show that S4 targets cohes loading to specific genomic loci, separable from its role in establishing sister chromatid cohesion across the genome. Cohesin, a ring-shaped protein, wraps around sister chromatids and is the molecular agent of sister chromatid cohesion, which persists from DNA replication until anaphase. A distinct complex containing S2 and S4 initiates cohesin linkage with DNA. The structure of S4 bound to a minimal fragment of S2 has been used to derive separation of function alleles that uncouple cohes loading from cohes targeting centrom. Experiments demonstrate cohes targeting in yeast depends on S4, consistent with genetic evidence for an S4-dependent cohes localization pathway. Perturbation of the conserved patch eliminates centrom localization of S2 in mitotic cells and reduces association of cohes subunit with centrom and pericentere, but not chromosome arms. S2 localization is observed in cells progressing through S phase, the stage at which cohes loading is initiated. The pathway targets cohes to centrom requires S4 residues that are in some cases invariant across eukaryotes, suggesting the S4-dependent cohes targeting is a general feature of control of cohes loading, consistent with previous reports.
Being born late in the active season has significant implications for growth, fattening, torpor use, winter survival, and fecundity in hibernators. A study on garden dormice (Eliomys quercinus) investigated differences in growth, fattening, energetic responses, winter survival, and fecundity between early-born (EB) and late-born (LB) juveniles. LB juveniles grew and gained mass twice as fast as EB individuals. Torpor use was low during intensive growth but increased during pre-hibernation fattening. LB juveniles showed higher torpor use, reached similar body sizes, but lower fat content than EB individuals before hibernation. LB individuals showed similar hibernation patterns but a higher proportion of breeders during the following year than EB dormice. The results suggest torpor is incompatible with growth but promotes fattening and consolidates pre-hibern fat deposits. In garden dormice, being late in the reproductive season is associated with a fast life history. The study hypothesized that LB dormice grow and fatten at higher rates due to higher torpor use and reach similar pre-hibern levels of body size but lower body content, leading to differences in hibern patterns and mass overwinter compared to EB. Food availability affected the rate of growth, pre-hibern fattening, tor use of energy, body size, and fat prior hibern, overwinter hibern patterns, and subsequent fecundity in juvenile dormice. The pre-hibern fat reserves of LB were sufficient to fuel their shorter winter duration due to a five-week delay of hibern start. Extensive torpor seemed incompatible with structural growth but supports pre-h fattening and preservation of reserves prior hibern, especially for EB that reached higher fat levels prior to hibern. LB delayed onset of hibern by 5 weeks. LB dormice grow twice as fast as EB, reaching lower fat prior hibern, using short torpor to minimize energetic challenges during certain parts of the day.
Researchers have investigated the role of ADAR enzymes in the nematode worm Caenorhabditis elegans, specifically the only A-to-I editing enzyme ADR-2, to understand its impact on neural function and behavior. ADAR enzymes alter gene expression by catalyzing adenosine to inosine RNA editing and binding to regulatory elements in target RNAs, and their loss affects neuronal function in all studied animals. C elegans lacking ADAR exhibit reduced chemotaxis, but the responsible targets for this phenotype remain unknown. An unbiased assessment of ADR2 effects on the neural transcriptome identified 736 A-to-I editing events across the neural transcriptome using publicly available software SOR. Intersecting the neural editome with adr2 associated gene changes revealed an edited mRNA, cle-41, whose expression is dependent on deamination. Restoring cle-41 expression in adr2 deficient neural cells rescued the chemaxis defect, providing evidence that neuronal phenotypes of AD mutants can be caused by altered gene expression. The study indicates aberrant behavioral phenotypes caused by lack of C elegans adr2 are a result of altered gene expression rather than recoding events. This is the first study to link a phenotype associated with loss of C elegans AD enzymes to editing-dependent regulation of a specific gene. Combining neural cell isolation with RNA sequencing and editing site detection with SOR identified over 730 A-to editing sites in the C eleg neural transcriptome, including 104 edited genes. Previous studies have shown worms lacking adr2 exhibit defects in chemotaxis, yet the underlying mechanism has yet to be determined. The neuralome identified was queried for genes involved in chemotaxis or locomotion to elucidate ADR target mRNAs important for neural function. The study provides the first analysis of tissue-specific role of ADR2 in A-to editing and regulating gene expression in C elegans and suggests changes may not observed in whole worm analysis. Restoring cle41 expression in adr2 deficient cells rescued chemotaxis defect, providing first evidence that neuronal phenotypes of AD mutants can be caused by altered gene expression.
Influenza A virus (IAV) employs a strategy known as host shutoff to repress cellular mRNA translation while allowing the efficient translation of viral mRNAs. To explore the mechanisms utilized by IAV to induce host shutoff, RNA sequencing and ribosome profiling were used. The results show that viral transcripts are not preferentially translated and the decline in cellular protein synthesis is mediated by viral takeover of the mRNA pool. Measurements reveal strong variability in the levels of cellular reduction, with short transcripts being less affected by IAV. mRNAs refractory to IAV infection are enriched in maintenance processes such as oxidative phosphorylation. Continuous oxidative phosphorylation activity is important for viral propagation. The analysis indicates that viral translation efficiencies fall within the range of host translation, suggesting that direct co-opting of translation machinery does not play a dominant role in cellular shutoff. Instead, host shutoff stems from viral dominance of the mRNA pool. At 8 hours post-infection, viral mRNAs take over 53% of translation activity and 57.3% of mRNAs in cells are viral. The reduction in cellular expression is the main cause of host shutoff during infection. Various mechanisms contribute to observed shutoff, including cap-snatching cellular pre-mRNAs, inhibition of pre-mRN polyadenylation, and PA-X endonuclease activity. The findings concur that NS1 is required for shutoff of host protein production and PA-Xonuclease plays a dominant role in host shutoff during IAV infection. The results advance understanding of IAV-induced shutoff, suggesting a mechanism that facilitates translation of genes with housekeeping functions. Viruses utilize host shutoff to eliminate competition from cellular transcripts, achieved by co-opting translation machinery or takeover of mRNA pool. The study demonstrates that IAV takeover of mRNA pool is the primary mechanism for host shutoff, allowing efficient translation of viral mAs while repressing cellular translation.
Gradients of signaling proteins are essential for inducing tissue morphogenesis, but the mechanisms of gradient formation remain poorly understood. Researchers characterized the distribution of fluorescently-tagged signaling proteins, FGF and FGFR, expressed at physiological levels from genomic knock-in alleles in Drosophila, and found that FGF produced in the larval wing imaginal disc moves to the air-sac-primordium through FGFR-containing cytonemes that extend from the air-sac-primium to contact the wing disc. The number of FGF-receiving cytonemes extended by air-sac-primium cells decreases gradually with increasing distance from the source, generating a recipient-specific FGF gradient. Acting as a morphogen in the air-sac-primium, FGF activates concentration-dependent gene expression, inducing pointed1 at higher and cut at lower levels. The transcription-factors Pointed1 and Cut antagonize each other and differentially regulate formation of FGFR-containing cyton, creating regions with higher-to-lower numbers of F-receiving cyton. This study aimed to address how cyton-mediated cell-cell communication creates long-range signals to regulate tissue morphogenesis by characterizing the gradient formation of Drosila F family protein, Branchless. In Drosila, Branchless is the primary signal guiding branching morphogenesis of tracheal epithelial tubes and is traditionally thought to function as a diffusible signal forming a gradient by passive extracellular dispersion. However, findings showed that cyton-dependent signaling provides precision and adaptability in shaping long-range positional gradients and tissue architectures in space and time. The shape of the Branchless gradient dynamically adapts to diverse morphologies through feedback regulation of cyton-mediated transport. In the Drosila larval air-sac-primium, the wing-disc source is spatially separated from the recipient epithelium, and the number of F-receiving cyton extended by air-sac-prim cells decreases with increasing distance from the source. The transcription factors Pointed1 and Cut regulate the formation of FG-containing cyton as a function of the levels of signal received, self-generating gradients adopting recipient-specific shapes. These results reveal a mechanism where morphogens self-generate precise tissue-specific contours through feedback regulation of cyton-mediated dispersion, providing a new understanding of how local cell-cell communication creates long-range signals to regulate tissue morphogenesis.
α-Helical coiled coils are a well-understood protein fold, with a backbone that can be described by parametric equations, allowing for manipulation in detail. However, the insertion of two or six residues into the underlying heptad repeat of a parallel trimer coiled coil leads to the formation of a new type of fiber. These insertions strain the supercoil, causing the formation of short β-strands, which move the path of the chain by 120° around the trimer axis, resulting in an α/β coiled coil retaining one hydrogen bond per unit from the parent coiled coil. α-Helical coiled coils are found in various structural and functional contexts and were the first protein described in atomic detail, with a backbone computable by parametric equations, placing them at the forefront of protein design efforts. Insertions of 2 and 6 residues cause strain on α-helical geometry, breaking α-helices to form short β-strands, which associate into a triangular supersecondary structure, the β-layer. Removing α-hical residues results in a minimalistic α/β coiled coil built of hexads with three residues in β and three in α region of Ramachran plot. β-Layers are found in natural coiled coils, forming fibers with alternating α and β-structure, a previously undescribed protein fold. The α/β coiled coil is a new class of protein fiber based on a novel supersecondary element with alternating α and β-layers. The first β-layer is part of a 6-residue element, occurring after a position of the preceding heptad, biased towards c position, yielding same g position as T6 and T96. The α/β coiled coil retains only one hydrogen bond per unit from parent coiled coil, showing a novel backbone possible within Ramachran space with minor mutations to a known fold.
Epsin is an evolutionarily conserved endocytic clathrin adaptor whose critical function in clath coat dynamics remains elusive. To elucidate its function, researchers generated embryonic fibroblasts from conditional eps triple knockout mice, which displayed a dramatic cell division defect and robust impairment in clath-mediated endocytosis with accumulation of early and U-shaped pits. This correlated with perturbation of coupling between clath coat and actin cytoskeleton, confirmed in cell-free endocytosis assay. Results indicate a key conserved function of eps is generating force for invagination and fission of clath-coated pits, in addition to roles including low affinity interaction with SNAREs. Other endocytic proteins, including clath, have been implicated in organization of mitotic scaffolds. Complete absence of three epsins impacted clath-mediated endytosis. RNAi-mediated knockdown in fibroblastic cells reported global impairment of clath-mediated endytosis due to fission defect. Study aimed to provide conclusive evidence of eps function and sites in endocytosis using gene strategy eliminating all epsins. Eps reported roles in clath-independent endocytosis linked to actin regulation, with weak interaction of ENTH domain with synaptobrevin2/VAMP2. Intracellular vesicles need SNAREs to fuse with target membranes, requiring coupling between vesicle budding and SNARE incorporation. Study suggests eps critical at clath coat maturation stages with accumulation of shallow U-shaped pits in eps TKO cells. Critical function at transition between shallow and invaginated pits. Eps couples endocytic clath coat to actin with Hip1, helping invaginate pit. Impairment of eps stalls actin-dependent invagination of endocytic clath-coated pits.
A case-control study was conducted in urban Uganda to investigate risk factors for asthma among schoolchildren aged 5-17 years. The study included 555 cases and 111 controls, with asthma diagnosed by clinicians. The main risk factors for asthma were found to be tertiary education of fathers and mothers, with adjusted odds ratios of 2.32 and 1.85, respectively. Area of residence at birth also played a significant role, with children born in small towns or cities having increased risk of asthma compared to those born in rural areas, with odds ratios of 2.16 and 2.79, respectively. Additionally, a history of asthma in fathers and mothers, children's own allergic conditions, atopy, and cooking with gas/electricity were also risk factors. Asthma was associated with a strong rural-city gradient, higher socio-economic status, and urbanicity. The risk of asthma was predicted by area of residence in early life, particularly at birth, with the highest risk among children whose early life spent in small towns and cities. This risk increased with other factors such as parental history of allergic disease, children's atopy, higher socio-economic status, and urbanicity. Participants had a mean age of 11 years, with children with asthma being slightly older and more likely to have parents with tertiary education and a history of asthma. Compared to children born in rural Uganda, those born in towns or cities had increased risk of asthma with adjusted odds of 2.16 and 2.79. Rapid urbanization in Africa and low and middle-income countries is likely to increase asthma prevalence. The study provides a basis for investigating environmental and lifestyle factors increasing asthma risk in urban areas. Even without parental history of allergic disease, being born in town or city increased risk of asthma with odds 2.15 and 3.33. Parental history of disease increased risk from 2.78 in rural to 5.16 in towns.
Interactions between three pigment cell types are required to form the stripe pattern in adult zebrafish, but the molecular nature of these interactions is poorly understood. Mutations in the leopard gene, encoding Connexin 41.8, a gap junction subunit, cause a phenotypic series of spotted patterns. A dominant allele, leot3, leads to a complete loss of the pattern, suggesting a dominant negative impact on another component of gap junctions. In a genetic screen, this component was identified as Cx39.4. Loss-of-function alleles demonstrate that Cx39.4 is required for stripe formation in zebish, however, the fins are almost not affected. Double mutants and chimeras show that leopard and Cx39.4 are only required in xanthophores and melanoph, but not iridoph, suggesting both connexins form heteromeric gap junctions. The phenotypes indicate these promote homotyp interactions between melanoph and xanth, and instruct patterning of iridoph. 

Fish homozygous for leot3 show an identical phenotype of completely dissolved pattern, individual melanoph that hardly cluster together are present mostly associated with iridoph. A cartoon of Connin41.8 depicts positions of the mutations, gap junctions composed of two hemi-channels in adjacent cells, each hemi-channel made of six connin subunits, can be identical or different. A hetereric/heteric gap junction is schematically shown, alignment amino acid sequence from zebish C41.8 with human orthologue GJA5, transmem regions shaded, newly identified mutations in Connin41.8 highlighted. The leot3 phenotype is stronger indicating mutant C41.8 has a negative effect some component involved in pigment formation, possibly another connin in hetereric junctions with C41.8. 

Wild zebish show a pattern of dark and light stripes, at higher magnification melanoph in dark regions and xanth in light regions discernible. Mutants homozy for le1 and heter for le3 stripes dissolved into spots, clusters of melanoph visible. Expansion of iridoph is indirect possibly caused by xanth covering irid. Data suggest C41.8 and C39.4 interact to form hetereric gap junctions responsible for interactions among chromatoph while pattern is established. These channels are essential for color pattern formation in zebrafish.
Insects detect volatile molecules using olfactory receptors (OR) or ionotropic receptors (IR) and in some cases gustatory receptors (GR), with IRs expressed in olfactory organs across Protostomia, while ORs are hypothesized to be an adaptation to a terrestrial lifestyle. The olfactory system of the primary wingless bristletail Lepismilis yata, the firebrat Thermobia domestica, and the neoptan leaf Phyllium sifium was investigated, revealing OR and olfactory coreceptor Orco are likely lacking in Lepismilis, three Orco candidates in Thermobia, and a fully developed OR/Orco system in Phyll. This suggests ORs did not arise as an adaptation to terrestrial lifestyle but evolved later in insect evolution, with Orco present before ORs. Data on insect olfactory receptors are based on neopteran insects, while the identity of receptors in evolutionarily ancient apterygote and paleopter insects is unknown. The evolution of coreceptor Orco occurred after bristails split from the common ancestor with remaining insects, and an olfactory system relying on ORs and Orco evolved after wing emergence. Insect olfactory function as heteromultimers composed of at least one ligand-specific OR and coreceptor Orco. Insects provide an opportunity to study ancestral characteristics and building blocks of olfaction, with selected species at crucial positions of the phylogenetic tree having a functional olfactory system adapted to terrestrial conditions. This collection provides a model to study the early evolution of the olfactory system, addressing which receptors are involved in odor detection in basal insects. Neopter insects recognize most volatile stimuli by ORs, multitransmembrane proteins unrelated to nematode vertebrate olfactory, displaying distinct topology.
The global burden of malaria has decreased over the last decade, and many nations are attempting elimination, but asymptomatic infections pose a major hurdle for these efforts. Mass drug administration (MDA) is a potential solution, with success depending on adequate population participation. A study in Myanmar analyzed malaria episodes and asymptomatic infections in four villages undergoing MDA, finding that individuals from neighborhoods with low MDA adherence had 2.85 times the odds of having a malaria episode post-MDA compared to those from high adherence neighborhoods, regardless of individual participation, suggesting a herd effect. High mosquito biting rates, living with someone with malaria, or having asymptomatic infection were also predictors of clinical episodes. Spatial clustering of non-adherence to MDA, even in villages with high participation, may frustrate elimination efforts. The aim of antimalarial MDA is to interrupt transmission, and the notion of a herd providing additional population protection is plausible but has not been empirically examined. Drawing from micro-epidemiological and spatial data from an MDA trial in Kayin, Myanmar, the study describes geographic and epidemiological patterns of clinical and subclinical P. falcum malaria in villages undergoing MDA, investigating associations between individual and group participation, subclinical infections, and episodes post-MDA. The primary objective was to look for potential herd effect and impact of nonadherence to MDA for P. fum malaria, finding an apparent group effect from M adherence, suggesting protection from decreased clinical episodes among groups for longer than prophylactic effect of administered antimalials. The study site consisted of four villages along the Myanmar-Thailand border in Kayin State, selected based on P fum malaria prevalence using ultrasensitive quantitative PCR and part of MDA pilot. Episodes after MDA corresponded to increase village HBR, documenting herd effect by MDA for P fum malaria.
The conservation of transcription factor binding specificities across 600 million years of bilateria evolution has been investigated, revealing a striking level of conservation between Drosophila and mammals. The binding specificities of 242 Drosophila transcription factors were determined and compared to human and mouse data, showing that transcription factor binding specificities are highly conserved between Drosila and mammals. For orthologous transcription factors, the similarity extends to the level of subtle dinucleotide binding preferences, indicating that transcription factor specificities are not subject to substantial evolutionary drift. Fruit flies have almost as complex a repertoire of transcription factor binding motifs as humans, with conservation extending to secondary modes of binding and subtle dinucleotide preferences. The evolution of transcription factor specificities contributes to the emergence of novel types of differentiated cells, as human transcription factors with divergent functions are found in cell types not present in flies. High-throughput systematic evolution of ligands by exponential enrichment was used to determine transcription factor specificities in Drosila melanogaster, providing 122 novel high-resolution motifs and shedding light on new features of Drosila DNA-binding characteristics. Transcription factors belonging to the same structural family generally bind similar sites, with ortholog transcription factors having identical core binding profiles, flanking sequences, and secondary modes of binding despite diverging over 600 million years ago. Divergence and drift in transcription factor DNA binding contribute to animal diversity, with subtle DNA preferences potentially changing faster and independently of the core recognition motif, allowing specificity to slowly drift in evolution. Transcription factors with divergent functions are found in cell types not present in flies, indicating that evolution of transcription factor specificities contributes to emergence of novel cell types, with some transcription factors having multiple roles, such as bZIP and HOX proteins and nuclear receptors.
Researchers have developed a novel strategy for simultaneously differentiating human-induced pluripotent stem cells into both mesoderm-derived cardiac and endoderm-derived lung epithelial lineages, allowing for the study of cardio-pulmonary interaction during human embryogenesis. This was achieved through temporal tuning of WNT and nodal signaling in the absence of exogenous growth factors, resulting in the formation of concentric cardio-pulmonary microtissues in 3D suspension culture. Upon withdrawal of WNT agonist, the cardiac and pulmonary components segregated with concurrent initiation of cardiac contraction. This multilineage differentiation model offers an experimentally tractable system for investigating human cardio-pulmonary interaction and tissue formation during embryogenesis. In 3D suspension culture, cardio-pulmonary microtissues effectively segregated into separate cardiac and pulmonary tissues, which was partially inhibited by WNT activation, providing an in vitro model for studying mechanistic interplay between developing heart and lung. Following WNT agonist withdrawal, concentric cardio-pulmonary microtissues reorganized into cardiac-only and pulmonary-only tissues, offering a human-induced pluripotent stem cell-based model for cardio-pulmonary co-development and tissue segregation. Using small molecule cocktails modulating WNT, nodal, and TGFβ signaling, researchers engineered pulmonary-centered cardio-pulmonary microtissues with accompanying cardiac lineage promoting alveolar maturation. Co-induction of meserm and enderm drove simultaneous cardiac and pulmonary specification from respective germ-layer progenitors. Transitioning co-induced progenitors to 3D culture expedited alveolar maturation within 3 days, supported by the cardiac lineage. Further investigation is required for morphological and functional maturation of both lineages and crosstalk. This work provides a model for investigating molecular and cellular mechanisms of human cardio-pulmonary co-development and boundary formation.
Researchers have demonstrated that Escherichia coli exhibits an unexpected flexibility in cellular mutation rates in response to changes in selective pressure, allowing the organism to adapt to stressful environments. While most changes in an organism's DNA negatively impact fitness, specific mutations enable adaptation, and the mutation rate is strictly regulated and often considered a slowly-evolving parameter. However, the study shows that hypermutation independently evolves when different Esichia coli cultures adapt to high ethanol stress, with hypermutator states being transitory and repeatedly alternating with decreases in mutation rates. Population mutation rates rise when cells experience higher stress and decline once cells are adapted. Cellular mortality is identified as the major driving force behind the quick evolution of mutation rates. 

The study aimed to understand the dynamics of hypermutation under near-lethal complex stress using E. coli exposed to high ethanol as a model, where multiple mutations epistatically interact and diverse evolutionary trajectories lead to adaptation. Surprisingly, in addition to the increase in mutation rate, hypermutator states are transitory and mutation rates decrease once cells are adapted to the environment. Current knowledge of mechanisms underlying changes in mutation remains largely fragmentary, but the study identified cellular mortality as a major modulator of population rate. Experimental evolution revealed an essential role for hypermutation for de novo adaptation to high stress, using a collection of mutators with distinct rates to identify optimal mutation for rapid growth under ethanol stress.

The findings shed light on the dynamics of mutation rate evolution, explaining why maintaining high rates is limited in time and how organisms balance robustness and evolvability, contributing to the prevalence of hypermutation in various settings such as emergence of antibiotic resistance in microbes and cancer relapses upon chemotherapy. Bacteria undergoing antibiotic treatment and cells exposed to chemotherapy are prime examples of cells under stressful conditions where hypermutation quickly and recurrently arises with increases in stress, declining once cells are adapted. Overall, the study demonstrates that mutation rates are not fixed and can change rapidly in response to selective pressure to avoid extinction under near-lethal stress.
Metabolic adjustment to changing environmental conditions, particularly balancing growth and defense responses, is crucial for organisms to survive. The evolutionary conserved AMPK/Snf/SnRK kinases are well-known metabolic regulators of the low-energy response in animals and plants, acting by modulating key metabolic enzymes and transcriptional reprogramming. Researchers identified Arabidopsis transcription factor bZIP63 as a key regulator of the starvation response and direct target of SnRK kinase. Phosphorylation of bZIP by SnRK changed its dimer preference, affecting target expression and primary metabolism. A bzip knock-out mutant exhibited starvation-related phenotypes, complemented by wild type bZIP but not by a version with point mutations in SnRK target sites. SnRK kinase AKIN10 is responsible for starvation-induced hyperphosphorylation of bZIP, presenting the first transcription factor acting as a direct target of SnRK in transcriptional energy response. bZIP phosphorylation at SnRK sites is crucial for function, underlined by complementation of bzip mutant with genomic bZIP constructs, with wild type but not S29/S294/S300 mutant complementing metabolic and senescence phenotypes. bZIP is a metabolic regulator under stress conditions, phosphorylated at multiple sites in sugar and energy-dependent manner. SnRK targets conserved serine residues in N and C-terminus of bZIP. Phosphorylation of sites is crucial for b63 dimer and activity, proposing a model for phosphorylation-triggered switch of bZIP dimer partners regulating metabolic reprogramming.
Researchers have identified Ring finger protein 10 (RNF10) as a novel synaptonuclear messenger that enables bidirectional communication between synapses and nuclei, facilitating the transfer of information encoded macromolecules. R10 is activated by calcium signals in the postsynaptic compartment, leading to discrete changes at the transcriptional level, and is enriched at excitatory synapses where it associates with the GluN2 subunit of N-methyl-D-aspartate (NMD) receptors. Activation of synaptic Glu2-containing NMDs and induction of long-term potentiation (LTP) leads to the translocation of R10 from dendritic segments and dendritic spines to the nucleus through import-dependent long-distance transport from synap-dendritic compartments. R10 dissociates from the NMD complex in an activity-dependent manner, sharing the Glu2 binding region with CaM, and contains sequences for downstream signaling and scaffolding molecules. Silencing R10 prevents maintenance of LTP and LTP-dependent modifications of dendritic spines, indicating impaired R10-mediated synapuclear signaling reduces dendritic density. The NMD complex is a rich source of messengers capable of trafficking to the nucleus, with R10 encoding and transducing synaptic and extrasynaptic Gl2 NAR signals to the nucleus, docking an NMD-receptor-derived signalosome to nuclear sites in a stimulus-dependent manner. Activation of synaptic but not extrasynaptic NMDs leads to increased R10 nuclear localization, describing a novel synapse-nucleus pathway linking synaptic Glu2-containing NAR to nuclear expression. R10 is highly expressed in the nucleus and synapses, part of the NMD complex, directly interacting with the cytoplasmic tail of the Glu2 subunit of NARs.
The study investigates the structural basis for recognition and remodeling of the TBP:DNA:NC complex by Mot1, a single-unit remodeler that dissociates TATA-binding protein (TBP):DNA complexes, offering insights into the mechanisms of Swi2/Snf ATPases. The crystal structure of the N-terminal domain of Mot1 in complex with TBP, DNA, and transcription regulator negative cofactor 2 (NC2) reveals that Mot1 reduces DNA:NC interactions and unbends DNA compared to the TBP:DNA:NC state, suggesting Mot primes TBP:NC displacement in an ATP-independent manner. Electron microscopy and cross-linking data show the Sw2/Snf domain of Mot1 associates with upstream DNA and histone of NC, similar to nucleosome remodelers. This study provides a framework for how Swi/Snf ATPase interacts with its DNA:protein complex. Swi/Snf-type ATPases regulate chromatin structure and epigenetic state during transcription, DNA replication, and repair, and Mot1 serves as a model for structural and mechanistic studies. Binding of Mot1 leads to conformational changes between substrate proteins and DNA, priming the complex for ATP-dependent disruption. The interface between Mot1 and NC helps separate NC's main TBP helix from TBP and DNA. The study presents a pseudoatomic structure of Swi/Snf enzyme Mot bound to protein:DNA and a framework for interaction with TBP:DNA:NC. Swi/Snf enzymes remodel protein:DNA by disrupting the interface between nucleic and protein partners. The crystal structure of MotNT in complex with TBP, NC, and TATA DNA at 3.8 resolution, along with biochemical and cross-link studies of full-length Mot, provides the first pseudo view of Swi/Snf ATPase in DNA:protein complex. Association of Mot with TBP:DNA:NC results in conformation changes that may have implications for ATP-dependent dissociation. Mot1 hydrolysis-dependent translocation of Swi/Snf ATPase provides chemical force, but binding of the remodeler induces conformation changes in the target. Overall, the study provides insights into the structural basis for recognition and remodeling of TBP:DNA complex by Mot1.
Researchers have designed synthetic variants of the natural product ternatin, a cytotoxic and anti-adipogenic compound, and found them to be up to 500 times more potent than the original compound in killing cancer cells. Using a photo-affinity probe, they identified the translation elongation factor-1 ternary complex as the specific target of ternatin and demonstrated competitive binding with the unrelated products didemnin and cytotin. Mutations in the domain of elongation factor-1 prevent ternatin binding and confer resistance to its cytotoxic effects, indicating the hydrophobic surface as a functional spot for elongation factor modulation. Ternatin and its variants inhibit protein synthesis with potencies that correlate with their ability to block cell proliferation. Photo-ternatin identified elongation factor-1 as a plausible target and crosslinked to purified elongation factor-1 in the context of its ternary complex with GTP and aminoacylated tRNA. The binding of small molecules to the elongation factor ternary complex has not been previously reported. The study aimed to identify the target of the cytotoxic product ternatin, characterized as a potent adipogenesis inhibitor, and found compounds 3 and 4 to be up to 500 times more potent than ternatin. Ternins may be attractive leads for medicinal chemistry due to their structural simplicity and accessibility. The elongation factor-1 ternary complex with GTP and aminoacyl-tRNA is a common target for evolution of cytotoxic products, including ternatin, didem, and cytot. The mechanism by which binding blocks translation is unknown but may be analogous to the antibiotic kirromycin, suggested by genetic dominance of tern sensitivity. The discovery of two structurally unrelated products competing for elongation factor ternary complex binding is unexpected and awaits further biochemical and structural studies. Elongation factor-1 and its ternary complex with GTP and aminoacyl-tRNA are targets for the evolution of cytotoxic products.
Mutations in the inositol 5-phosphatase OCRL cause Lowe syndrome and Dent's disease, and although OCRL, a direct clathrin interactor, is recruited to late-stage clathrin-coated pits, clinical manifestations have primarily been attributed to intracellular sorting defects. Research has shown that OCRL loss in Lowe patient fibroblasts impacts clath-mediated endocytosis and results in an endocytic defect, with cells exhibiting accumulation of clathrin-coated vesicles and an increase in U-shaped clath-coated pits, which may result from sequestration of coat components on uncoated vesicles. Endytic vesicles fail to lose their coat and the majority of the actin comets present in patient cells, with SN9, an adaptor that couples late-stage endocytic pits to actin polymerization, binding directly to OCRL and remaining associated with such vesicles. The absence of OCRL in patient cells impairs endocytic clath coat dynamics and results in an endocytic defect, with cells accumulating clath-coated vesicles that fail to loose their coat. The lack of 5-phosphatase activity of OCRL delays endocytosis, stalling late pits, impairs uncoating, and triggers nucleation of comets from clathrin vesicles that retain PI(4,5)P2 and fail to uncoat. OCRL participates in uncoating and facilitates recycling of endocytic factors, a process required for normal progression of endytosis. Defects in clath-mediated endytosis have not been reported in Lowe syndrome fibroblasts, but analysis of clath-coated dynamics in patient and control cells by time-lapse microscopy revealed numerous actin comets driven by clath-positive vesicles, supporting a close relationship between endytic clath coats and actin. The presence of actin comets in Lowe syndrome cells is driven by clath-positive vesicles, suggesting a link between endytic clath coats and actin. Overall, the findings indicate that OCRL acts as an uncoating factor, and defects in clath-mediated endytosis likely contribute to pathology in patients with OCRL mutations.
Aberrant activation of Wnt/β-caten signaling plays a crucial role in colorectal cancer, but identifying effective Wnt inhibitors for cancer treatment remains a challenge. Researchers have discovered a novel FAK/PYK/GSKβ/Y216/β-caten regulation axis where FAK and PYK, elevated in adenomas in APCmin mice and human colorectal tissues, function redundantly promote the Wnt/β-caten pathway by phosphorylating GSKβ216, leading to β-caten accumulation and intestinal tumorigenesis. Phosphorylation of GSKβ216 is a determinant of GSK recruitment of β-TrCP, and Wnt-induced β-caten accumulation requires Wnt-induced GSK/β-TrCP interaction. Pharmacological inhibition of FAK/P2 suppressed adenoma formation in APCmin mice, accompanied by reduced intestinal levels of phospho-GSKβ216 and β-caten, indicating the FAK/PYK/GSKβ216 axis is critical for Wnt/β-caten activation in APC-driven tumenesis. The intestinal level of phospho-GSKβ216 is higher in APCmin mice than control mice, and PF-562 treatment suppressed GSKβ216 phosphorylation and GSKβ ubiquination, supporting that FAK/PYK contributes to APC-driven tumenesis by phosphorylating GSKβ216 to promote W/β-caten signaling. Loss of functional APC induces FAK and PY2 expression, and FAK/PYK phosphorylates GSK216 to stabilize β-caten, initiating and supporting tumenesis. Phosphorylation of GSK216 was discovered two decades ago, but the mechanisms remain unclear. Dual FAK/PYK inhibitor PF-271 showed anti-tumorigenic effects in APCmin mice, a model predisposed to intestinal adenoma formation due to aberrant Wnt/β-caten activation. FAK and PY2 redundantly regulate W/β-caten signaling in CRC by phosphorylating GSKα/β279/Y216, raising the possibility they also function redundantly in W-driven tumenesis. Loss of functional APC results in less efficient GSK-mediated phosphorylation of β-caten, leading to reduced β-caten degradation, mimicking Wnt stimulation. APC mutation activates W signaling constitutively, but histological studies show only 50% colon cells display nuclear β-caten staining. Intestine-specific deletion of F alone caused abrogation of adenoma formation in APC-mut mice. The study reveals a novel FAK/PYK/GSKβ216/β-caten regulatory circuit in tumenesis and indicates FAK/PYK/GSK216 axis is critical for W/β-caten activation in APC-driven tumenesis.
Researchers investigated the neural control of transitions between posture and movement, focusing on the regulation of vestibular stabilizing mechanisms to enable motion. The study tested the continuity of vestibular control during transitions between quiet standing and locomotion and between two postures in healthy subjects exposed to electrical vestibular stimuli. Time-frequency analyses of the relationship between stimuli and ground forces revealed discontinuities corresponding to null periods preceding movement initiation and termination, indicating humans interrupt vestibular balance stabilizing mechanisms to transition between motor states. The vestibular system encodes head motion in space and is vital for maintaining stability during standing and locomotion, influencing muscle activation in posture control. To transition between motor states, balance responses are disrupted, and prior to transitions, vestibular influence on whole-body responses is suspended, starting while subjects maintain upright stance before transition. The suppression of vestibular balance mechanisms is not specific to one type of transition, suggesting a general process during standing and motor state transitions. Results demonstrate humans stop balancing before moving and stop moving before balancing, related to different sensory gains controlling whole-body movements by quiet and locomotor policies. The vestibular control during locomotor or posture-posture transitions can be monitored by quantifying vestibular-evoked responses over the transition. This approach has been used to monitor engagement and disengagement of vestibular control between self and externally driven states of balance. The study supports optimal theory suggesting transitions require disengagement of one motor policy before implementation of another, showing discrete change between control policies. The findings indicate a period of suspension of vestibular-evoked responses related to different sensory gains controlling whole-body by quiet and locomotor policies, with quiet standing having small whole-body accelerations and ground reaction force oscillations within base support limits. An adaptive controller adjusts to these changes, and the vestibular control of balance during transitions can be continuously monitored, providing insight into the neural control of posture and movement. Overall, the study provides evidence for the downregulation of vestibular stabilizing mechanisms to enable transitions between motor states, a fundamental aspect of human motor control.
The transcription factor RpaA is the master regulator of circadian transcription in cyanobacteria, driving genome-wide oscillations in mRNA abundance, and its deletion has no effect on viability in constant light conditions but renders cells inviable in cycling conditions when light and dark periods alternate. The rpa- strain cannot maintain energy status at night, does not accumulate carbon reserves during the day, and is defective in transcription of genes for utilization of carbohydrate stores at night. Reconstruction of carbon pathways combined with provision of an external carbon source restores energy and viability of the rpa strain in light/dark conditions. The circadian pathway controls and temporally coordinates essential pathways in carbon metabolism to maximize fitness of cells facing periodic energy limitations. Both accumulation and utilization of carbon reserves for energy production during darkness are defective in the rpa strain and correction of these defects restores viability. The circadian output enhances fitness of cyanobacteria through coordination of central metabolism with demands by periodic changes in the environment. Functional circadian mediated by Rpa is required for anticipatory accumulation of carbon during the day and metabolic switching to catabolism at dusk, allowing cells to stimulate energy production and sustain viability during darkness. Exposure to darkness restricts energy availability, creating unique demands in cyanobacteria dependent on sunlight for energy through photosynthesis, and the rpa strain exhibits defects in growth and viability in cyclic but not constant environments. The phosphorylated form of Rpa regulated by the circadian clock is critical for coordination of anabolism and catabism and viability in light/dark cycles. Deletion of rpa prevents glycogen accumulation in light, rendering cells unable to utilize glycogen at dusk, leading to alterations in energy charge and severe reduction in fitness. The cyanobacterial circadian system anticipates periodic dark-induced resource limitation through Rpa activity, temporally coordinating metabolism to prepare cells for demands of darkness.
Transition metals, such as zinc, are essential micronutrients in organisms but toxic in excessive amounts, and heavy-metal transporting P-type ATPases are crucial for homeostasis, conferring detoxification and redistribution of these ions across cellular membranes. The PIB-4-ATPases, a subclass with the broadest cargo scope, lacks structural information, and their topology remains elusive. The structure and functional analysis of an archetypal PIB4-ATPase, sCoT from Sulfitacter sp. NAS-1, reveals the architecture devoid of classical heavy-metal binding domains and provides insights into the mechanism and diversity of heavy-metal transport. The data disclose novel P-type ATPase features, including a dual role in heavy-metal release and an internal ion of invariant histidine. The turnover of PIB-ATPases is potassium independent, contrasting many other P-type ATPases. PIB-ATPases are subdivided into groups based on conserved motifs and selectivity towards transition metal ions, with Cu+ and Zn transporting PIB1 and PIB2 ATPases well characterized, but little known regarding PIB4 proteins, comprising the simplest and shortest within the P-type ATPase superfamily. The PIB4 model sCoT was used to shed light on the structure and mechanism of the PIB4-class, with ATP activity assessed in vitro in lipid-detergent solution using the Baginski assay in the presence of different heavy metals. The metal specificity of PIB4-ATPases remains elusive, with some members having cobalt specificity, others broader or altered ion profiles, transporting ions such as Zn2+, Ni2+, Cu+, and Ca2+. The ability to adapt to environmental changes in heavy-metal levels is paramount for cells, as elements essential for cellular processes yet toxic at elevated concentrations. Transition metal transporting P-type P ATP proteins are critical for cellular homeostasis, providing efflux of copper, zinc, and cobalt from the intracellular milieu. The structure of PIB4-type ATPase reveals topology, an eight-helix domain configuration, and no heavy-metal binding domains, at least in members without extended N-termini. The results open efforts for drug discovery, as PIB4ATPases function as virulence factors in pathogens, and development of tools to manipulate heavy-metal accumulation in plants to prevent or enrichment.
Researchers have identified a chromatin signature associated with mitotically stable monoallelic expression (MAE) in human cells, characterized by the simultaneous presence of active (H3K36me3) and silencing (H3K27me) chromatin marks in the gene body. This signature is present in approximately 20% of ubiquitously expressed genes and over 30% of tissue-specific genes across cell types. Notably, it is enriched among key developmental genes that have bivalent chromatin structure in pluripotent cells. The detection of the MA signature does not rely on single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) or clonality, making feasible the analysis of MA in primary samples, including comparisons of normal and diseased tissue. The study found that among widely expressed genes, about 20% have a chromatin pattern for MAE in at least one tissue, and taking genes with tissue-specific expression as a group, estimate between 39% and 48 have the MA pattern. Genes with MA chromatin signature share pronounced features as a group. MA identified in clones is reflected by the chromatin signature in polyclonal samples. The study tested the chromatin signature as a predictor of the MA state by identifying novel genes with MA chromatin pattern and assessing allelic bias in monoclonal cell lines. A large fraction of known bivalent genes (>80%) acquire the MA chromatin signature in at least one of the differentiated cell types. Upon activation, these master genes preferentially resolve into a state with characteristic MA chromatin signature. The results open a new approach to the study of MA, independent of polymorphisms, and suggest MA is linked to cell differentiation.
AAA+ chaperones, such as ClpC, exert ATP-fueled substrate unfolding by threading through a central pore, requiring mechanisms for tight repression and substrate-specific activation. ClpC, with peptidase Clp, forms a bacterial protease essential for virulence and stress, and adaptor MecA activates ClC by targeting substrates and stimulating Clp ATP activity. Cryo-EM structures of ClC without MecA reveal large helical assemblies that associate via head-to-head contacts between coiled-coil middle domains, repressing ClC in its ground state. Binding of MecA converts this resting state to an active planar structure by interacting with coiled-coil middle domains. Loss of ClC repression in mutants causes constitutive activation and severe cellular toxicity. Coiled-coil domains control ClC activity by sequestering ClC molecules in an inactive state, extending the role of coiled-coil domains as regulatory devices controlling AAA protein activity. In Clp/Hsp disaggregase, a coiled-coil middle domain forms a repressing belt around the AAA ring, reducing ATP activity. ATP repression is relieved by dissociation and binding to Hsp adaptors, preventing reassociation with the ClB/Hsp ring. Activity control and adaptor action require ATP activity to be repressed in the ground state, achieved by regulatory coiled domains inserted into an AAA module. ClC MDs form intermolecular head-head contacts, docking two layers of ClC molecules in helical conformation, restricting substrate binding and ATP hydrolysis. Mutating key MD residues causes cellular toxicity, suggesting a general mechanism controlling bacterial AAA chaper systems. Severe toxicity of ClC and ClE mutants qualifies AAA chaperones as targets for antimicrobials.
Researchers utilized a Drosophila model to investigate the behavior of adult muscle precursors, a type of stem cell found in fruit flies, to understand how they maintain their undifferentiated quiescent state and are reactivated. Adult muscle precursors send out thin filopodia that contact neighboring muscles and maintain this association throughout dormancy and proliferation, suggesting muscles play a role in reactivation. Genetic analysis indicates muscles send inductive insulin-like peptide 6 signals that activate the insulin pathway in associated adult precursors, leading to Notch activation, which regulates proliferation via dMyc. The study reveals Drosila adult precursors display homing behavior to the muscle niche and the niche-driven insulin-Notch-dyc cascade plays a key role in setting the activated state of adult precursors. Adult precursors emerge during mid-embryogenesis, express progenitor markers, and lie dormant during embryonic and most larval life, but once activated, they proliferate to provide myoblasts for muscle growth and regeneration. dMyc acts as a positive regulator of reactivation, providing evidence that muscle cell proliferation is regulated by the same genes in Drosila and vertebrates. Notch acts downstream of the insulin pathway and dyc functions downstream of Notch. The data suggest muscle behaves as an adult precursor niche and plays a driving role in reactivation in larval life, governed by the muscle-induced insulinNot/dyc cascade. Adult precursors maintain filopodia-based association with muscles throughout dormancy and when they start to proliferate, indicating muscles could play a role in reactivation. Muscle appears to be the source of local inductive insulin signals reactivating adult precursors via non-canonical Notch and its target dyc, inducing proliferation in larval stages. dyc is required for adult precursor reactivation and proliferation, with genetic experiments showing Notch acts downstream of insulin and dyc functions downstream Notch, illustrating the muscle-induced insulin/Not/dyc cascade governing reactivation of dormant adult precursors.
Researchers have identified the sodium leak channel NALCN as a crucial component in controlling the tonic firing and glycolytic sensitivity of substantia nigra pars reticata (SNr) neurons, a major output of the basal ganglia. SN neurons fire spontaneously at high rates, providing tonic inhibition to downstream brain areas, supported by a depolarizing leak current, whose molecular basis was previously poorly understood. Using single-cell RNA sequencing, the transcriptome of individual mouse SN neurons was analyzed, revealing that SN neurons express NAL, and its absence impairs spontaneous firing.
