NAL is involved in modulating excitability changes by glycolysis and activation of muscarinic acetylcholine receptors. Disruption of NAL could impair basal ganglia circuit function, potentially underlining severe motor deficits in humans with NAL mutations. Whole transcriptome sequencing detected several nonselective cation channels (NS) that could generate the tonic depolar current, with NAL standing out as a prime candidate due to its high expression. SN neurons require NAL for tonic firing, suggesting NAL mutations could contribute to basal ganglia-related disorders, such as excessive muscle tension and dystonia. Hyperactivity of SN is observed in Parkinsonian animals. NAL's high expression and interaction with Unc80, Unc79, and Fam155 genes, known to interact with NAL, supports its role in the depolarizing current. The discovery of NAL's role in SN neurons provides insight into basal ganglia circuit function and potential therapeutic targets for motor disorders.
The discovery of antibiotics from culturable microorganisms has saved millions of lives since the 20th century, but the increasing formation of resistance has rendered these compounds ineffective, while the majority of microbial chemical diversity remains inaccessible for exploration. Conventional methods for culturing recalcitrant bacteria are limited by low throughput. To address this, a droplet-based microscale cultivation system was developed, directly coupled with high-throughput screening for antimicrobial activity prior to strain isolation. This system allows for highly parallelized cultivation starting from single cells, resulting in the cultivation of yet-uncultured species and higher bacterial diversity than standard agar cultivation. Strains able to inhibit reporter strains were isolated from the system, with a variety of antimicrobial compounds detected for a potent antibiotic producer. The cultivation strategy was verified by culturing a bacterial community from a soil sample and comparing the outcome to classical plate cultivation. Microfluidic droplets provide genotype-phenotype linkage for secreted products, allowing compounds to stay confined with the cell of origin, and can be stored in bulk for incubation under controlled conditions. A high-throughput screening for antimicrobial products was implemented using bacterial reporter strains in droplets. The combination of strain discovery during cultivation with selection for natural product biosynthesis streamlines efforts towards new structures for antibiotic development. A modular droplet-microfluid platform was devised, mimicking environmental conditions in situ, with miniaturized droplet volume facilitating high-density long-term cultivation of compartmentalized single cells. The approach was made practical by combining cultivation with a screening step to detect droplets containing cells producing antibiotic compounds. Axenic cultures of putative species could not be maintained after isolation, but cells could be used for obtaining high-quality genome information.
Tuberculous meningitis is a disease with high mortality rates linked to excessive inflammation, and while adjunctive anti-inflammatory corticosteroids reduce mortality by only 30%, suggesting that inflammatory pathophysiology causes a subset of deaths. Research in Vietnam found that the benefit of anti-inflammatory corticosteroids was most pronounced in patients with a C/T promoter variant in the leukotriene A4 hydrolase (LTAH) gene, which encodes an enzyme regulating inflammatory eicosanoids. Patients with the LTAH TT genotype had increased expression, consistent with corticosteroids benefiting individuals with hyper-inflammatory responses. However, a study in Indonesia did not find an LTAH TT genotype survival benefit. Using Bayesian analysis of both studies, it was found that LTAH TT genotype confers survival benefit that begins early and continues long-term in both populations. This benefit is nullified in the most severe cases with high mortality. LTAH genotyping together with disease assessment may target glucocortic therapy to patients most likely to benefit from it. The findings support the model that mortality from tuberculous meningitis is due to distinct inflammatory states, and LTA genotype may be a determinant of inflammation and response to adjunctive treatment. Personalized genotype-directed glucortic treatment may be warranted, with the drug given to TT patients. The LTA TT effect may be limited to the least severe patients due to underlying association being overridden by severe disease, a strong correlate of mortality. The beneficial effect of dexamethasone to LTA TT may not have time to come into play in severe patients, or corticoids may no longer be beneficial due to dysregulated inflammation not revealed by standard metrics. The Indonesia cohort presented more severe disease and died earlier, suggesting the beneficial effects of dexamasone for LTA TT were nullified in severe disease. The concept of disease being too severe for outcomes to be influenced by intervention has precedent, suggesting LTA genotyping and disease assessment may guide glucortic therapy to patients most likely to benefit.
Researchers have investigated the mechanism by which growth cones navigate axonal projections in response to guidance cues, specifically how they translate local cues into protrusive forces to decide migratory direction. The study focused on the role of Shootin1, a protein involved in axon guidance, and its interaction with Netrin-1, a molecule known axon guidance cue. Mice lacking Shoot1 exhibited abnormal forebrain commissural axon projections, similar to those seen in Netrin-1 deficient mice, indicating a link between Shoot1 and Netrin-1 in axon guidance. Gradients of Netrin-1 induced polarized Pak1-mediated phosphorylation of Shoot1 within growth cones, increasing interaction between Shoot1 and L1-CAM, promoting F-actin adhesion and force generation for growth cone migration. Spatially regulated Shoot1 phosphorylation within growth cones was necessary for axon turning in response to Netrin-1 gradients. The study defines a mechano-effector for Netrin signaling, demonstrating Shoot1 phosphorylation as a critical readout for Net-1 gradients resulting in directional mechanoresponse for ax guidance. Data showed high levels of Shoot1 expression in developing forebrain commissural axons, with small Netrin-1 concentration differences eliciting polarized Shoot1 phosphorylation within growth cones. Netrin-elicited Shoot1 phosphorylation promoted Shoot1 and L1-CAM interaction, generating traction force for cone motility. Disrupting spatially regulated Shoot1 phosphorylation inhibited axon turning, but not outgrowth, induced by Net-1 gradients. Uncoupling F-act adhesion by Shoot1 inhibited Net-1 induced axon growth and turning on laminin. The study indicates Net-1 induced ax guidance, mediated by Shoot1-L1 interaction, is not limited to growth cones on L1. Polarized Shoot1 phosphorylation within cones, elicited by Net-1 gradients, plays a crucial role in ax guidance, with tracing signals from phosphorylated Shoot1 upstream to DCC leading to better understanding of the gradient-reading mechanism in Net-1 regulated ax guidance. Shoot1 is highly expressed in developing forebrain commiss axons, with knockout mice displaying dysgenesis and misprojection of these axons, highlighting the importance of Shoot1 in Net-1 ax guidance. Overall, the study demonstrates the critical role of Shoot1 in translating Net-1 gradients into directional forces for ax guidance.
Researchers investigated the biophysical origin of the hummingbird's hum by studying six freely hovering Anna's hummingbirds using acoustic nearfield holography with a 217 microphone array and directly measuring 3D aerodynamic forces with a new aerodynamic platform. An idealized acoustic model integrating aerodynamic forces with wing kinematics showed the timbre of the hummingbird's hum arises from oscillating lift and drag forces on each wing. Comparing birds and insects, the characteristic timbre and radiated power of flapping wings originates from higher harmonics in aerodynamic forces supporting body weight. Analysis across insects and birds revealed allometric makes larger birds quieter and elongated flies louder, clarifying bioacoustic behavior. The aerodynamic platform measured steady and unsteady components of pressure around the bird up to three times wingbeat frequency, associated with net 3D aerodynamic forces. A new aerodynamic platform directly measured net 3D force generated by freely hovering hummingbirds, integrated into an aeroacoust model predicting sound radiated due to oscillating forces from flapping. In vivo 3D aerodynamic and wing kinematics measurements corroborated spatially and temporally through nearfield holography showed hummingbirds generate highly directional hum, broadband pressure oriented downward, and acoustic directivity dependent on harmonic parity. The model assumes rigid airfoils, neglecting effects like wingtip, feather, and turbulent vortex dynamics. Comparison with acoustic recordings for six hummingbirds corroborated the predictive power of the minimal model, pointing to oscillating aerodynamic forces generating hum. Numerical simulation of Fow Williams Hawkings aeroacoustic equation showed farfield of flapping mosquito wings driven by aerodynamic fluctuation, despite lacking in vivo nearfield measurements.
Researchers investigated the roles of chance, contingency, and necessity in the evolution of ancestral proteins by combining ancestral reconstruction and continuous evolution technology to mutate and select proteins in the B-cell lymphoma-2 (BCL-2) family to acquire protein-protein interaction specificities that occurred during animal evolution. By replicating evolutionary trajectories from multiple ancestral proteins, they found that contingency generated over historical timescales steadily erased necessity and overwhelmed chance as the primary cause of acquired sequence variation. Trajectories launched from phylogenetically distant proteins yielded virtually no common mutations, even under strong and identical selection pressures. Chance arose because many sets of mutations could alter specificity at any point, while contingency arose because historical substitutions changed these sets. The results suggest that patterns of variation in BCL2 sequences, and likely other proteins, are idiosyncratic products of a particular and unpredictable course of historical events. Present-day BCL2 family proteins and potentially others are physical anecdotes of their unpredictable histories, reflecting accumulated events during descent from common ancestors with necessity imposed by physics, chemistry, and natural selection. Contingency generated by sequence change over phylogenetic times plays a profound role in B2 protein evolution under laboratory selection for new functions. Using human BID and NOXA as fixed partners introduced opportunities for chance and contingency to affect B2 sequence evolution, while differences between the design and biological setting may have overestimated chance's historical role. Additional dimensions of B2 function imposed selective constraints on evolution historically, reducing functionally equivalent genotypes available to chance. The paths through sequence space leading to historical protein-protein interactions changed repeatedly during the B2 history, even when binding profiles did not evolve. For chance to influence adaptive evolution, multiple paths to a phenotype must be accessible with similar probabilities of being taken.
A genome-wide haplotype-based analysis of 48 sub-Saharan African groups reveals the structure of genetic diversity and gene-flow, indicating historical connections between populations due to admixture. The study shows that coastal populations experienced an influx of Eurasian haplotypes over the last 700 years, while Eastern and Southern Niger-Congo groups share ancestry with Central Africans as a result of recent expansions. Most sub-Saharan populations share ancestry with groups outside their current geographic region due to gene-flow within the last 400 years. The analysis provides insight into haplotype sharing across ethno-linguistic groups and the movement of alleles into new environments, relevant to genetic epidemiology studies. Admixture has shaped genetic diversity in sub-Saharan Africa, with evidence of Eurasian ancestry movement into the continent over various timescales. Previous studies have described the influence of Eurasian and hunter-gatherer ancestry on population history, but questions remain on the extent and timing of key events and their impact on contemporary populations. The inferred groups reflect broad geographic patterns, while finer analysis reveals smaller groups and differentiates closely related populations consistent with self-reported ancestry. Gene-flow between populations relates to historical movements of people and within sub-Saharan Africa. Admixture occurs when genetically differentiated ancestral groups mix, a common feature of human populations globally. Genome analyses refine models of the continent's history and its impact on genetic diversity, providing a quantitative measure of coancestry between groups and identifying dominant events shaping current diversity. The study's findings are relevant to studying genotype-phenotype associations in Africa, with implications for understanding the movement of alleles and haplotypes into and within the continent.
Researchers investigated the mechanism of mitotic spindle scaling during Xenopus development, focusing on how the spindle adapts to changing cell dimensions during early embryogenesis. An in vitro system using cytoplasmic extracts from embryos was established to study spindle size differences between stage 3 and stage 8. The kinesin-13 kif2 was identified as a driver of developmental scaling, whose microtubule-destabilizing activity is inhibited in stage 3 spindles by importin α and activated in stage 8 when importin partitions to a membrane. Altering spindle size in embryos impaired spindle orientation during metaphase, but chromosome segregation remained robust. Spindle size in Xenopus development is coupled to cell size through a ratiometric mechanism controlling microtubule destabilization. Reconstituting spindle formation in embryo extracts elucidated a mechanism coordinating spindle size with cell size through differential activation of kif2. Hundreds of spindle-associated proteins were considered, but kif2 was identified as the physiological effector operating to scale the spindle during embryogenesis. Modifying spindle size without perturbing function revealed its role in maintaining orientation in large cells, suggesting physical cell features drive intracellular scaling through biochemical partitioning and redistribution. The mechanism is consistent with changes in meiotic to mitotic morphology during mouse development, where cell size impacts biochemical reactions altering spindle architecture. Import α phosphorylation liberates from membrane stores into cytoplasm, promoting its activity, and correcting nonspecific binding showed k2 retrieves import α from stage 3 extracts more than stage 8, indicating sufficient partitioning alters interaction with k2. Identification of the mechanism enabled testing its significance during development, suggesting changes in gradients and assembly accompany cell size, influencing spindle size. An embryo system assembling spind in cytoplasm extracts at any stage of development was created to address how spindle size is determined during vertebrate embryogenesis, up to the midblast transition when divisions become asynchronous.
Researchers have discovered a riboswitch that forms a natural Boolean logic gate to control purine metabolism in bacteria, where gene systems interpret multiple signals to set expression levels of genes they regulate. The riboswitch is composed of tandem arrangements of ligand-binding aptamers that approximate the function of specific two-input logic gates. Specifically, phosphoribyl pyrophosphate (PR) aptamers exist in single or tandem arrangements with guanine aptamers, allowing them to bind target ligands independently or in combination to regulate transcription of messenger RNAs for de novo purine biosynthesis. The guanine-PR aptam functions as an IMPLY logic gate, where terminator and antiterminator stems are stabilized or destabilized depending on concentrations of guanine and PR. This sophisticated all-RNA regulatory system senses ribonucleotide derivatives to control RNA synthesis, supporting the hypothesis that RNA organisms could manage complex metabolic states without protein factors. The guanine-PR system is the first example of tandem aptamers with different ligand specificities sharing an expression platform, implementing new logic functions. These RNA systems demonstrate complex chemical sensing and regulatory functions, reflecting metabolite monitoring and regulation in ancient RNA organisms. The discovery suggests additional variations of two-input logic gates will be represented by riboswitches yet to be discovered in modern cells. The existence of PR riboswitches supports the idea that RNA organisms used PR to generate biopolymer building blocks, with apters selectively binding biosynthetic precursors persisting as riboswitches in modern cells. The discovery of guanine-PR riboswitch demonstrates how complex molecular devices can be formed from RNA, with in vitro data representing the output of a population of RNA transcripts.
The reliability of synaptic transmission is dictated by the number of neurotransmitter-filled vesicles released into the synaptic cleft with each action potential, and variability in this property provides diversity of synaptic function across brain regions. The prevailing view is that release of single or multiple vesicles reflects variability in vesicle release probability, which is supported by calcium dependence of release. However, research using mouse brain slices demonstrates that the number of vesicles released is regulated by the size of the readily-releasable pool, upstream of vesicle release probability. Protein kinase A and its vesicle-associated synapsin dynamically control release site occupancy to dictate the number of vesicles released without altering release probability. Diversity in calcium homeostasis and protein kinase A may contribute to variability in regulation of release across synapses. Multivesicular release is widespread in the rodent CNS and appears to be the dominant mode in human cortex, while some synapses are limited to univesicular release and others switch modes in activity-dependent. The number of vesicles released from an active zone is the product of vesicle release probability and the number available for release. The readily-releasable pool is a functional measure of the number of release-competent vesicles per zone under control of synapsins, constraining the number released. High probability synapses display unives release and low probability synapses exhibit multives, illustrating that probability alone is insufficient to dictate vesicle mode, pointing to the contribution of vesicle availability. Vesicles transition between sequential states from loose to tighter with release machinery followed by calcium-triggered fusion, akin to transitions from replacement to docking sites prior to fusion. Understanding mechanisms that determine active zone supports unives or multives release is important for synaptic diversity and unifying presynaptic function models accounting multiple presyn parameters.
Researchers have reconstituted the selective packaging of HIV-1 RNA in vitro using a minimal system consisting of purified RNAs, recombinant HIV-1 Gag, and giant unilamellar vesicles, to understand how HIV-1 Gag selects and packages dimer unspliced viral RNA in the context of excess cytosolic human RNAs. The study found that as Gag assembles on the plasma membrane, the HIV-1 genome is enriched relative to cellular RNAs by an unknown mechanism. Mutations in the CA-CT domain of Gag, which affect the self-assembly of Gag, abrogated RNA selectivity, indicating that the ability of HIV-1 Gag to selectively package its genome and self-assembly on membranes are interdependent on one another. The results suggest that viral RNA with high-affinity sites for Gag can recruit G molecules to the membrane if one dissociates from membrane-cluding tRNA. The NC domain of G is necessary but not sufficient for the selective packaging of HIV-1 genomic RNA. The majority of plasma-proximal HIV-1 RNAs are monomeric in the absence of HIV-1 Gag, but in cells expressing Gag, monomeric RNAs coalesce to dimers on the membrane. The study underscores the importance of membrane-localized G assembly in HIV-1 genome selection and packaging. Lattice contacts conferring G ability to multimerize on membrane are crucial for selective packaging viral RNA. The 5' untranslated region is recruited to membranes by multimerized Gag without changes to its secondary structure. The association of G with tRNA inhibits genome-less assembly. Gag assembling on membranes packages HIV-1 5 untranslated region at subnanolar concentrations in presence of competitor RNA. Mutations that affect Gag clustering on membrane abolish RNA selectivity. The findings lead to a model for HIV-1 genome selection where Gag multimerization on membrane is necessary for packaging dimeric viral genomes with high probability. The study provides insight into how cytos G binding mainly cellular RNA assembles into particles packaging dimer viral genomes with 90 probability.
